Mishneh Torah, Marriage
משנה תורה, הלכות אישות
Mishneh Torah, trans. by Eliyahu Touger. Jerusalem, Moznaim Pub. c1986-c2007
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001020101/NLI

Mishneh Torah, Marriage



Chapter 1

Before the Torah was given, when a man would meet a woman in the marketplace and he and she decided to marry, he would bring her home, conduct relations in private and thus make her his wife. Once the Torah was given, the Jews were commanded that when a man desires to marry a woman, he must acquire her as a wife in the presence of witnesses. [Only] after this, does she become his wife. This is [alluded to in Deuteronomy 22:13]: "When a man takes a wife and has relations with her...."
This process of acquisition fulfills [one of] the Torah's positive commandments.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Positive Commandment 213) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 552) include this as one of the Torah's 613 commandments.</i> The process of acquiring a wife is formalized in three ways: through [the transfer of] money, through [the transfer of a] formal document and through sexual relations.<br>[The effectiveness of] sexual relations and [the transfer of a] formal document have their origin in the Torah [itself], while [the effectiveness of transfer of] money is Rabbinic in origin.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad and others object to this statement, noting that <i>Kiddushin</i> 4b derives the concept that the transfer of money is an effective means of formalizing a marriage bond from a <i>gezerah shavah</i>, a correlation between two verses in the Torah, indicating that this practice also has its source in the Torah.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> draw attention to the Rambam's statements in <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (General Principle 2), which state that any law that is not explicitly stated in the Torah, but rather derived through the Thirteen Principles of Biblical exegesis, is considered to be Rabbinic in origin (<i>midivrei soferim</i>). This classification does not, however, in any way diminish the status of this practice, and it is as if it were explicitly stated in the Torah. Thus, a marriage bond formalized through the transfer of money has the same status as one formalized through either of the other means mentioned by the Rambam.<br>Rav Kapach differs and states that the Rambam altered the text in his later years, and the correct version states, "All three are from the Torah." In explanation, he draws attention to the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Kiddushin</i> 1:1) and to one of the Rambam's responsa, and on this basis differs with the above principle.<br>He maintains that whenever the Rambam uses the expression <i>midivrei soferim</i>, he means that the practice is Rabbinic in origin and does not have the status of Torah law. The only practices that are considered to be ordained by the Torah are those explicitly stated in the Torah or mentioned by the Sages as having the status of Torah law.<br>In this context, he explains the Rambam's approach. Originally [as evidenced by the Rambam's statements in <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Positive Commandment 213)], the Rambam thought that sexual relations are the only <i>kiddushin</i> that are effective according to the Torah. For not only the effectiveness of the transfer of money, but also that of the transfer of a legal document is derived by the Sages only through Biblical exegesis. Afterwards, on the basis of certain passages that state that the effectiveness of the transfer of a legal document has the status of a Torah practice, the Rambam changed his opinion and wrote that the transfer of a document is also effective according to the Torah. This opinion is reflected in the Commentary on the Mishnah and the original version of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. Even later, the Rambam accepted the opinion that the effectiveness of the transfer of money also stems from the Torah itself. This is reflected in Chapter 3, Halachah 20, and the corrected text mentioned above.<br>(See <i>Birkat Avraham</i>, Responsum 44, in which the Rambam's son, Rabbenu Avraham, substantiates Rav Kapach's version of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>.)</i><br>This process of acquisition is universally referred to as <i>erusin</i> ("betrothal") or <i>kiddushin</i> ("consecration"). And a woman who is acquired in any of these three ways is referred to as <i>mekudeshet</i> or <i>me'ureset</i>.
Once this process of acquisition has been formalized and a woman has become <i>mekudeshet</i>, she is considered to be married even though the marriage bond has not been consummated and she has not entered her husband's home. Should anyone other than her husband engage in sexual relations with her, he is liable to be executed by the court. If her husband desires to divorce her, he must compose a <i>get</i> [a formal bill of divorce].
Before the Torah was given, when a man would meet a woman in the marketplace, and he and she desired, he could give her payment, engage in relations with her wherever they desired, and then depart. Such a woman is referred to as a harlot.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad and others differ and maintain that a woman is not considered to be a harlot unless she is a professional prostitute. The difference between this approach and the Rambam's involves only the severity of the prohibition. Both agree that sexual relations outside the context of marriage are forbidden. With regard to a <i>pilegesh</i>, a woman one designates as a sexual partner but who is not consecrated as a wife, see <i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 4:4.</i><br>When the Torah was given, [relations with] a harlot became forbidden, as [Deuteronomy 23:18] states: "There shall not be a harlot among the children of Israel."<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Negative Commandment 355) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 570) include this as one of the Torah's 613 commandments.</i> Therefore, a person who has relations with a woman for the sake of lust, without <i>kiddushin</i>, receives lashes as prescribed by the Torah, because he had relations with a harlot.
Whenever it is forbidden to engage in relations from the Torah, and engaging in relations makes one liable for <i>karet</i> - i.e., the [forbidden relationships] mentioned in <i>Parashat Acharei Mot</i>, such as a person's mother, his sister, his daughter and the like - these relations are called <i>arayot,</i> and each particular forbidden relationship is called an <i>ervah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">These include incestuous and adulterous relationships as mentioned in Leviticus, Chapter 18. The forbidden relationships that are punishable by execution are discussed in <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i>, Chapter 1, and those for which one is liable for <i>karet</i> and for which lashes are given are discussed in <i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i>, Chapter 19.</i>
There are other women with whom relations are forbidden according to the Oral Tradition; these prohibitions are Rabbinic in origin. These women are called <i>shniyot</i> (prohibitions of a secondary nature). There are twenty such women, including:<br>a) one's maternal grandmother; this prohibition continues upward without interruption: a person's maternal grandmother's maternal grandmother - and also those further removed - are also forbidden;<br>b) the mother of a person's maternal grandfather; this prohibition applies to her alone [and not her forbears];<br>c) a person's paternal grandmother; this prohibition continues upward without interruption: a person's paternal grandmother's maternal grandmother - and also those further removed - are also forbidden;<br>d) the mother of his paternal grandfather; this prohibition applies to her alone [and not her forbears];<br>e) the wife of his paternal grandfather; this prohibition continues upward without interruption; the wife of our Patriarch Jacob is forbidden to any one of us;<br>f) the wife of his maternal grandfather; this prohibition applies to her alone;<br>g) the wife of his father's maternal brother;<br>h) the wife of his mother's brother, whether a paternal or a maternal brother;<br>i) his son's daughter-in-law; this prohibition continues downward without interruption; any one of our wives is forbidden to our Patriarch Jacob;<br>j) the daughter-in-law of one's daughter; this prohibition applies to her alone;<br>k) the daughter of one's son's daughter; this prohibition applies to her alone;<br>l) the daughter of one's son's son; this prohibition applies to her alone;<br>m) the daughter of one's daughter's daughter; this prohibition applies to her alone;<br>n) the daughter of one's daughter's son; this prohibition applies to her alone;<br>o) the daughter of the son of one's wife's son; this prohibition applies to her alone;<br>p) the daughter of the daughter of one's wife's daughter; this prohibition applies to her alone;<br>q) the maternal grandmother of one's wife's father; this prohibition applies to her alone;<br>r) the paternal grandmother of one's wife's mother; this prohibition applies to her alone;<br>s) the maternal grandmother of one's wife's mother; this prohibition applies to her alone;<br>t) the paternal grandmother of one's wife's father; this prohibition applies to her alone.<br>Thus, the categories of <i>shniyot</i> that continue without interruption are four: one's maternal grandmother - this continues upward without interruption; one's paternal grandmother - this continues upward without interruption; the wife of one's paternal grandfather - this continues upward without interruption; and the wife of one's son's son - this continues downward without interruption.
All relations with women that are forbidden by the Torah, but that are not punishable by <i>karet</i>, are referred to as <i>issurei lavin</i> (prohibitions forbidden by negative commandments); they are also referred to as <i>issurei kedushah</i> (prohibitions [that encourage] holiness).<br>They are nine: relations between a widow and a High Priest;<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus 21:14.</i> those between a divorcee, a <i>zonah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 18:1 defines this term as meaning either a woman who is not Jewish, a Jewish woman who has engaged in relations with a man she is forbidden to marry, or one who engages in relations with a <i>challal</i> (a male born from relations between a priest and a woman he is forbidden to marry).</i> or a <i>chalalah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">A woman who engages in relations with a priest despite a prohibition against doing so, or a female born from relations between a priest and a woman he is forbidden to marry (<i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 19:1).</i> and either a High Priest or an ordinary priest,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus 21:7, 21:14.</i> those between a bastard<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">A bastard is defined as a person born from any of the forbidden sexual relations that are punishable by execution or <i>karet</i>, with the exception of relations with a woman in the <i>niddah</i> state. This term does not refer to a child born out of wedlock. Deuteronomy 23:3 forbids a bastard from marrying a native-born Jewish male or female.</i> and a native-born Jewish male or female, those between a native-born Jewish woman and a Moabite or Ammonite convert,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See Deuteronomy 23:4.</i> those between a man and his divorcee after she has been married to another person,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See Deuteronomy 24:4.</i> those between a native-born Jewish woman and a man with crushed testicles or a cut member,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See Deuteronomy 23:2.</i> and those between a <i>yevamah</i> and a man other than [one of her deceased husband's brothers] while she is still obligated to them.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>yevamah</i> is a childless widow, who is obligated to marry one of her deceased husband's brothers or to be discharged of that obligation through the rite of <i>chalitzah</i>. Until she and her brother-in-law fulfill this rite, she is forbidden to marry anyone else. (See Deuteronomy 25:5-10.)</i><br>According to Rabbinic decree, an equation is established between a divorcee and a woman who undergoes <i>chalitzah</i>, and the latter is also forbidden [to engage in relations] with a priest. The Rabbis also placed <i>netinim</i> in the same status as bastards. In <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i>, we will explain who the <i>netinim</i> are.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>netinim</i> are the Givonites, who were forbidden to marry into the Jewish people even after their conversion by Joshua. King David reinforced the ban against them. (See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 12:22-23.)</i>
There are certain relationships for which there is a prohibition resulting from a positive commandment [<i>issurei aseh</i>], but they are not prohibited by a negative commandment. There are three such prohibitions: the first and second generations of Egyptian or Edomite converts, both men and women [to all native-born Jews and Jewish women], and a woman who is not a virgin to a High Priest.<br>In these instances, there are no verses that state "He shall not enter [the congregation of God]..." or "he may not take...." The prohibition [against the marriage of the Edomite and Egyptian converts] is instead derived [from Deuteronomy 23:9], which states that "in the third generation they may enter the congregation of God." This implies that the first and second generations may not enter [this marriage group].<br>Similarly, from [the positive commandment, Leviticus 21:13]: "He [the High Priest] shall marry a virgin," we can derive that he is forbidden to marry a woman who is not a virgin. A prohibition that is derived from a positive commandment has the status of a positive commandment.

Chapter 2

From the day of a girl's birth until she becomes twelve years old, she is called a <i>k'tanah</i> (minor) and/or a <i>tinoket</i> (baby). Even if several [pubic] hairs grow [on her body] during this time, they are [not significant according to Jewish law and are] considered to be merely hairs growing from a mole. If, however, two hairs grow in the pubic area after she becomes twelve years old [her status changes, and] she is considered a <i>na'arah</i> (maiden).
Growing two pubic hairs at this age is referred to as the lower sign [of physical maturity]. Once a girl manifests this sign [of physical maturity], she is referred to as a maiden for six months. From the last day of these six months and onward, she is referred to as a <i>bogeret</i> (mature woman). The difference between the stages of maidenhood and maturity is only six months.
From the time a girl reaches the age of twelve years and one day<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The intent in this halachah, and similarly, whenever the term "...years old and one day" is mentioned, is not that an additional day must pass after the woman's twelfth birthday. Rather, the intent is that she has completed twelve complete years of life and begun the following day.</i> until the age of twenty, if she does not grow two pubic hairs, she is still considered to be a child, even if she manifests the physical signs of barrenness.<br>If [during this period], she grows two pubic hairs, even if [this occurs] in her twentieth year, she is considered to be a maiden for six months. Only afterwards is she considered to be a mature woman.
Should a woman be less than thirty days below the age of twenty, not have grown two pubic hairs, and have manifested [all] the physical signs of barrenness,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i>) differs with the Rambam on two points: a) the girl need only be 19 and one month, not 19 and eleven months (Chapter 155) and b) that she need manifest only one, but not all signs of barrenness (Chapter 44, 172).<br>With regard to the first issue, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> appears to favor the <i>Tur's</i> view, although that of the Rambam is also mentioned. With regard to the second issue, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> appears to follow the Rambam's view, while the Ramah cites that of the <i>Tur</i>.</i> she is deemed an <i>aylonit</i> (a barren woman).<br>If she does not manifest all the sign of barrenness, she is still considered to be a child until she grows two pubic hairs or until she reaches the age of 35 years and one day.
Should a woman reach this age without growing two pubic hairs, she is deemed barren even though she does not manifest physical signs of barrenness. A barren woman does not [go through the six-month] period of maidenhood. Instead, directly after having been considered a child, she is considered to be a mature woman.
The following are the physical signs of barrenness: a) she lacks [protruding] breasts; b) she stiffens during sexual relations; c) her lower abdomen does not resemble a woman's, d) her voice is deep and cannot be differentiated from that of a man.<br>All three, a maiden, a mature woman and a barren woman, are referred to by the term <i>gedolah</i> [adult woman]. [Unlike children, they are held responsible for their conduct.]
[In addition to growing pubic hairs,] a woman has signs of physical maturity that are manifest in her upper body. They are referred to as "upper signs." Among them are:<br>a) when the woman stretches her hand backward, a crease forms in the place of her breast; b) the color of the tip of the breast becomes darker; c) when a person places his hand on the end of the breast and it remains depressed slightly before rising; d) creases form at the end of the breast, and a nipple takes shape; my teachers taught that the formation of creases is sufficient; e) the breasts protrude; f) they become erect; g) the mound of Venus forms above the woman's genitals, below her stomach; h) the flesh of this mound becomes soft and not hard. These are eight signs.
If one or even all of these signs of maturity appear in a woman before she becomes twelve years of age, no attention is paid to it. When she becomes twelve years old and one day, and she manifests the lower sign of physical maturity, no attention is paid to [the presence or lack of] these [upper] signs of physical maturity [and she is considered to be a maiden].<br>If she does not manifest the lower sign of physical maturity, but she does manifest one of these [upper] signs, there is doubt whether she should be considered a child or a maiden, and the more stringent perspective is followed [with regard to all halachic questions] concerning her. If she manifests all these [upper] signs, she is definitely considered to be an adult. For it is impossible for her to manifest all these signs [and yet not have had two pubic hairs grow]. We assume these hairs have grown, but they have dropped off.
When a woman gives birth after reaching the age of twelve years, she is deemed an adult, even though she did not manifest either upper or lower signs of maturity. [Giving birth to] children is a sign of maturity.
A male, from birth until the age of thirteen, is called a <i>katan</i> (minor) and/or a <i>tinok</i> (baby). Even if several [pubic] hairs grow [on his body] during this time, they are [not significant according to Jewish law] and are considered to be merely hairs growing from a mole. If, however, two hairs grow in the pubic area after he attains the age of thirteen years and one day, [his status changes, and] he is considered a <i>gadol</i> (adult male) and/or an <i>ish</i> (man).
Should a child reach this age without growing two pubic hairs, he is still considered a minor until he reaches the age of twenty years less thirty days, even though he manifests signs of impotency. Should he reach the age [of twenty years less thirty days] and not have grown either pubic hairs or hairs of the beard [the following rules apply]. If he manifests one of the physical signs of impotency, he is considered impotent (a <i>saris</i>), and he is considered to be an adult with regard to all matters.<br>If he does not manifest any of the signs of impotency, he is still considered to be a minor until he grows two pubic hairs or until he reaches the age of thirty five years and one day.
If he reaches this age, he is considered impotent, although he did not manifest any of the signs of impotency. If he reached the age of twenty years less thirty days without growing two pubic hairs, but did grow two hairs on his beard, he is not considered to be impotent, even if he manifests one sign of impotency, until he either manifests all the signs of impotency or reaches the age of thirty five years and one day.
These are the signs of impotency: a) One lacks a beard, b) his hair grows inadequately, c) his flesh is hairless, d) his urine does not produce vapor, e) his urine does not flow in an arc, f) his semen is off color, g) his urine does not ferment, h) when he washes in the winter, his flesh does not produce steam, and i) his voice is high pitched and cannot be differentiated from that of a woman.
An impotent person of this type is referred to as a <i>s'ris chamah</i> [one who became impotent because of fever].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the interpretation of the <i>Shulchan Aruch. Yevamot</i> 80a interprets this as meaning, "one who never saw the light of the sun while fit."</i> When, however, the genitals of a male have been cut, severed or crushed, as the gentiles do, the person is called a <i>s'ris adam</i> [one who became impotent as a result of human activity]. When such a person reaches the age of thirteen and one day, he is considered to be an adult, for he will never manifest signs of maturity.
When a male reaches the age of thirteen and one day and does not grow any pubic hairs, but manifests the upper signs of physical maturity,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he grows hairs of the beard and in his underarms; his voice changes; his hair grows adequately; and his flesh is not silky (<i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i>).</i> doubt exists whether he is considered to be an adult or a minor. If, however, his pubic area was not inspected, but he manifests signs of physical maturity in his upper body, he is presumed to be an adult.
Whenever the term "two pubic hairs" is mentioned with regard to a male or a female, the intent is that the hairs are long enough to be bent in half, with their point touching their base. If they grow to the extent that they can be cut by scissors, but are not [long enough] that they can be bent in half with their point touching their base, [there is doubt regarding the decision], and the more stringent ruling is always followed.<br>Therefore, when a boy's or girl's pubic hairs have grown to the point that they can be cut by scissors, the individual is considered to be an adult with regard to those matters concerning which the ruling would be more stringent if he or she were so classified. And with regard to those matters concerning which the ruling would be more stringent if he [or she] were classified as a minor, the individual is so classified because the pubic hairs are not long enough to be bent in half with their point touching their base.
These two hairs must be located in the pubic area. The entire pubic area is appropriate for the signs to be located. There is no difference whether they are located in the upper area, the lower area or on the sexual organ itself.<br>The two hairs must be located in a single place, and there must be a follicle at their base. If both of them stem from the same follicle, it is acceptable. If two follicles are located next to each other without hairs growing from them, they are, nevertheless, considered a sign. We follow the presumption that a follicle will not exist without hair. [Surely,] there were hairs [that grew from the follicle], and they fell.
As we have explained, when a girl grows two pubic hairs before she is twelve, or a boy grows two pubic hairs before he is thirteen, they are considered to be merely hairs growing from a mole. Even if these hairs remain in their place after the boy reaches the age of thirteen, or the girl reaches the age of twelve, they are not considered signs of physical maturity.
When does the above apply? When the child was inspected [before attaining the age of majority], and the hairs were deemed to be hairs growing from a mole. If, however, no such inspection was made until they reached the age of majority, and afterwards an inspection was made and two hairs were discovered, they are considered acceptable signs of physical maturity. We do not say that perhaps the hairs grew before the child reached majority, and they are merely hairs growing from a mole.
Whenever a girl is inspected for [signs of physical maturity] - whether during her twelfth year, after she became twelve or when she is older - the inspection is carried out by trustworthy, ethical women. Even when an inspection has been conducted by one woman, her word is accepted with regard to whether or not [the girl has manifested signs of physical maturity].<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Although the testimony of women is not generally accepted in court, an exception is made in this instance because of modesty. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 155:15) quotes the Rambam's wording. Nevertheless, the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 155:23 states that a woman's testimony can be accepted only with regard to making a more stringent ruling, but not a more lenient one.</i>
Whenever the term "years" is mentioned with regard to [the age of] a male or a female, endowment evaluations,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus, Chapter 27.</i> or any other matter, the intent is not lunar years, nor solar years, but rather the years as reckoned by the Jewish court to [juxtapose the solar and lunar calendars], whether ordinary years or leap years, as established by the [Jewish] court, as explained in <i>Hilchot Kiddush HaChodesh</i>. This is the intent whenever the subject of years is mentioned with regard to religious matters.
We do not rely on the testimony of women regarding a child's age, nor on that of relatives. Instead, the matter is determined by the testimony of two men who are fit to testify in court.
When a father says, "My son is nine years and one day old," or "My daughter is three years and one day old,"<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">If a male is less than nine years old, or a female is less than three years old, they are not fit to engage in sexual relations. Even if they do, in fact, engage in relations, these are of no halachic significance. Hence, if a boy above the age of nine engages in sexual relations with a married woman, the woman is liable. Similarly, if a man engaged in relations with a three-year old girl who was married, the man is liable.</i> his word is accepted with regard to the obligation of bringing a sacrifice [if sexual relations were carried out without knowledge of the sin involved], but not with regard to administering stripes [for rebellion] or other punishments.<br>If the father says, "My son is thirteen years and one day old," or "My daughter is twelve years and one day old," his word is accepted with regard to vows, endowment evaluations, property forsworn [and transferred to the priests],<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus, Chapter 27; <i>Hilchot Arachin</i> 6:1.</i> or the consecration of property, but not with regard to administering lashes or other punishments.
A person who possesses both a male sexual organ and a female sexual organ is called an <i>androgynous</i>. There is doubt whether such a person should be classified as a male or as a female; there is no physical sign that can ever enable such a distinction to be made.
A person who possesses neither a male sexual organ nor a female sexual organ, but instead, his genital area is a solid mass, is called a <i>tumtum</i>. There is also doubt with regard [to this person's status]. If an operation is carried out and a male [organ is revealed], he is definitely considered to be a male. If a female [organ is revealed], she is definitely considered to be a female.<br>When a <i>tumtum</i> or an <i>androgynous</i> reaches the age of twelve years and one day, they are assumed to be adults.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling, stating that it is necessary for these individuals to manifest physical signs of maturity before they are classified as adults. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that it appears that the Rambam is saying that there is no need for these individuals to manifest such signs. Needless to say, at the age of twelve these individuals are not considered to be adults with regard to the mitzvot incumbent upon males.</i> Whenever these terms are mentioned, the intent is individuals of this age.
Whenever the terms <i>cheresh</i> and <i>chereshet</i> are used, they refer to a male or female deaf mute, respectively. If, however, a person can speak but cannot hear, or can hear but cannot speak, he is considered to be an ordinary person.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Because a deaf mute's ability to communicate and respond is so limited, such a person is considered to be mentally incompetent and is not held responsible for his conduct, nor is he able to enter into a marriage contract, according to Scriptural law.</i> A male or a female who is intellectually competent, being neither a deaf mute nor emotionally disturbed, is referred to as a <i>pike'ach</i> or a <i>pikachat</i>, respectively.
We have thus defined twenty terms in these two chapters: <i>kiddushin, ervah, sh'niyah, issurei lavin, issurei aseh, k'tanah, na'arah, bogeret, aylonit, gedolah,</i> lower sign of maturity, upper sign of maturity, <i>katan, s'ris chamah, s'ris adam, gadol, androgynous, tumtum, chershim, pik'chim</i>. Keep these terms in mind at all times; do not forget their meaning, so that their intent will not have to be explained whenever they are mentioned.

Chapter 3

How is the bond of <i>kiddushin</i> established with a woman? If the man [desires to establish] the <i>kiddushin</i> by [the transfer of] money, [he must give] a <i>p'rutah</i>, either in coin or its worth.<br>[Before giving it], he tells her, "You are consecrated unto me...," "You are betrothed to me...," or "You become my wife through this." He must give her [the money or the item] in the presence of witnesses.<br>It is the man who makes the statement that implies that he acquires the woman as his wife, and it is he who gives her the money.
If she gave him [money] and told him: "Behold, I am consecrated to you," "Behold, I am betrothed to you," "I am your wife," or [she used] any other expression that implied acquisition, the marriage bond is not established. Similarly, if she gave [him money] and he made the statement, the marriage bond is not established.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For it is the man's actions that must precipitate the inception of the marriage bond, as implied by Deuteronomy 24:1: "When a man takes a wife." Note, however, Chapter 5, Halachah 22, which mentions an exception to this principle.</i> If he gave [her money] and she made the statement [the matter is unresolved,]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishneh LaMelech</i> offers two explanations of the doubt involved: a) We are unsure of his intent. Since he did not specify that he was giving the woman the money for the sake of <i>kiddushin</i>, it is possible that he gave her the money for another reason. This interpretation appears to be supported by the rulings of the <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 27:8) that in such a circumstance, the marriage bond is established if previously the man and the woman were speaking about getting married, or if the man confirmed the woman's statements. Halachah 8 appears to support this explanation.<br>b) The second view is that the doubt is one of principle: The Sages did not define whether the obligation that a man precipitate the marriage bond requires merely his giving money, or also making the statement. According to this conception, the doubt would remain even in the two situations mentioned by the <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>.</i> and the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the woman cannot marry another man until she receives a bill of divorce. Nevertheless, if another man engages in relations with her, he is not executed for adultery.</i>
If the man establishes the marriage bond with [the transfer of] a legal document, [the following rules apply:] He should write on paper, on a shard, on a leaf or on any other article<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The man may not, however, write the document on a leaf attached to a tree, or any other item that must be severed, for this is unacceptable when writing a bill of divorce. (See the following halachah and notes.)</i> he desires: "You are consecrated unto me...," "You are betrothed to me...," or any similar expression. He must then give her the document in the presence of witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference of opinion among the Rabbis if it is necessary for the man to make a verbal statement of intent as well. (See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 32:3.) The <i>Or Sameach</i> points to Halachah 18 as proof that a statement is required, but <i>Kin'at Eliyahu</i> notes that Halachah 7 appears to indicate that a verbal statement is unnecessary.</i>
The document must be written for the sake of the woman who is being married, as must a bill of divorce,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The acceptability of a legal document for the establishment of <i>kiddushin</i> from the association between marriage and divorce created by Deuteronomy 24:2: "When she leaves his household, she may go and become [married] to another man." <i>Kiddushin</i> 5a states that this verse indicates that just as she "leaves" through a bill of divorce, she can "become" through a bill of <i>kiddushin</i>. Hence, all the particulars that apply to a <i>get</i> (a bill of divorce) apply to a bill of <i>kiddushin</i>.</i> and it must be written with her consent.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This requirement does not apply with regard to a bill of divorce and is a point of debate in <i>Kiddushin</i> 9b. The opinion the Rambam cites maintains that since the woman is being acquired through this document, she must consent to its being written. Because of the debate in the Talmud, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 32:1) differs with the Rambam and maintains that the matter is unresolved, the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt, and [the stringencies required had] the marriage bond been established [must be followed].</i> If it was not written for her sake, or if it was written for her sake, but was written without her consent, the marriage bond is not established. [This applies] even when he gives her [the document] with her consent in the presence of witnesses.
If the man consecrates through sexual relations, he should tell the woman, "You are consecrated unto me...," "You are betrothed to me...," or "You become my wife through these relations," or choose a similar statement. He must enter into privacy with her in the presence of witnesses and engage in relations.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Although the man must enter into privacy with the woman in the presence of witnesses, and must make the statement of intent in the presence of witnesses, the relations themselves should be private, as stated in Chapter 14, Halachah 16, and <i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 10:18. It must be noted that this represents a change in the Rambam's thinking. In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Kiddushin</i> 1:1), he originally stated that the relations must also be observed by witnesses for the marriage bond to be established. Nevertheless, in the later texts of the Commentary on the Mishnah the Rambam amended the text according to the above conception.</i><br>When a person consecrates through sexual relations, one may assume that his intent is on the conclusion of the relations;<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The term "the conclusion of sexual relations" (<i>g'mar bi'ah</i>) in Hebrew is somewhat of a misnomer. It refers not to the man's withdrawal from the woman, nor to ejaculation, but to a far earlier stage: the insertion of the penis in its entirety into the vagina (<i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 1:10).</i> when the relations are concluded, the marriage bond is established. Regardless of whether the couple engage in vaginal or anal intercourse, the marriage bond is established.
The statements that the man makes when he consecrates [his wife] must imply that he acquires her as a wife, and not that he gives himself to her. What is implied?<br>Should he tell her, or write in the document he gives her: "I am your husband," "I am your betrothed," "I am your man," or the like, the marriage bond is not established at all. If he tells her or writes to her: "Behold, you are my wife," "Behold, you are my betrothed," "Behold, you are acquired by me," "Behold, you are mine," "Behold, you are my possession," "Behold, you are my designated one,"<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 19:20 associates this term with establishing relations with a maid-servant, as explained in Chapter 4, Halachah 17. Therefore, there is doubt among the Rabbis if it can be used in establishing the marriage bond with an ordinary woman. Thus, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 27:3) states that if this expression is used the matter is unresolved, and the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.</i> "Behold, you are within my property," "Behold, you are bound to me," or the like, the marriage bond is established.
Should he tell her, or write to her: "You are set aside for me," "You are earmarked for me," "You are my helper," "You are my counterpart," "You are my rib," "You are closed off for me," "You are below me," "You are my captive," "You have been taken by me," [the matter is unresolved, and] the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.<br>The above applies only when the man was [previously] speaking to the woman about establishing a marriage bond. If he was not speaking to the woman about such a matter, these words are of no consequence.
A man may consecrate a woman by making statements in any language that she understands, provided that, in that language, his statements mean that he is acquiring her, as explained.<br>If a man was speaking to a woman about consecrating her and she consented, and he immediately gave her [something] in her hand to consecrate her or engaged in sexual relations [with that intent], without [making a statement] clarifying [his purpose], it is sufficient. Since they were speaking about this matter, it is not necessary for him to be explicit.<br>Similarly, a man need not tell witnesses who observe <i>kiddushin</i> or divorce, "You are my witnesses."<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to the acknowledgement of a debt, such a statement is necessary. (See <i>Hilchot To'en V'Nit'an</i> 6:6-8, 7:1-2.)</i> As long as he has divorced or consecrated a woman in their presence, she is consecrated or divorced.
When a man tells a woman, "Become consecrated to my half," she is consecrated. To what can this be compared? To his saying, "May you become my wife, and also another woman." And so, she has only half a man.<br>If, however, he said: "Half of you is consecrated to me," she is not consecrated. For a woman cannot be consecrated to two men. Similarly, if he says, "Behold, you are consecrated to me and to him," the woman is not consecrated.
If he told her: "Behold, half of you is consecrated to me with a <i>p'rutah</i>, and half of you [is consecrated to me with another] <i>p'rutah</i>," or if he told her: "Behold, half of you is consecrated to me with half a <i>p'rutah</i>, and your other half [is consecrated to me with another] half a <i>p'rutah</i>," she is consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad and similarly, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 31:7) differ and maintain that the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt. It is only with regard to stringencies that the <i>kiddushin</i> are considered to be valid.</i><br>If he told her: "Behold, half of you is consecrated to me with a <i>p'rutah</i> today, and half of you [is consecrated to me with another] <i>p'rutah</i> tomorrow," or if he told her: "Your two halves are consecrated to me with a <i>p'rutah</i>,"<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This clause serves as the basis for the Ra'avad's objection to the Rambam's decisions above.</i> "Your two daughters are consecrated to my two sons with this <i>p'rutah</i>," "Your daughter is consecrated to me, and your cow is sold to me with this <i>p'rutah</i>," or "Your daughter... and your land... with a <i>p'rutah</i>" - in all of these circumstances, [the matter is unresolved, and] the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.
A father may consecrate his daughter without her knowledge while she is a minor. Even when she is a <i>na'arah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">At which time, the girl has the right to accept <i>kiddushin</i> from a man herself.</i> he still possesses this right, as [implied by Deuteronomy 22:16]: "I gave my daughter to this man."<br>[The money received as] <i>kiddushin</i> belongs to her father. Similarly, he has the right to [any ownerless property] she finds, [the wages she receives for] her labor, and [the money she receives as stipulated in] her <i>ketubah</i> if she is divorced or widowed before the marriage bond is consummated. He is entitled to all these until she becomes a <i>bogeret</i>.<br>Therefore, a father is entitled to receive <i>kiddushin</i> on behalf of his daughter from the day she was born until she becomes a <i>bogeret</i>. Even if she is a deaf mute or intellectually incompetent, if her father consecrates her [to another man], she is his wife.<br>If a girl is older than three years and one day, she can be consecrated through sexual relations with her father's consent. Should she be below this age, if her father has her consecrated through sexual relations, the marriage bond is not established.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">For until the age of three, the relations are not considered sexual in nature (this by no means insinuates that they are insignificant or potentially harmful, only that they are not sexual).</i>
After a daughter becomes a <i>bogeret</i>, her father has no rights over her; she is like all other women, and she can be consecrated only with her own consent.<br>Similarly, if her father had her married, the marriage bond was consummated [<i>nisu'in</i>],<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second stage of the marriage relationship, as explained in Chapter 11 onward.</i> and then she was widowed or divorced, [even] in her father's lifetime, she is considered to be independent, despite the fact that she is still a minor. Once a woman enters <i>nisu'in</i>, her father no longer has any authority over her.
When a girl receives <i>kiddushin</i> without her father's knowledge before she reaches the age of majority, the marriage bond is not established.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This and the other laws in this halachah apply when a girl is either a <i>k'tanah</i> or a <i>na'arah</i> (has reached the age of twelve), but not a <i>bogeret</i> (twelve and a half).</i> [This applies] even when the father consents subsequently.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>kiddushin</i> were not effective at the time they were given, the father's subsequent consent is of no significance. This point is, however, disputed by some of the later authorities. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 37:11.)</i> Moreover, if she is widowed or divorced after these <i>kiddushin</i>, she is not forbidden [to marry] a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">No priest may marry a divorcee, nor may a High Priest marry a widow. Nevertheless, because these <i>kiddushin</i> are not effective, these prohibitions do not apply.</i><br>Both she and her father can prevent [the marriage bonds from taking effect].<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <i>Maharik</i> (Responsum 30[32]), this applies when the <i>kiddushin</i> were given in her father's presence, but he remained silent. In both the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>ibid.</i>), Rav Yosef Karo differs and explains that according to the Rambam, the father must consent at the time of (or before) the <i>kiddushin</i>. If he desires to prevent the <i>kiddushin</i> at that time, he may. If he consents, the girl getting married can still prevent the <i>kiddushin</i> from taking place. For since her father charged her with this matter, even if he consented to the match it is dependent on her. (See also Chapter 22, Halachah 5, and <i>Hilchot Terumot</i> 8:16.)</i> Regardless of whether she was consecrated in the presence of her father or not, she is not consecrated.
[The following rules apply when] there is doubt whether or not the girl is a <i>bogeret</i>: Whether her father consecrated her without her consent, or she consecrated herself without her father's consent, the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt. Therefore, [to marry another man,] she must receive a <i>get</i> given because of the doubt.<br>A man may appoint an agent to consecrate a wife for him. [This applies] if he specifies a particular woman, or gives the agent the authority to consecrate any woman. Similarly, a woman past the age of majority<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">A woman below the age of majority does not have the authority to appoint an agent.</i> may appoint an agent to receive <i>kiddushin</i> for her. [This applies] if she specifies [that they be given by] a particular man, or gives the agent the authority to receive them from any man. Similarly, a father may appoint an agent to accept the <i>kiddushin</i> of his daughter as long as she is under his authority.<br>A man may tell his daughter who is below the age of majority, "Go out and receive your <i>kiddushin</i>."<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Among the explanations that are given for why this is acceptable: a) If the father had said, "Give an animal food, and my daughter will be consecrated to you," the <i>kiddushin</i> would be binding. When he tells the daughter to receive <i>kiddushin</i>, it is as if he makes a statement that it is acceptable to him that the man who consecrates her gives her the money (Ra'avad, Ramban).<br>b) Although a minor may not normally serve as an agent, this instance is an exception. Since the <i>kiddushin</i> are being given for her benefit, the girl is entitled to act in this capacity (Rabbenu Asher). The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 37:7 states that the Rambam subscribes to this view.</i>
When an agent is appointed to receive <i>kiddushin</i>, he must be appointed in the presence of two witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that since the marriage bonds are established through the acceptance of the <i>kiddushin</i>, witnesses must be present to acknowledge the agent's appointment, for "no matters involving forbidden sexual relations [are established if] fewer than two witnesses are present (<i>Yevamot</i> 88a)."<br>The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 35:3) quotes the opinion of Rabbenu Asher, who states that if all concerned acknowledge the appointment of the agent, the <i>kiddushin</i> are effective. He therefore rules that in such a situation, the woman needs to receive a <i>get</i> before she can marry another man.</i> When, by contrast, a man appoints an agent to consecrate a woman, there is no need for the appointment to be made in the presence of witnesses. For the only purpose witnesses would serve with regard to the agency of the man is to make known the truth of the matter.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's statements are based on the comparison to an agent appointed to bring a woman a <i>get</i>. The Ra'avad objects to the comparison, because a <i>get</i> is a formal legal document, and possession of it serves to verify the person's agency. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) quotes both opinions, but appears to favor that of the Rambam.</i> Therefore, if the agent and the principal acknowledge the appointment, there is no need for witnesses, [as in parallel cases, such as] an agent appointed to bring a <i>get</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Gittin</i> 6:4. This ruling itself is not accepted by all authorities, as noted in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 141:13).</i> or an agent appointed to separate <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Terumah</i> 4:1.</i><br>In all matters, a principal's agent is regarded as the principal himself, and there is no need to appoint witnesses.
An agent may serve as a witness. Therefore, if a person appointed two men as agents to consecrate a woman, and they did so, they serve both as agents and as witnesses. Hence, there is no need for them to consecrate her in the presence of two other witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">As clarified by the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 35:2), this ruling applies only when: a) the <i>kiddushin</i> are established by the transfer of a legal document worth less than a <i>p'rutah</i>, or b) the <i>kiddushin</i> are established by the transfer of money, and the woman admits receiving the money.</i>
All are fit to serve as agents [in this capacity] except a deaf mute, a mentally incompetent individual and a minor - for they are not responsible - and a gentile, because he is not a member of the covenant. [The latter exclusion is based on Numbers 18:28, which] states: "And so shall you set aside, and you...." [This is interpreted as] including an agent. [Our Sages commented:] Just as you are members of the covenant, your agents must be members of the covenant, thus excluding a gentile.<br>A [Canaanite] servant, although he is acceptable as an agent with regard to financial matters, is not acceptable as an agent with regard to <i>kiddushin</i> and <i>gittin</i>, because the laws of marriage and divorce do not apply to him.
An agent appointed by a man to consecrate a woman should tell her: "Behold, you are consecrated to so and so by virtue of this money" or "...by virtue of this legal document."<br>If an agent of the woman receives the <i>kiddushin</i>, the [man consecrating her] should tell [the agent]: "So and so who appointed you is consecrated to me," and the agent should reply, "I have consecrated her to you," "I have betrothed her to you," "I have given her to you as a wife," or the like.<br>Similarly, when a man consecrates a girl by [giving <i>kiddushin</i> to] her father, he should tell him, "Behold, your daughter so and so is consecrated to me," and the father should reply: "I have consecrated her to you." If the father or the agent says "yes," or even if he remains silent, it is sufficient. If they were discussing the matter, and the man gave the <i>kiddushin</i> to the father or to the agent without making any statement, it is sufficient, and the <i>kiddushin</i> are effective.<br>If the <i>kiddushin</i> are established by virtue of [the transfer of] a legal document, he must have the document written with the consent of the father or of the agent. In all matters pertaining to <i>kiddushin</i>, the same laws that apply to the man and the woman, apply when [the <i>kiddushin</i> are established by] the [man's] agent and [the woman's] agent or [her] father.
It is a mitzvah for a man to consecrate his wife by himself, rather than to charge an agent [with this matter]. Similarly, it is a mitzvah for a woman to [receive] <i>kiddushin</i> herself rather than to charge an agent with receiving them for her.<br>Although a father has the option of consecrating his daughter to anyone he desires while she is a minor or while she is a maiden, it is not proper for him to act in this manner. Instead, our Sages enjoined that a person should not consecrate his daughter while she is a minor until she matures and says, "I would like [to marry] so and so."<br>Similarly, it is not proper for a man to consecrate a girl below the age of majority. Nor should one consecrate a woman until one sees her and deems her fitting, lest she not find favor in his eyes, and he divorce her, or sleep with her while hating her.
<i>Kiddushin</i> established by virtue of sexual relations are effective according to Scriptural law. Similarly, <i>kiddushin</i> established by virtue [of the transfer] of a legal document are effective according to Scriptural law. Just as [the transfer of a legal document] concludes a divorce, as [Deuteronomy 24;1] states: "And he shall write her a scroll of divorce," so too, [the transfer of a legal document] concludes [the establishment of the marriage bond].<br>[The effectiveness of the transfer of] money stems from Scriptural law,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the Rambam's later emendations to the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The earlier version of the text states: "The effectiveness of [the transfer of] money stems from Rabbinic law." See the discussion of this concept in the notes on Chapter 1, Halachah 2.</i> but its interpretation is based on Rabbinic law. [Deuteronomy, <i>ibid.</i>,] states "When a man takes a wife," and our Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 4b.</i> explained: This [process of] acquisition involves [the transfer of] money, as implied by [Genesis 23:13]: "I have given the money for the field; take it from me."
Although this is the essence of the principle, it has already become universal Jewish custom to consecrate [a marriage bond] through [the transfer of] money or objects that are worth money. If one desires to consecrate [a woman] by [giving her] a legal document, one may, but at the outset one should not consecrate [a woman] through sexual relations.<br>If a man consecrates [a woman] through sexual relations, he is given stripes for rebelliousness,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The punishment given for transgressing a Rabbinic decree.</i> so that the Jewish people will not extend beyond the limits of modesty in this manner. Nevertheless, the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding.
Similarly, a man who consecrates a woman without establishing an engagement previously, or a man who consecrates a woman in the marketplace, is given stripes for rebelliousness, although his <i>kiddushin</i> are binding. [This was instituted] lest such a practice accustom people to licentious conduct and invite a comparison to a harlot, as existed before the giving of the Torah.
Whenever a man consecrates a woman,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Today, it is customary for the consecration to be made under the wedding canopy (<i>chuppah</i>), and the blessing is recited at this time.</i> whether by himself or via an agent, either he<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording implies that the blessing should be recited by the groom himself. At present, the current custom in both the Sephardic and Ashkenazic communities is to have the Rabbi conducting the marriage ceremony recite the blessing, lest a groom who does not know how to recite the blessing be embarrassed by his lack of knowledge. (See <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i>.)</i> or his agent should recite a blessing before the consecration, as one recites a blessing before performing any of the mitzvot. [After reciting the blessing,] he should consecrate [the woman].<br>If he consecrates a woman without reciting a blessing, he should not recite the blessing afterwards. It would be a blessing in vain, for the activity has already been performed.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Darchei Moshe</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 34), the Ramah states that one may recite the blessing afterwards as well. Nevertheless, in his gloss on the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, he does not make such a statement. The current custom in both the Sephardic and Ashkenazic communities is to recite the blessing before the <i>kiddushin</i>.</i>
Which blessing is recited?<br>Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who has sanctified us with His mitzvot and separated<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 34:1) states that the blessing should read: "and commanded us concerning," rather than "and separated us from." This is the common practice today.</i> us from illicit relationships, who has forbidden the <i>arusot</i> to us, and permitted to us those who are married<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 34:3 and others state that the blessing should read "those who are married to us." This is the custom in many communities.</i> by [the rites of] <i>chuppah</i> and <i>kiddushin</i>. Blessed are You, God, who sanctifies Israel.<br>This is the blessing of <i>erusin</i>. The people have established the custom of reciting this blessing over wine or beer. If there is wine available, one should recite the blessing over the wine, recite the blessing of consecration afterwards, and then consecrate the woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">At present, the custom is for the officiating Rabbi to recite the blessing over the wine and then the blessing of consecration. He then has the groom and bride drink from the wine, and afterwards the groom consecrates the bride.</i> If there is no wine or beer available, one should recite [the blessing of consecration] by itself.

Chapter 4

A woman may be consecrated only voluntarily. If one forces a woman to be consecrated, she is not consecrated. When a man, by contrast, is forced to consecrate [a woman], she is consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 48b states that since it was possible for a man to divorce his wife without her consent (in Talmudic times), there was no need for the Sages to abrogate the man's <i>kiddushin</i>. Even if he was forced into consecrating the woman, he could end their relationship at will. With regard to the woman, by contrast, since she cannot initiate a divorce, our Sages abrogated the marriage bond when she was compelled to establish it against her will.</i><br>A man may consecrate many women at one time, provided that he does so by [transferring] money, and there is enough money to give each one a <i>p'rutah</i>. One of these [women] or another person may accept the money on behalf of them all, [provided] they consent.
When a person [desires to] consecrate a woman, and with her consent gives the <i>kiddushin</i> to another woman, and tells the latter "And you as well," or uses another similar expression, they are both consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The first woman is consecrated based on the principle stated in the following halachah, that a woman may tell a person to give the <i>kiddushin</i> to an agent acting on her behalf. The second woman is consecrated, because she accepted the <i>kiddushin</i> in silence, which is interpreted as acquiescence.</i><br>When, however, the man places [the <i>kiddushin</i>] in the hand [of the second woman] and says, "And you," there is doubt whether or not the <i>kiddushin</i> are valid. Perhaps his intent was only to clarify her feelings. It was as if he asked her, "What would you say about this?" Therefore, she accepted the <i>kiddushin</i>, for she thought he was still asking her about her intent. For this reason, [the question is unresolved,] and the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.
If he told her, "Become consecrated to me with this <i>dinar</i>," and she took it and threw it in front of him or to the sea, into a fire or into anything that will cause it to be destroyed, she is not consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that she discarded the <i>kiddushin</i> is a clear indication of her rejection of his proposal.</i><br>If she told him, "Give it to my father," "...to your father" or "...to so and so," she is not consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Telling the person to give the <i>kiddushin</i> to someone else also indicates that she does not desire them, nor the consequences of accepting them (<i>Tosafot, Kiddushin</i> 8b).</i> If she told him, "Give it to him, so that he will accept it on my behalf,"<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., that the recipient should act as her agent.</i> she is consecrated.
[In the above instance, if] the woman told the man, "Place [the <i>kiddushin</i>] on [this] rock," she is not consecrated. If the rock belonged to her, she is consecrated. If the rock belonged to both of them, [the question is unresolved, and] the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.<br>If he told her, "Be consecrated to me with this loaf of bread," and she told him, "Give it to a poor person," she is not consecrated. [This applies] even if she supports the poor person in question.<br>[If she told him,] "Give it to a dog," she is not consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 30:11) quotes the opinion of the <i>Tur</i>, who maintains that if at the outset the woman says, "Put money down in this place and I will be consecrated to you," or "Give food to this dog and I will be consecrated to you," the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is doubtful. Since the man spent money because of her wishes, one might presume that this can be compared to the law stated in the previous halachah. Nevertheless, since the <i>kiddushin</i> were not given to a person, it is possible that the comparison is not in place, and the <i>kiddushin</i> are not definitely binding. (See also Chapter 5, Halachah 21.)</i> If the dog belonged to her, she is consecrated. If [the dog] was chasing after her and she told him, "Give it to this dog," [the question is unresolved, and] the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.
[The following laws apply when a man] was selling produce, utensils or the like, and a woman came and asked him: "Give me some of these." If he asked her, "If I give them to you, will you be consecrated to me?" and she said, "Yes," she is consecrated when he gives [the items] to her. If, however, she replied to him: "[Just] give them to me," "Heave them over," or another reply that means "Don't fool around with me regarding such matters, just give me [what I asked for]," she is not consecrated although he gave her [what she asked for].<br>A similar [decision is rendered] if [a man] was drinking wine and [a woman] asked him, "Give me a cup," and he asks her, "If I do, will you be consecrated to me with it?" If she replies, "[Just] let me drink," "Give me," "Serve me drink," or "Dish it out," she is not consecrated. Her words imply: "Just give me a drink, and don't fool around with me regarding such matters."
When [a man] consecrates [a woman] in the presence of a single witness, his <i>kiddushin</i>] are of no consequence. [This applies] even when both [the man and the woman] acknowledge [that the <i>kiddushin</i> were given].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 42:2) quotes an opinion that requires stringency in such an instance, but states that leniency should be granted if following the stringent view will cause a woman great difficulty in getting married.<br>Although the need for witnesses with regard to marriage and divorce is derived from an analogy (<i>gezerah shavah</i>) to claims of monetary law, a difference exists between the two. With regard to a financial claim, no witnesses are necessary if both litigants agree regarding a matter.<br>As stated in this halachah, this concept does not apply with regard to the laws of marriage and divorce. Two rationales are given for this matter:<br>a) When the man and woman agree with regard to the establishment of a marriage bond, they are limiting the privileges of others, for they are unable to consecrate the woman. With regard to financial matters, by contrast, the rights of others are not restricted (Rashba).<br>b) There is a fundamental difference between the function of witnesses in cases involving financial matters and their function with regard to wedding and divorce. With regard to financial matters, the function of witnesses is to clarify the truth (<i>eidei berur</i>). With regard to marriage and divorce, by contrast, the witnesses' function is to notarize the event (<i>eidei kiyyum</i>). For a marriage bond to be established - or broken - even when the husband and wife agree that the event took place, witnesses must observe the proceedings (<i>Tumim</i> 90:14, <i>Tzaphnat Paneach, Kallei HaTorah</i>).</i> Surely this applies when [a man] consecrates [a woman] without any witnesses at all [observing the act].<br>When [a man] consecrates [a woman] in the presence of individuals who are disqualified from serving as witnesses by Scriptural law, she is not consecrated. [When he consecrates her in the presence of] individuals who are disqualified from serving as witnesses by Rabbinic law,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling sheds light on a theme of larger scope: the interrelation between Rabbinic law and Scriptural law. For the fact that a woman needs a <i>get</i> to marry another person appears to indicate that according to Scriptural law, the <i>kiddushin</i> are effective.</i> or in the presence of witnesses regarding whom there is doubt whether or not they are acceptable according to Scriptural law, [the following rules apply:] If he desires to consummate the marriage, he should consecrate the woman again in the presence of acceptable witnesses. If he does not desire to consummate the marriage, the woman must receive a <i>get</i> from him [to enable her to marry others], because of the doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Many of the manuscript copies and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> state that the obligation to give a <i>get</i> is of Rabbinic origin. This view would appear to be supported by the Rambam's ruling in Chapter 5, Halachah 1.</i> [This ruling applies] even when the woman denies [the matter], contradicting the witnesses and saying that she was never consecrated.<br>This ruling applies with regard to all situations in which the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt. If [the man] desires to consummate the marriage, he should consecrate the woman again in a manner that is unequivocally acceptable. If he does not desire to consummate the marriage, the woman must receive a <i>get</i> from him, because of the doubt.
When a minor consecrates [a woman], his <i>kiddushin</i> are of no consequence. When, by contrast, a male past the age of majority consecrates a girl below the age of majority who is an orphan,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">If she is not an orphan and has never been married, the right to consecrate her belongs to her father, not to her.</i> or who has left her father's authority,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., she was married, the marriage was consummated, and then she was either divorced or widowed. In this and the above instance, the girl does not have sufficient authority to create a marriage bond that is binding according to Scriptural law. Nevertheless, a bond that is binding according to Rabbinic law may be established, as the halachah continues to explain.<br>The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 155:2) quotes opinions that maintain that the above applies only when the girl's marriage was arranged by her brother or her mother. If she arranged the marriage herself, it is not binding, even according to Rabbinic law. The Ra'avad mentions a third opinion, which states that for a girl between the ages of six and ten, the marriage must be arranged by her family to be binding. After the age of ten, it is binding even if she arranged it herself.</i> [different rules apply]: If she is below the age of six, even if she is one who shows deep understanding of secret matters,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the commentary of <i>Metzudot</i> on Isaiah 3:3.</i> and can differentiate and discern, she is not married, and there is no need for <i>mi'un</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The annulment of a marriage that a girl below the age of majority initiates, as explained in the following halachah.<br>When a girl is below the age of six, we assume that she does not have sufficient understanding of the nature of marriage to make a commitment that is binding in any way.</i><br>If she is more than ten years old, even when she is very foolish, since she willingly accepted the <i>kiddushin</i>, she is consecrated [according to Rabbinic law] and [must perform] <i>mi'un</i> [should she desire to nullify the marriage]. If she is between the ages of six and ten, [the rabbis] must evaluate her ability to discern. If she is able to differentiate and discern with regard to matters of marriage and <i>kiddushin</i>, [the marriage is binding according to Rabbinic law] and <i>mi'un</i> is necessary. If she lacks [this degree of discernment], she is not consecrated [at all], and need not perform <i>mi'un</i> [to nullify the marriage].
What is meant by the statement that she is consecrated [according to Rabbinic law], and [must perform] <i>mi'un</i> [should she desire to nullify the marriage]? If she was consecrated but no longer desires to remain with her husband, she must perform <i>mi'un</i> in the presence of two witnesses. She should say: "I no longer desire him." Afterwards, she leaves [the relationship] without a divorce, as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Gerushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 11, Halachah 8.</i><br>Why does she leave [the relationship] without a divorce? Because the consecration is not absolutely binding according to Scriptural law; it is merely a Rabbinic institution. [According to Scriptural law, the outcome] is tentative. If she continues living with her husband until she reaches the age of majority, the <i>kiddushin</i> are finalized, and she becomes a married woman in the complete sense of the term.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam states in <i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 11:6, if the girl reaches majority before she states that she desires to nullify the marriage bond, Rabbinic law requires her to receive a <i>get</i> before she marries another person. If she continues living with her husband and they engage in sexual relations after she attains majority, the marriage bond is binding according to Scriptural law.</i> There is no need for [her husband] to consecrate her again after she attains majority. If she does not want [to continue] living with him, she must perform <i>mi'un</i>; she then leaves [the relationship] without a divorce.
When a male deaf mute marries a mentally competent woman, or a female deaf mute marries a mentally competent man, the marriage bond is not absolutely binding according to Scriptural law; it is merely a Rabbinic institution. Therefore, if a mentally competent man consecrates the wife of a deaf mute man who is herself mentally competent, she is considered to be consecrated to the mentally competent man. He must give her a <i>get</i>, and she is permitted to remain married to her deaf mute husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's decision, stating that the woman should not be allowed to remain married to her deaf mute husband. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> justifies the Rambam's decision, explaining that since the marriage of a deaf mute is a Rabbinic institution, our Sages did not impose the same restrictions that would apply had the marriage been effective according to Scriptural law.<br>Others state that the Rambam's ruling applies only when the mentally competent man merely consecrated the deaf mute's wife. If he consummated the marriage (<i>nisu'in</i>), the deaf mute is forbidden to continue living with her after her divorce.</i><br>When, by contrast, a mentally incompetent man consecrates a mentally competent woman, or a mentally competent man consecrates a mentally incompetent woman, the marriage bond is not at all binding - neither according to Scriptural law nor according to Rabbinic law.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages differentiated between the marriage of a deaf mute and that of a mentally incompetent person as follows: the marriage of a deaf mute may be harmonious and has the possibility of enduring. The marriage of a mentally incompetent person, by contrast, will surely be plagued by friction and will not endure. For a mentally competent will never be comfortable living with a mentally incompetent person. (See Chapter 11, Halachah 6 and <i>Yevamot</i> 112b.)<br>The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 44:2) states that this is a sensitive matter, for it is difficult to determine when a person is completely incompetent or not.</i>
When a sexually impotent male - whether a <i>s'ris chamah</i> or a <i>s'ris adam</i> - consecrates [a woman], and similarly, when [a man] consecrates an <i>aylonit</i>, the <i>kiddushin</i> are absolutely binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">All the terms mentioned in this halachah are explained in Chapter 2. Although <i>s'ris adam</i> (a person who has been castrated) is forbidden to marry, if he violates this prohibition, the marriage bond is binding and a <i>get</i> is required for severing the relationship. With regard to an <i>aylonit</i>, see Chapter 24, Halachot 1-2, and the accompanying notes.</i>
When a <i>tumtum</i> or an <i>androgynous</i> consecrates a woman, or when either of these individuals has been consecrated by a man, there is doubt whether these <i>kiddushin</i> are binding, and because of the doubt, a <i>get</i> is required.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 44:5) quotes the <i>Tur</i> as stating that an <i>androgynous</i> is considered to be a male, and the <i>kiddushin</i> that he gives are binding.</i>
When a person consecrates one of the women forbidden as <i>arayot</i>, his act is of no consequence. For <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding with regard to these forbidden relationships, with the exception of [<i>kiddushin</i> given] a <i>niddah</i>. When a man consecrates a <i>niddah</i>, the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding absolutely. It is, nevertheless, improper to do so.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling is relevant in the present age, for it is frequent that a woman is a <i>niddah</i> at the time of a marriage. A wedding should never be planned to coincide with the woman's <i>niddah</i> state. Nevertheless, since weddings are often planned well in advance, and women's menstrual cycles are flexible, it is possible that the calculations will be in error, and the wedding will be scheduled for the time when the woman is a <i>niddah</i>. In such a situation, the commonly accepted ruling is to hold the wedding. Nevertheless, restrictions are placed on the couple's being in private until the woman purifies herself. Needless to say, sexual relations are forbidden.</i>
When a married woman accepts <i>kiddushin</i> from another man in the presence of her husband, she is considered to be consecrated to the second man. For a woman's word is accepted when she tells her husband to his face that he had divorced her. We assume it axiomatically that a woman would not act so brazenly in her husband's presence [unless it were true].<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad states that the woman's word should be accepted only insofar as to require her to receive a <i>get</i> from the second person. She is not allowed to marry him, nor may she collect her <i>ketubah</i> from her first husband unless she proves that she has been divorced. The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:2) states that in the present age, since brazen behavior is more common, the presumption upon which the Rambam's ruling rests is no longer a viable support.</i><br>If, however, the other person consecrates her outside her husband's presence, these <i>kiddushin</i> are not considered to be binding unless she brings proof that she was divorced before she was consecrated. As long as she is outside her husband's presence, it is possible that she will act brazenly.
When a man consecrates one of the <i>shniyot</i> or a woman forbidden because of a negative commandment [not associated with <i>karet</i>] or because of a positive commandment, the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding absolutely. [The same ruling applies when] a <i>yavam</i> consecrates a woman who was married to the same man as his <i>yevamah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The situation is as follows: A man who was married to two women died while childless. As required by Deuteronomy, Chapter 5, one of his brothers (the <i>yavam</i>) marries one of his widows (the <i>yevamah</i>). Afterwards, neither the <i>yavam</i> nor any other of the deceased's brothers is allowed to marry the deceased's second wife. (See <i>Hilchot Yibbum</i> 1:12.)</i><br>There is one exception to the above principle: when a person other than [the <i>yavam</i>] consecrates a <i>yevamah</i>, [the question is unresolved, and] the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt. For our Sages were unsure whether the <i>kiddushin</i> of a <i>yevamah</i> are valid, like those of others in which the relationship is forbidden by merely a negative commandment, or whether the <i>kiddushin</i> are of no consequence, as in the case of an incestuous relationship.<br>In all the situations mentioned above, although the man who gave the <i>kiddushin</i> is forbidden to consummate the marriage, he must terminate it by giving a <i>get</i>.
[When] a man consecrates a gentile woman or a [Canaanite] maidservant, the <i>kiddushin</i> are of no consequence; the woman's status is the same after receiving the <i>kiddushin</i> as beforehand.
