Similarly, when a gentile or a [Canaanite] servant consecrates a Jewish woman, the <i>kiddushin</i> are of no consequence.<br>When an apostate Jew consecrates [a woman], his <i>kiddushin</i> are absolutely valid, despite the fact that he willingly worships a false deity. The woman must receive a <i>get</i> from him.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Rav David Cohen (Section 9, Responsum 1) states that the intent is that we are obligated to force the apostate to divorce his wife, lest he cause her to violate Torah law.</i>
When a man consecrates a woman who is half a maidservant and half a free woman,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., a Canaanite maidservant who was owned in partnership by two masters. One granted the woman her freedom, but the other did not.</i> she is not completely consecrated until she becomes [totally] free. Once she becomes free, the <i>kiddushin</i> are [automatically] completed, like the <i>kiddushin</i> of a minor who comes of age. There is no need for her to be consecrated again.<br>If another man consecrates such a woman after she was granted her freedom [before the person who consecrated her originally consummates their marriage], there is doubt regarding the matter,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the commentary of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, who explains that there is a question whether or not the <i>kiddushin</i> of the first person are valid. Nevertheless, if the couple marry and consummate their relationship, this establishes their wedding bond.</i> and the status of both their <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.
What then is a <i>shifchah charufah</i> [a betrothed maidservant] as described by the Torah [Leviticus 19:20]? A woman who is half a maidservant and half a free woman, who was consecrated by a Hebrew servant.<br>When a male who is half a servant and half a free man consecrates a woman, [the matter is unresolved, and] the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.
When a drunk gives [a woman] <i>kiddushin</i>, they are valid, even if he is very drunk. If he reaches a state of drunkenness comparable to that of Lot,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Who was totally unaware of what he was doing (Genesis, Chapter 19).</i> the <i>kiddushin</i> are of no consequence. This matter requires ample deliberation.
When a man gives money worth less than a <i>p'rutah</i> as <i>kiddushin</i>, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid. When a man consecrates a woman with food or with a utensil worth less than a <i>p'rutah</i>, the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt; perhaps the <i>kiddushin</i> are worth a <i>p'rutah</i> in another place.<br>From this one can deduce that whenever a person consecrates a woman with an article worth money, if it is worth a <i>p'rutah</i> in that country the <i>kiddushin</i> are definitely binding. If it is not worth a <i>p'rutah</i> [there], the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt [as above].<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries (see <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 31:6) debate the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> when one knows for a fact that they are worth a <i>p'rutah</i> in another place. Some maintain that they are absolutely binding, and others maintain that their status remains doubtful.</i><br>It appears to me that if [a man] consecrated [a woman] with cooked food, a vegetable that will not be preserved or the like, and the item is not worth a <i>p'rutah</i> in that place, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding at all. For by the time this item reaches another place, it will spoil and be worthless. This is a reasonable inference; one may rely on it.
When [a man] consecrates a woman with less than a <i>p'rutah's</i> worth, or he consecrates two women with a <i>p'rutah</i>, the women are not consecrated. [This applies] even when he sends wedding presents [worth more than a <i>p'rutah</i>] afterwards. Similarly, when a minor consecrates a woman, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid, despite the fact that he sent marriage presents after he had attained majority. [This ruling was delivered because the presents] were sent because of the original <i>kiddushin</i>, which were invalid [and therefore are not considered to be significant in their own right].
When [a man] consecrates a woman by [giving her] money or a legal document, he does not have to place the <i>kiddushin</i> in her hand. Instead, if she consents that he throw them to her and he does so, she is consecrated, whether he throws them into her hand, her bosom, her courtyard or her field.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 30) states that the woman's field or courtyard must be protected. It is possible to state that this is the Rambam's opinion as well, and he relies on the statements he makes in <i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 4:9 regarding giving a present (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). Nevertheless, it is possible to differentiate between the two and explain that the laws governing <i>kiddushin</i> are more lenient. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 30:3 maintains that this is the view of the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 30:1). See <i>Chelkat Mechokeik</i> 30:2.</i><br>If she is standing in a domain belonging to her [prospective] husband, he must place them in her hand or in her bosom. If she is standing in a domain that belongs to both of them, and he threw <i>kiddushin</i> to her with her consent, but they did not reach her hand or her bosom, the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt. Even when she tells him, "Put the <i>kiddushin</i> down in this place," if the place belongs to both of them the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Although this would not be acceptable with regard to a business transaction (<i>Hilchot Mechirah</i>, Chapter 4), an exception is made here, because of the possibility that the man lent the woman rights to his share of the property.</i>
[The following rules apply when the two] are standing in the public domain or in a domain that does not belong to either of them, and he throws <i>kiddushin</i> to her: If they are closer to him, she is not consecrated. If they are closer to her, she is consecrated. If they are halfway between the two of them, or if there is doubt whether they were closer to him or to her,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 29:9, which interprets this as meaning that two pairs of witnesses observed the throwing of the <i>kiddushin</i>, one maintained that the <i>kiddushin</i> were able to be guarded by the man, and one that they were able to be guarded by the woman. If, however, there was only one pair of witnesses, and they were unsure whether the woman could guard them, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not effective, because it is as if they were given without being observed by witnesses.</i> and they were lost before they reached her hand, there is doubt regarding the status of the <i>kiddushin</i>.<br>What is meant by "closer to him," or "closer to her"? A situation in which he can guard [the <i>kiddushin</i>] and she cannot is considered as "closer to him." One in which she can guard them and he cannot is considered to be "closer to her."<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Makneh</i> states that the Rambam relies on his statements in <i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 5:13, which state that the woman must be able to bend over and take the object. Others do not make such a stipulation.</i> One in which they can both guard them or neither can guard them is considered to be "halfway between the two of them."

Chapter 5

When a man consecrates a woman with an object from which it is forbidden to derive benefit - e.g., a mixture of milk and meat, <i>chametz</i> on Pesach, or other similar objects from which it is prohibited to derive benefit - she is not consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is forbidden to derive benefit from the article, according to the Torah, it has no value whatsoever. For a woman to be consecrated, she must receive an article worth a <i>p'rutah</i>.</i> [This ruling applies] even if the prohibition against deriving benefit from the object is merely Rabbinic in origin<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 28) understand the Rambam as saying that all articles that are forbidden to be used by Rabbinic decree cannot establish a bond of <i>kiddushin</i>. Rav Yosef Karo (in the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>) differs and explains that the example given by the Rambam specifies the scope of the ruling. Only when a Rabbinic commandment has its source in a prohibition from the Torah are the <i>kiddushin</i> of no effect.<br>From the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Pesachim</i> 2:1), his view is clearly that even if the prohibition is entirely Rabbinic in origin, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding.<br>In the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 28:21), Rav Yosef Karo follows the opinion of Rabbenu Asher, who states that if the article is forbidden by force of Rabbinic decree, and that prohibition has no source in the Torah, the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding. If the prohibition has its source in the Torah, the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.<br>(The rationale for this ruling is that since, according to Scriptural law, the article is worth money, and the woman accepts it as <i>kiddushin</i>, the criteria for <i>kiddushin</i> have been met.)<br>The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 28:52 justifies the <i>Maggid Mishneh's</i> interpretation of the Rambam's view, explaining that since in practice the article is worthless because of the Rabbinic decree, the woman has not been given an article of value, and the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding. In support, he cites another example: The man must own the article he gives as <i>kiddushin</i>. If he acquired that article through a <i>kinyan</i> (contractual act) that is Rabbinic in origin and is not accepted by Scriptural law, the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding.<br><i>Kin'at Eliyahu</i> explains that the difference between these two views can be explained using the concepts of <i>cheftza</i> (the article) and <i>gavra</i> (the person). The Rambam's perspective puts the emphasis on the person, the woman receiving the <i>kiddushin</i>. She must receive an object from which she can derive benefit. Hence, since the Rabbis forbade deriving benefit from such an object, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding.<br>Rabbenu Asher, by contrast, puts the emphasis on the article given as <i>kiddushin</i>. For <i>kiddushin</i> to be effective, an article that is worth a <i>p'rutah</i> must be given. Since according to Scriptural law the article has intrinsic worth, the fact that our Sages forbade using it is not relevant in this context.</i> - e.g., <i>chametz</i> during the sixth hour on the fourteenth of Nisan.
If a man transgresses and sells an article from which it is forbidden to derive benefit, and consecrates [a woman] with the money [he receives] for it, the <i>kiddushin</i> are valid. [There is one] exception. If a person consecrates a woman with the money [received] for a false deity, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid. For it is forbidden to derive benefit from the money received for a false deity, just as [it is forbidden to derive benefit from] the false deity itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 7:9.</i><br>When [a man] consecrates [a woman] with the dung of cows [consecrated to] a false deity, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid. For it is forbidden to derive benefit from anything produced by entities [consecrated to] a false deity, as [Deuteronomy 13:18] states: "Let nothing that is condemned cling to your hand."<br>If, by contrast, [a man] consecrates [a woman] with the dung of an ox condemned to be stoned,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For goring a person. (See Exodus 21:28.)</i> the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding. Although it is forbidden to derive benefit from an ox condemned to be stoned, this prohibition does not apply to its dung. For the dung is considered of negligible importance when compared to the ox.
When [a man] consecrates [a woman] with the produce of the Sabbatical year,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Although the produce of the Sabbatical year is ownerless, once a person takes possession of it, it becomes his private property and has value. Hence, it can be used to consecrate a woman.</i> with the ashes of the Red Heifer, or with water that was drawn for the purpose of sprinkling [the ashes of the Red Heifer],<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam states in his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Kiddushin</i> 2:10, based on <i>Kiddushin</i> 58a), it is forbidden to receive money for consecrating or sprinkling the water of the ashes of the Red Heifer. One may, however, take payment for drawing the water and transporting it. Thus, the woman can derive this benefit from the water and/or ashes she is given.</i> the <i>kiddushin</i> are valid.<br>[The following rules apply when a man] consecrates [a woman] with property dedicated to the Temple. If he was unaware [that the property had been dedicated], the <i>kiddushin</i> are valid. He must give the value [of the dedicated property] and an [additional fifth] to the Temple treasury and bring a guilt offering, as is required of all those who unwittingly make mundane use of property dedicated to the Temple.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Me'ilah</i> 1:3.</i> If he consecrated the woman knowing [that the property was dedicated], she is not consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For dedicated property that was consciously used for a person's private purposes retains its sacred nature and does not enter the possession of the person to whom it was given. (See <i>Hilchot Me'ilah</i> 6:3.)</i>
When [a man] consecrates [a woman] with the produce of the second tithe - whether unknowingly or knowingly - the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid. For unless a person redeems [this produce], it does not belong to him to use for his other purposes, since with regard to [this] tithe, [Leviticus 27:30] states: "It is God's."<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">We are required to eat the produce of the second tithe in Jerusalem or redeem it and use the money to buy food to be eaten in Jerusalem. Although one derives personal benefit from eating this produce, it is not considered to be one's own property.</i>
When a priest consecrates [a woman] with his share of offerings of the most sacred nature or [his share of] offerings of lesser sanctity, she is not consecrated. For one was permitted merely to eat these sacrifices.<br>When, by contrast, a priest consecrates [a woman] with the great <i>terumah</i>, the <i>terumah</i> taken from the tithe or with the first fruits, the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding. [This same ruling applies] when a Levite consecrates [a woman] with [produce from] the first tithe, or an Israelite consecrates [a woman] with [produce from] the tithe of the poor.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In all the latter instances, although the person receives the produce in question because of the Torah's decree - and with regard to <i>terumah</i>, it still possesses a dimension of ritual sanctity - once he has received it, it is regarded as his personal property entirely, and he may use it as he pleases. Hence, it is fit to be used to consecrate a woman.</i>
The gifts [required to be separated from produce] that have not been separated are considered as if they have already been separated. Therefore, when an Israelite inherited produce from his maternal grandfather who was a priest, and none of the required gifts had been separated from that produce, he may separate the <i>terumah</i> and the tithes [and keep the portions to be given to the priests as his own]. It is as if he inherited the <i>terumah</i> and the tithes from his maternal grandfather. Therefore, if he consecrates a woman with them, she is consecrated. Although they are not fit for [the Israelite] to eat, he has the right to sell them to someone for whom they are fit.<br>When, by contrast, an Israelite consecrates [a woman] with <i>terumah</i> that he separates from his grain heap, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not effective. For he does not have the right to sell this <i>terumah</i>; he possesses merely the privilege of giving it to the priest of his choice. This privilege is not considered to be money.
[The following rules apply when] a person consecrates a woman [with property that] he robbed, stole or took against its owner's will. If the owner has despaired of the return of the article,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">A thief or robber cannot normally become the legal owner of an article through the owner's despair alone. The article must be given to a third party or undergo a change before it is considered to have left its original owner's property. Nevertheless, in this instance, since the woman receiving can legally acquire the article - for she is a third party - the <i>kiddushin</i> are effective (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> and it is known<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's intent is that if the witnesses to the consecration know that the article was stolen, they must know that the owner of the article has despaired of its return. If they do not have such knowledge, they cannot serve as witnesses. Hence, the <i>kiddushin</i> are invalid, for it is as if they were performed without being observed by witnesses (<i>Noda Biy'hudah, Even HaEzer</i>, Volume II, Responsum 77).</i> that [the man] acquired it through the owner's despair, the consecration is effective. If not, it is not valid.
When a person enters a colleague's home and takes an object, food or the like, and consecrates a woman, she is not consecrated. [This ruling applies] even when the owner comes and says, "Why did you not give her a more valuable article than the one you gave her?" He is making this statement only to prevent the person from being shamed [and it does not reflect his true intent]. Since the man consecrated [a woman] with property belonging to a colleague without the colleague's knowledge, this is robbery, and the woman is not consecrated.<br>If [the man] consecrated [the woman] with an article that the owner would not object [to its being taken] - e.g., a date or a nut - the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries have questioned this ruling, for it appears to be the Rambam's own addition. The <i>Noda Biy'hudah</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i>, Volume I, Responsum 59) states that it would appear that this refers to a situation in which the owner is present and does not object. Nevertheless, since none of the sages of the earlier generations offered this interpretation, he is not willing to do so.<br>The <i>Edut BiY'hosef</i> (Volume II, Responsum 77) states that this ruling depends on those in the previous halachah. Since <i>kiddushin</i> are valid after the owner relinquishes his ownership of stolen property by despairing of its return, they are valid in the present instance. Since the owner does not object to the person's taking the object, he is considered to have relinquished his ownership. A similar interpretation is found in the <i>Chatam Sofer, Even HaEzer</i>, Responsum 85.<br>The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 28:45 states that the doubt is that perhaps the owner indeed objects. The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> explains that the doubt concerns the object's worth. Although it is not of significant value in the place of the <i>kiddushin</i>, maybe it is valuable in another locale, as stated in Chapter 4, Halachah 19.</i>
When a person owns merchandise in partnership with a colleague and divides the merchandise without his colleague's knowledge, using it to consecrate [a woman], the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid. [The rationale is that for the division of a partnership's assets to be effective,] an evaluation by the court is necessary. One [partner] may not take what he wants as his own and leave [the remainder for his colleague].
[The following rules apply when] a person robbed or stole an article from a woman or took it without her consent, and afterwards consecrated her with the article that he took from her, saying: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this." If the two were already engaged, and she took the article in silence, she is consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">We interpret her silence as implying that she granted him the stolen object as a present and accepted it as <i>kiddushin</i> (Rashi, <i>Kiddushin</i> 52b). There is a difference of opinion among the Rabbis whether or not he is obligated to return the value of the stolen property to her. The Rashba maintains that he is not required, while Rabbenu Nissim states that he is. (See the Ramah and commentaries, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 28:2.)</i> If, however, there was never an engagement between them, she is not consecrated, even if she remained silent when he gave her [the stolen articles] as <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">For she merely accepted her own property.</i> If, however, she explicitly agreed [to the <i>kiddushin</i>], she is consecrated.
Similar [concepts apply when a man] entrusts an article to [a woman] for safekeeping and tells her: "Take care of this article," and afterwards tells her: "Behold, you are consecrated with it." If he told her this before she took [possession of] the article, and she took it in silence, she is consecrated. If, however, he made his second statement after she had accepted the article for the purpose of safekeeping, and she remained silent, [the <i>kiddushin</i>] are not valid. For whenever [a woman] remains silent after money has been given, [the <i>kiddushin</i>] are not valid. If, however, she explicitly agreed, she is consecrated, even though she made the statement after accepting the article.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since she acknowledged the <i>kiddushin</i>, the situation becomes parallel to that mentioned in Halachah 18.</i>
[The following rules apply when a man] pays a debt that he owed [a woman] and [upon paying it,] says: "You are consecrated with it." If the two were engaged, [the man made the statement] before she accepted the money, and she accepted it in silence, she is consecrated. If they were not engaged, she is not consecrated unless she explicitly agrees.<br>If he states [his desire to consecrate her] after she accepted payment of the debt, she is not consecrated, even if she explicitly agrees. For nothing has been given her; she merely took what was rightfully hers. The debt he owed was repaid when she took the money, and she cannot demand repayment again.
When [a man] consecrates [a woman] with a debt, even with [a debt that is recorded] in a promissory note,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 28:7) notes that if the promissory note is worth a <i>p'rutah</i> and he returns it, there are opinions that maintain that the consecration is binding.</i> she is not consecrated.<br>What is implied? [The woman] owed [the man] a <i>dinar</i>; if he tells her, "Behold, you are consecrated to me with the <i>dinar</i> that you owe me," she is not consecrated. [The rationale is that] a loan is given to be spent, and there is nothing that presently exists for her to derive benefit from [and to accept as <i>kiddushin</i>]. For she has [- or it is as if she has - ]<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even if she has not actually spent the money, from the time she received the loan, the money is hers and not the lender's, and he cannot consecrate her with it (<i>Beit Yosef, Even HaEzer</i> 28). See also <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 28:19.</i> already spent that <i>dinar</i> and has derived benefit from it already.
[A different rule applies when] he has given her a loan [and received] collateral for it. If he consecrates her with the loan and returns the collateral,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Kiddushin</i> 19a, states that the <i>kiddushin</i> are effective even if the collateral is not returned. Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 28:11) appears to favor the Rambam's view, it also quotes the other opinion.</i> she is consecrated. For she derives benefit from the collateral from that time onward, and thus, [as a result of the <i>kiddushin</i>,] she has derived benefit.
When [a man] consecrates [a woman] with the benefit [derived from] a loan, the consecration is valid.<br>What is implied? The consecration is binding if he lends her 200 <i>zuz</i> [at the time of the <i>kiddushin</i>] and tells her: "Behold, you are consecrated to me through the benefit [you receive] by my extending the length of this loan for you. It may be in your possession for so many days, and I will not demand payment until this date." For she is receiving benefit now [from the opportunity] to use the loan until the end of the time period fixed.<br>It is forbidden to make [such a condition], because it is like taking interest.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since in addition to the eventual repayment of the debt, the person also receives the benefit of consecrating the woman, it is regarded like interest. The Rabbis (Meiri, <i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i>) explain that the Rambam's wording is precise. The expression "like interest" implies that it is not actually considered to be taking interest, as forbidden by Scriptural law.</i> My teachers interpreted the expression "the benefit [derived from] a loan," in a way that is not worthy of mention.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is referring to Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi, who interprets the passage from <i>Kiddushin</i> 6b as referring to a person who extends the length of a loan at the time that payment is due. The Rambam does not accept that interpretation, because it is not logical that extending the length of the loan would be more effective than forfeiting the debt entirely (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).<br>Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi's view is also followed by Rashi and the Ra'avad. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 28:9) quotes the Rambam's interpretation (for even the opinions that differ agree that such <i>kiddushin</i> are binding). In the law that follows, it also quotes the opinion of Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi. Although the opinion of the Rambam is mentioned, the other view is favored. The Ramah, however, considers the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> to be doubtful because of the Rambam's view.</i>
If [the man] tells [the woman]: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this <i>p'rutah</i> and with the debt that you owe me," she is consecrated. Similarly, if he tells her, "[Behold, you are consecrated...] with the debt that you owe me and with this <i>p'rutah</i>, the consecration is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Although the man mentions the debt, since he also gives her a <i>p'rutah</i>, we assume that she considers the money that she actually receives together with the loan. Therefore, the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding (<i>Kiddushin</i> 46a).</i>
When [a man] is owed a debt by a third party, and he tells [a woman] in the presence of the third party: "Behold, you are consecrated to me by virtue of the debt that I am owed by this person," the consecration is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 6:8, when such a statement is made in the presence of all the concerned parties, our Sages accepted it as a formal means of transferring the debt. This law shows that even when money is transferred through means ordained by Rabbinic and not Scriptural law, the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding according to Scriptural law.<br>There are opinions that maintain that the woman is not consecrated. These opinions maintain that even after such a transfer has been made, the original creditor can nullify a debt that has been transferred through such a process. Since there is a possibility that the debt will be nullified, they maintain that the woman will not make the commitment required by <i>kiddushin</i>. (See Rabbenu Nissim and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 28:13 and commentaries.)</i>
[The following rule applies when a man] consecrates [a woman] with an object that he has entrusted to her for safekeeping or with an article that he has lent her: If the entrusted object or borrowed article is worth a <i>p'rutah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Yemenite manuscripts and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The wording of the standard printed text is somewhat confusing. It could be rendered: "If a <i>p'rutah's</i> worth of the article remains..." - i.e., even if the article is lost or stolen, if a <i>p'rutah's</i> worth remains - the consecration is binding. See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 28:6) and commentaries.</i> and it exists within her property, she is consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the entrusted object or borrowed article has been lost, stolen or destroyed, even if the woman is obligated to reimburse the man for its value, that obligation is considered similar to other debts, and the woman cannot be consecrated through it.<br>Although the entrusted object or borrowed article was located in the woman's property at the time of the <i>kiddushin</i>, since she was not the legal owner, she is considered to have received sufficient benefit to make the <i>kiddushin</i> effective.</i>
[The following rule applies when a man] tells [a woman]: "Behold, you are consecrated to me in consideration of my speaking to the ruling authorities on your behalf." Although [the man] indeed spoke to the ruling authorities on her behalf - [and his words had an effect,] causing them to refrain from prosecuting her, she is not consecrated unless he gives her a <i>p'rutah</i> of his own.<br>[The rationale is that] the benefit that she received from his speaking [on her behalf] is regarded as a loan,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Speaking on her behalf is considered equivalent to working for her. Hence, an equation is established between this law and the following halachah.</i> and when one consecrates [a woman] with a loan, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding.
[The following rule applies when a man] tells [a woman]: "Behold, you are consecrated to me [in return] for the work that I will perform on your behalf." Although [the man] indeed performs [the work he promised], she is not consecrated unless he gives her a <i>p'rutah</i> of his own.<br>[The rationale is that] a worker earns his wages [continuously] from [the time he] begins [working] until the end. As he performs a portion of the work, he earns an [equivalent] portion of his wages. Thus, [in the above situation, the man's] wages are considered to be a debt that she [owes him].<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is not as if the man's entire wage becomes due at the time he completes his work. Instead, for each moment of work, he earns a corresponding amount of his wages. This money is considered as a loan which is not due until the end of his employment. Thus he is in fact consecrating the women with a loan.</i> And when one consecrates [a woman] with a loan, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding.
[The following rule applies when a woman] tells [a man]: "Give so and so a present, and I will be consecrated to you." If he tells her, "Behold, you are consecrated to me for the sake of the present I gave upon your request," the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 7a compares this situation to that of a guarantor who becomes liable to pay a loan if the borrower cannot. In both instances, the benefit received by another person causes the person who made the commitment (the guarantor or the woman) to incur an obligation.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> (4:4) and others compare this law to Chapter 4, Halachah 4, but explain that there is a difference between the two cases. In Chapter 4, the man does not respond to the woman's suggestion, while in this halachah, he makes a clear statement acknowledging the woman's offer of <i>kiddushin</i>. The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 29:2) puts the emphasis on the fact that in this halachah, the woman initially made this suggestion, even before the man proposed the <i>kiddushin</i>. In the previous law, by contrast, her statement was made in response to his proposal, and her facetious intent becomes clear.</i> Although she [personally] did not receive anything, she derived benefit from the fact that her will was carried out, and the other derived benefit because of her.<br>Similarly, if she told him, "Give a <i>dinar</i> to so and so as a present, and I will be consecrated to him," the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 7a derives this law by making a twofold comparison: to a guarantor (as in the law explained in the first portion of the halachah) and to a Canaanite servant. To explain: The servant becomes free when other people give his master money for that purpose, even though he himself gives nothing at all. Similarly, the person receiving the present acquires the woman as a wife even though he did not give anything for that purpose himself. Although there is a difference between the two - because the servant's owner receives money for the sake of freeing him and the woman does not receive any money herself - the comparison to a guarantor resolves that difficulty, as explained above.</i> provided the person who receives the present tells [the woman]: "Behold, you are consecrated to me by virtue of the pleasure [you derived] from the present that I received at your request."
[To cite a similar instance: A man] tells [a woman]: "Take this <i>dinar</i> as a present and become consecrated to so and so"; the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding provided that the other person tells her: "Behold, you are consecrated to me by virtue of the benefit you received on my behalf," despite the fact that he himself did not give her anything.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin, ibid.</i>, derives this law from a comparison to a Canaanite servant, as explained above.</i><br>[The following rule applies when a woman] tells [a man]: "Take this <i>dinar</i> as a present and I will become consecrated to you"; he receives the present and tells her "Behold, you are consecrated to me by virtue of the pleasure [you received] in my accepting a present from you." If he is an important person, she is consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 27:9) states that clarification is necessary to determine what is meant by "an important person." Because of the doubt involved, it is proper to require a divorce if the woman desires to become consecrated to another man (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 27:21).</i> For she derives satisfaction from the fact that he has benefited from her, and for the sake of this satisfaction, she consecrates herself to him.
When [a man] tells a woman: "Become consecrated to me with a <i>dinar</i>. [Take this article] as security until I give you the <i>dinar</i>," she is not consecrated to him. For she did not receive the <i>dinar</i>, and the security was not given to her for it to be her own.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Thus, it is as if she has received nothing. Therefore, she is not consecrated.</i><br>If the man has in his possession security that he was given for a debt that a third party owes him, and he gives a woman the security as <i>kiddushin</i>, the consecration is binding although [the security] does not belong to him. For a creditor has certain rights with regard to the ownership of security.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 28:12) quotes the <i>Tur</i> as stating that this law applies only when the security was taken at the time the loan was given. Otherwise, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding.</i>
When [a man] tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this <i>dinar</i> on condition that you return it to me," she is not consecrated, regardless of whether or not she returns it. For if she does not return it, his condition will not be met. And if she returns it, she will not have derived any benefit, for she will not have received anything.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's wording, it appears that there is no reason to say that the woman has been consecrated. Rabbenu Asher and others maintain that according to Scriptural law, the consecration is valid, for a present of this nature is considered to be a valid transaction. It is merely that the Rabbis nullified these <i>kiddushin</i> lest they resemble <i>chalifin</i> (barter).<br>The difference between these two approaches is that the Rambam puts the emphasis on the benefit the woman receives (or does not receive). Hence in this situation, since the woman did not receive any benefit, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding. Rabbenu Asher, by contrast, puts the emphasis on whether or not the man performed a valid act of transfer. Since he did, the <i>kiddushin</i> would be binding, were it not for our Sages' decree (<i>Or Sameach</i>).</i>
[These rulings were issued with regard to the following instances:] [A man] gave [a woman] a wreath of myrtle or the like and told her: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this." She accepted it, but [protested,] saying: "But it is not worth a <i>p'rutah</i>." He responded, "Become consecrated with the four <i>zuz</i> that are hidden in the wreath."<br>If she said yes, she is consecrated. If she remained silent, she is not consecrated with this money, for remaining silent after money has been given is of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., at the time the money was given, she was not aware of it, and afterwards to be consecrated she must explicitly express her consent. Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi differs and maintains that in such an instance there is a doubt whether or not the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding, and the more stringent ruling must be followed in every instance. His view is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 28:5).</i> There is nonetheless a doubt: perhaps the <i>kiddushin</i> are valid, lest the wreath be worth a <i>p'rutah</i> in another place.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 4, Halachah 19 above.</i>
[The following rules apply when a man] tells a woman: "Become consecrated to me with this date. Become consecrated to me with this one. Become consecrated to me with this one." If one of them is worth a <i>p'rutah</i>, she is consecrated. If not, the <i>kiddushin</i> are merely of doubtful status,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Karo raises a question on this ruling, noting that <i>Kiddushin</i> 46a interprets this law as following the reasoning of Rabbi Shimon. In similar instances (see <i>Hilchot Sh'vuot</i> 7:10 and <i>Hilchot Nedarim</i> 4:11), the Rambam rejects Rabbi Shimon's reasoning.<br>In his gloss on <i>Hilchot Nedarim</i>, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> resolves that issue, explaining that we find that there is a <i>mishnah</i> in the tractate of <i>Kiddushin</i> (stated without mentioning the name of the author) that follows Rabbi Shimon's view, and a <i>mishnah</i> in the tractate of <i>Nedarim</i> that follows the opposing view. One of the principles of Talmudic law is that a <i>mishnah</i> is taught without mentioning its author to show that it is accepted by the majority of the Sages. Accordingly, one may presume that since the Rambam saw that the redactor of the <i>Mishnah</i> chose to follow Rabbi Shimon's reasoning in one instance and to differ with it in another, the Rambam followed suit.</i> [their viability stemming only from] the possibility that one of the dates would be considered to be worth a <i>p'rutah</i> in another place.
[Different rules apply if] he told her: "Become consecrated to me with this one, with this one and with this one." If together, they are all worth a <i>p'rutah</i>, she is consecrated. If not, the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is doubtful.<br>[Different rules apply if] she eats [the dates] one after another as he gives them to her: If the last date is worth a <i>p'rutah</i>, she is consecrated. If not, the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is doubtful. For the dates that she ate are considered to be a loan, and when [a man] consecrates [a woman] with a loan, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid. Thus, the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> [depends] solely on [the worth of] the final date.
If he tells her: "Behold, you are consecrated with these," the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding if all the dates together are worth a <i>p'rutah</i>. [This applies] even when she eats [the dates] one after another as he gives them to her. She is consecrated, for she is eating her own property.
[The following rules apply when a man] tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this cup." If it is filled with water, the consecration [depends on the combined value of] the cup itself and its contents. If it is filled with wine, the consecration [depends on the value of] the cup itself, but not its contents. If it is filled with oil, the consecration [depends on the value of] the contents, but not of the cup itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Since the water is of little value, it is considered to have no independent importance. Hence, its value is considered together with that of the cup. The wine is not of negligible value, but - in the Talmudic era - it was worth less than the cup containing it. Hence, the wine is given independent importance and is not considered together with the cup. The oil - in the Talmudic era - was considered to be very valuable, more valuable than the cup containing it. Moreover, oil is not necessarily all used at one time. Therefore, it is apparent that the cup is subservient to the oil, and it is the value of the oil that is the determining factor.</i><br>Therefore, if the oil was not worth a <i>p'rutah</i>, the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is doubtful. If the oil is worth a <i>p'rutah</i>, she is definitely consecrated; no attention is paid to [the value of] the cup.

Chapter 6

[The following rules apply when a man] consecrates [a woman] based on a conditional agreement: If the condition is met, the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding. If not, they are of no consequence. This applies regardless of whether the condition was stipulated by the man or by the woman.<br>Every [valid] conditional agreement whatsoever - whether with regard to <i>kiddushin</i>, divorce, commercial transactions or other questions of business law - must conform to the following four rules.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 14 and notes.</i>
These are the four rules governing all conditional agreements:<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">We find a conditional agreement in the Torah: Moses' granting the lands of Transjordan to the tribes of Reuven and Gad (Numbers 32:29-30). All these four rules were evident in Moses' phrasing of the stipulation. Accordingly, our Sages (<i>Kiddushin</i> 61a) consider this a prototype for all future conditional agreements.</i><br>a) the stipulation must be twofold [with both a positive and negative statement];<br>b) the positive aspect must be stated before the negative aspect;<br>c) the stipulation should be mentioned before the completion of the deed that one desires to make conditional;<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Rambam's interpretation of the requirement that in its Hebrew original states: שיהיה התנאי קודם למעשה. The Ra'avad (in his gloss on Halachah 4) interprets the phrase differently. He states that in the wording of the person making the stipulation, the stipulation must be stated before the result of its completion: e.g., "If you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>, you will be consecrated..., and if you do not give me that sum, you will not be consecrated." The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 38:2 accepts the Ra'avad's interpretation and not that of the Rambam.</i><br>d) the stipulation must be something that is possible to comply with.<br>If one of these rules was not kept when a conditional agreement was made, the stipulation is nullified; it is as if there is no condition at all. Thus, [the woman] is either consecrated or divorced immediately, and the commercial agreement is completed as if no condition had ever been made, for one of the four rules of conditional agreements was not met.
What is implied? [When a man] tells a woman: "If you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>, you are consecrated to me with this <i>dinar</i>. And if you do not give me [that sum], you are not consecrated," and after making this stipulation gives her the <i>dinar</i>, the condition is valid, and the <i>kiddushin</i> are subject to its terms. If she gives him 200 <i>zuz</i>, she is consecrated. If she does not give him, she is not consecrated.
If, however, [the man] told [the woman]: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this <i>dinar</i>," gave her the <i>dinar</i> in her hand and then made a stipulation, saying: "If you give me 200 <i>zuz</i> you are consecrated," and if you do not give me [that sum] you are not consecrated," the stipulation is of no consequence, because he performed the deed first by giving it to her, and then making the stipulation.<br>[The above applies] even if everything occurred within a brief span of time;<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">We have chosen a very loose translation. The Hebrew <i>toch kedei dibbur</i> has a precise connotation, meaning the amount of time it takes to say the words <i>Shalom alecha rabbi umori</i>.</i> she is consecrated immediately and does not have to give [her husband] anything at all.
Similarly, when [a man] tells [a woman]: "If you give me 200 <i>zuz</i> you are consecrated to me with this <i>dinar</i>," and then places the <i>dinar</i> in her hand, the stipulation is of no consequence, because the condition was not stated in a twofold manner. He did not tell her: "If you do not give me, you will not be consecrated." [Therefore] she is consecrated immediately without having to give him anything.
Similarly, when [a man] tells [a woman]: "If you do not give me 200 <i>zuz</i>, you will not be consecrated to me. But if you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>, you are consecrated to me with this <i>dinar</i>," and then places the <i>dinar</i> in her hand, the stipulation is of no consequence, because the negative dimension of the stipulation was stated before the positive one. [Therefore,] she is consecrated immediately without having to give him anything.
Similarly, when [a man] tells [a woman]: "If you ascend to the heavens or descend to the depths, you are consecrated to me with this <i>dinar</i>. But if you do not ascend to the heavens or descend to the depths, you are not consecrated." If he places the <i>dinar</i> in her hand afterwards, the stipulation is of no consequence, and the <i>kiddushin</i> are effective immediately. For it is well known that she cannot keep this stipulation; he is merely speaking facetiously in a jesting and teasing manner.
[The following rules apply when a man] makes a condition with regard to a deed that is possible to be performed, but that is forbidden by the Torah - e.g., he told a woman: "If you eat fat or blood, you are consecrated to me with this <i>dinar</i>. But if you do not eat fat or blood, you are not consecrated," or [a man tells his wife]: "If you eat the meat of pigs, this is your <i>get</i>. But if you do not eat it, the <i>get</i> is not effective." If, after making this stipulation, he placed the <i>dinar</i> or the <i>get</i> in her hand, the stipulation is valid. If the woman transgresses and eats [the forbidden article as stipulated], she will be either consecrated or divorced [accordingly]. It is not with regard to such a situation that it is said, "the person made a stipulation that contradicts what is written in the Torah." For the woman has the option not to eat and not to be consecrated or divorced.
With regard [to which situations] did in fact our Sages say:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 19b.</i> "Whenever a person makes a stipulation that contradicts what is written in the Torah, his stipulation is nullified, except with regard to financial matters, in which instances his stipulation is binding"?<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">An exception is made with regard to financial matters, because with regard to these matters the Torah grants the person the right to waive monetary privileges that are due him. Privileges that are not monetary in nature may not be waived.</i><br>When a person consecrates, divorces, gives or sells, dependent on a stipulation through which he wants to acquire a right that the Torah did not grant him, but rather prevented him from obtaining, or to use this stipulation to free himself from an obligation for which the Torah made him liable. In such an instance, he is told, "Your stipulation is of no consequence. The deed you have performed is binding. You are not freed from any responsibility for which the Torah obligates you, nor can you acquire any privilege that the Torah does not grant you."
What is implied? For example, when a man consecrates a woman on condition that he is not obligated to provide her with her provisions or garments, nor grant her conjugal rights, he is told: "With regard to provisions and garments, your stipulation is binding, for these are financial obligations. With regard to conjugal rights, however, your condition is not binding,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Based on the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Bava Metzia,</i> the conclusion of Chapter 7), the Ritba (<i>Kiddushin</i> 19a) and the Mordechai (gloss on <i>Bava Metzia</i> 93a) maintain that even conjugal rights can be considered to be a financial consideration, for it is a matter of physical pleasure. Nevertheless, this opinion is not accepted as halachah. Instead, withholding conjugal relations is considered a matter of physical anguish. Hence a woman does not have the prerogative of waiving this right.</i> for the Torah has obligated you to grant these [to a woman]. Therefore, she is consecrated and you are obligated to grant her conjugal rights. You do not have the potential to free yourself of this responsibility with this stipulation." The same applies in all similar situations.<br>Similarly, if a man consecrates a woman whom he took as a captive for sexual relations on condition that he may have her perform servile tasks,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Deuteronomy 21:11-14 describes the right of a soldier to have relations with a female captive of war whom he desires. Once he has relations with her, he may no longer treat her as a servant.</i> she is consecrated and he is forbidden to have her perform these tasks, for after he had relations with her this was prohibited by the Torah. His stipulation does not empower him to a privilege that the Torah held back from him. The same applies in all similar situations.
If a man established a condition with a woman at the time of <i>kiddushin</i> or divorce requiring her to engage in sexual relations with her father, her brother, her son or the like, it is as if he made a stipulation that she ascend to the heavens or descend to the depths, and his condition is of no consequence. For it is not within the woman's capacity to cause others to transgress and to engage in a forbidden sexual relationship. Thus, he has made a stipulation that she is incapable of fulfilling. The same applies with regard to all similar instances.
If, however, the man made a stipulation that she [influence] so and so to "give me his courtyard or to have his daughter marry my son," the stipulation is binding. For it is in her capacity to fulfill it, she can give so and so a large amount of money so that he will [consent to] give the man [making the condition] his courtyard or have his daughter marry that man's son. For in this instance, there is no sin involved. The same applies with regard to all similar instances.
Have in mind at all times all these guidelines that have been mentioned with regard to conditional agreements. Whenever you hear the expression "A man consecrated [a woman] on the basis of these and these conditions," "gave a divorce on the basis of these and these conditions," or made a sale or gave a present conditionally, you will know that the condition must fit the four rules mentioned. Thus, it will not be necessary to repeat them on every occasion. If one of these rules is not kept, the stipulation is of no consequence.
Some of the later <i>geonim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries have pointed to Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi and Rabbenu Shmuel ben Chofni HaCohen.</i> maintain that a person is required to make a conditional statement twofold only with regard to <i>kiddushin</i> and divorce. With regard to financial matters, by contrast, a twofold statement need not be made.<br>It is not proper to rely on this ruling, for our Sages derived the need to make a twofold statement of the condition, and the other four rules, from the condition made [with] the members [of the tribes] of Gad and Reuven, as [Numbers 38:29-30] states: "If the members [of the tribes] of Gad... cross over. But if they do not cross over...." And this condition involved neither <i>kiddushin</i> nor divorce. [My ruling echoes] the decisions of the great <i>geonim</i> of the previous eras, and it is fitting to follow it.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad, the Ramban and the Rashba differ with the Rambam's reasoning. According to the position of these authorities, it is only one Sage, Rabbi Meir, who maintains that the rules regarding conditional agreements were derived from the agreement made between Moses and the tribes of Reuven and Gad. They maintain that the need to repeat the condition applies only with regard to <i>kiddushin</i>, and was instituted only because of the severity of the establishment and annulment of the marriage relationship. With regard to other matters, however, there is no such requirement. The <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Choshen Mishpat</i> 241:9) follow the Rambam's view.</i>
When a man consecrates a woman conditionally, the <i>kiddushin</i> become effective at the time the stipulation is fulfilled, and not at the time of the [original] <i>kiddushin</i>.<br>What is implied? [For example, a man] tells a woman: "If I give you 200 <i>zuz</i> this year, you are consecrated to me with this <i>dinar</i>. But if I do not give you, you are not consecrated." If he [made these statements and] gave her the <i>dinar</i> in Nisan, but gave her the 200 <i>zuz</i> that he stipulated only in Elul, it is in Elul that the consecration takes effect. Therefore, if another person consecrates her before the first completes carrying out his stipulation, she is consecrated to the second.<br>Similar laws apply with regard to divorce and monetary law. When the stipulation is fulfilled, the divorce is effective or the sale or gift is completed.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The above applies when the agreement is made verbally. If, however, a conditional sale or a present is recorded in a legal document, it is considered to be effective retroactively from the date stated in the document, although the stipulation is not carried out until much later.<br>Others maintain that the same principle applies with regard to a <i>get</i>, and if a date is included in a conditional bill of divorce, the divorce is retroactively effective from the date of the <i>get</i>, even though the stipulation is carried out much later. As stated in <i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 8:1, the Rambam does not follow this approach. (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 143:2.)</i>
When does the above apply? When a stipulation was made, and [the person making it did not state that the agreement took effect] from this time onward. If, however, [a man] told [a woman]: "Behold, you are consecrated to me from this time onward with this <i>dinar</i> if I give you 200 <i>zuz</i>,"<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In such an instance, the stipulation need not be restated, as mentioned in the following halachah.</i> when at a later date he gives her the 200 <i>zuz</i> she is consecrated. Retroactively, the <i>kiddushin</i> are considered to have taken effect at the time they were given, despite the fact that the stipulation was not fulfilled until after much time had passed. Therefore, if a second person consecrates her before the stipulation has been fulfilled, she is not consecrated to that [second] person. Similar laws apply with regard to divorce and monetary law.
Whenever a person makes a stipulation and states [that it is effective] "from this time onward," it is not necessary for him to make a twofold statement of the stipulation,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since the condition does not have to be restated, there is also no need for the positive statement to precede the negative.</i> nor is it necessary to state the stipulation before performing the deed involved.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the Rambam's interpretation of the Talmud's wording שיהיה התנאי קודם למעשה, as explained in Halachah 2.</i> Even when he performs the deed first, his stipulation is effective. He must, however, make a stipulation that is possible to fulfill. A person who makes a stipulation that is impossible to fulfill is merely speaking facetiously; there is no [intent to make] a [binding] stipulation.<br>When a person appends a stipulation to an agreement using the wording <i>al menat</i> ("on condition that"), the rules that apply when the person states "from this time onward" also apply.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot</i> and many subsequent Ashkenazic authorities do not accept this ruling. The difference of opinion is noted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 144:4).</i> It is not necessary for him to make a twofold statement of the stipulation, nor is it necessary to state the stipulation before performing the deed involved.
What is implied? When [a man] tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me on condition that you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>," "here is your <i>get</i> on condition that you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>," or "this courtyard is given to you as a present on condition that you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>," the stipulation is binding. She is consecrated or divorced, or she acquires the field, but she must give the 200 <i>zuz</i>. If she does not give [the money], she will not be consecrated or divorced, nor will she acquire the field.<br>[The above applies] even when the man did not make a twofold condition, and even though he performed the deed before stating the condition - i.e., he placed the <i>kiddushin</i> or the <i>get</i> in her hand or let her take possession of the courtyard, and then completed [the statement of] his stipulation. [The rationale for these leniencies is that] when the stipulation is fulfilled, she retroactively either acquires the field or is consecrated or divorced from the time the deed was performed, as if a stipulation had never been made at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Among the other rationales offered are that the rules for a conditional agreement are derived from the agreement between Moses and the tribes of Gad and Reuven, and in that instance that condition was phrased using the term "if," rather than "from this time onward" or "on condition that" (Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi). The Ra'avad explains that stating a stipulation using the wording "if" nullifies the act the person performs. For a stipulation to have this power, it must be worded precisely. If, however, the wording "on condition that" or "from now onward" is used, the implication is that the act is not nullified, but is merely dependent on the fulfillment of the condition. Since the stipulation is not that powerful, its wording need not be as precise.</i>

Chapter 7

[The following rules apply when a man] tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me on condition that my father will consent." If his father consents, she is consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">It appears that, according to the Rambam, what is significant is the father's consent (or his objection) the first time he hears of the matter. The Ra'avad and others do not share this view and maintain that the father has the option of consenting (or objecting) at all times. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 38:8) quotes the Rambam's wording.</i> If he does not consent, if he remained silent, or if he died before he heard of the matter, she is not consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">There are two opinions in <i>Kiddushin</i> 63a, the source for this halachah, regarding the meaning of "consent": a) to say "yes," b) not to object. The Rambam takes the first view, while the Ra'avad and other authorities favor the second. Both views are mentioned in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 38:9). Significantly, in his Commentary on the Mishnah, the Rambam mentions the second view.</i><br>[If the man tells her: "Behold, you are consecrated to me] on condition that my father does not object." If he hears and objects, she is not consecrated. If he does not object or he dies, she is consecrated. If the son dies, and the father hears afterwards, we instruct the father to say: "I do not consent," so the <i>kiddushin</i> will not be effective, and the woman will not be obligated to undergo the rites of <i>yibbum</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>kiddushin</i> are not effective, the woman will not be under any obligation to marry the brother of her intended husband. Were the father to indeed consent, she would be under obligation either to marry the deceased's brother, or have the obligation removed through <i>chalitzah</i>.</i>
[The following rules apply when a man] tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this [item] on condition that I possess 200 <i>zuz</i> or land on which it is fit to grow a <i>kor</i> of grain."<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Kiddushin</i> 3:2-3), the Rambam writes that it is necessary to mention both land and money, because it is difficult to hide the ownership of land. Were land to be mentioned in the stipulation, one might think that if it were not known that the person did not own land, we would assume that the <i>kiddushin</i> would be void.</i> If there are witnesses who say that he possesses these entities, the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding. If there are no witnesses, [the <i>kiddushin</i> are not nullified entirely; instead,] their status is doubtful. Perhaps he possesses these entities and says he does not own them in order to cause the woman difficulties.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., his desire is that she marry another man. He will then show how her original <i>kiddushin</i> were valid, causing her to be considered an adulteress and to be forbidden to her second husband.</i>
[The following rules apply when he tells her:] "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this [item] on condition that I possess 200 <i>zuz</i> or land on which it is fit to grow a <i>kor</i> of grain in a particular place." If he possesses these entities in that place, the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding. If he does not possess these entities in the place he specified, [the <i>kiddushin</i> are not nullified entirely; instead,] their status is doubtful. Perhaps he possesses these entities in that place [and says he does not own them] in order to cause the woman difficulties.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe HaCohen and others object to the Rambam's ruling, explaining that in such an instance, it is highly unlikely for a man to possess a field in a particular place without people's knowing about it. Hence, if there are no witnesses, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid at all; there is no doubt about the matter. The Radbaz (Volume III, Responsum 39) justifies the Rambam's decision, explaining that it is possible that the person temporarily gave the land as a present, or had a deed written in the name of another person to conceal the matter. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 38:20) quotes the Rambam's decision.</i>
[The following rules apply when he tells her:] "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this [item] on condition that I show you 200 <i>zuz</i> or land on which it is fit to grow a <i>kor</i> of grain." When he shows her these entities, she is consecrated. If he shows her money that is possessed by someone else or land on which it is fit to grow a <i>kor</i> of grain in a field belonging to someone else, she is not consecrated; he [must] show her what belongs to him.<br>If he borrowed the money, rented a field or took it on a sharecropping arrangement and showed it to her, she is not consecrated; he [must] show her what belongs to him. For when he says "I will show you," that implies that "I will show you the entity I mentioned that belongs to me and is in my possession."
[The following rules apply when] the man owns land on which it is fit to grow a <i>kor</i> of grain, but it contains clefts ten handbreadths deep or rocks ten handbreadths high. If the clefts are filled with water, they are considered to be rocks and are not included in the total measure, because they are not fit to be sown.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 38:22) differentiate between a cleft filled with water that is not fit to use for irrigation, and a cistern of water that is. The latter is included in the measure of the field, even when it is filled with water, because it enhances the value of the field.</i> If they are not filled with water, they are included in the total measure, because they are fit to be sown.
[The following rules apply when a man] tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this [item] on condition that you are not bound by vows." The <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding if she is bound by any of the following three vows: that she may not eat meat, that she may not drink wine, or that she may not wear colored ornaments.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The same law applies regarding a vow not to wear any other jewelry, clothing or cosmetics that women will frequently wear to adorn themselves. (See Chapter 25, Halachah 1.)<br><i>Ketubot</i> 72b describes these vows as involving ענוי נפש, "the oppression of the soul" (cf. Numbers 30:14 . Simply put, a woman who must live under such restrictions will not be happy, and it will therefore not be pleasant for her husband to live with her.<br>In the <i>Beit Yosef</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 39:1), Rav Yosef Karo mentions that the vows that nullify a relationship have a larger scope than those involving ענוי נפש; it also includes those בינו לבינה, affecting the relationship between the husband and wife (cf. Numbers 30:17 . (For a more detailed explanation of these types of vows, see <i>Hilchot Nedarim</i>, Chapter 12, and <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Yoreh De'ah</i>, Chapter 234.)</i> If she is bound by any vow other than these, she is consecrated, even when [the husband] states: "I object even with regard to these."<br>If he told her, "[Behold, you are consecrated...] on condition that you are not bound by <b>any</b> vow," even if she has made a vow [as insignificant as] not to eat carobs, she is not consecrated.
[The following rules apply when a man tells a woman:] "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this [item] on condition that you do not have any physical blemishes." If she has one of the physical blemishes that cause a woman to be deemed unfit [as a wife], she is not consecrated. If she has a physical blemish other than these, she is consecrated, even though he states, "I object even with regard to these."<br>What are the physical blemishes that cause a woman to be deemed unfit [as a wife]: All the physical blemishes that cause a priest to be deemed unfit [for service in the Temple] cause a woman to be deemed unfit. In <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i>, all the blemishes affecting the priests are explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus, Chapter 21, states that a priest who possesses certain physical blemishes may not serve in the Temple. In <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i>, Chapters 6-8, these blemishes are listed.</i> In addition, [there are other blemishes that cause] women [to be deemed unfit]. They include: foul body odor, [excessive] sweating, foul breath, deep voice, breasts of abnormal size, being more than a handbreadth larger than those of other women,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Ketubot</i> 7:5).</i> a distance of more than a handbreadth between one breast and the other, a scar in the place where she was bit by a dog, and a birthmark on her forehead.<br>This includes even a birthmark that is very small, even if it is close to her hairline, and even if there are no hairs growing from it. This is the birthmark that is mentioned as a disqualifying factor for a woman and not for a priest. If, however, a birthmark has facial hair growing from it, or if it is as large as an <i>isar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">An Italian coin equivalent in weight to four barley corns, with a diameter of 2.7 cm (Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Kiddushin</i> 1:1).</i> even when no hair grows from it, it is a disqualifying blemish, both for priests and for women.
When a man consecrates a woman without making any specific stipulations, and it is discovered that she has one of the physical blemishes that cause a woman to be deemed unfit, or [it is discovered that] she is bound by one of the three vows mentioned above, the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">She cannot marry another man until she receives a divorce, nor may she consummate this marriage unless the husband consecrates her again, stating that he has no objections to her condition.<br>This ruling is given because we are unsure whether these vows or physical blemishes are disturbing enough to cause a person who did not express concern about the matter to consider himself as having been deceived about the nature of his marriage partner.</i><br>If [a man] consecrates [a woman] on condition that she is not bound by vows, and she was bound by vows, but [afterwards,] she went to a wise man who nullified them for her,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">A wise man has the authority to release people from vows they have taken if they regret having taken them. (See <i>Hilchot Nedarim</i>, Chapter 4.)<br>The <i>kiddushin</i> are binding only when the wise man nullifies the vows before the woman's intended husband discovers their existence. Once he discovers that she is bound by vows, the <i>kiddushin</i> are nullified even when she has the vows nullified afterwards (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 39:2).</i> she is consecrated.
If [a man] consecrates [a woman] on condition that she does not have physical blemishes, and she does have blemishes, she is not consecrated, even if [afterwards,] she goes to a physician who heals these blemishes.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The wording used by the man is significant. If he states: "Behold, you are consecrated on condition that you will not have blemishes," the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding if a physician is able to heal her (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 39:7).</i><br>When, by contrast, a man enters into a marriage contract on condition that he is not bound by any vows, and that he does not have any physical blemishes, although he is indeed bound by vows and has physical blemishes, if he goes to a wise man who nullifies the vows, and if he goes to a physician who heals the blemishes, the marriage is valid. [The rationale is that] there is no shame for a man to have had physical blemishes once they have been healed. A woman will not object because of such a thing.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam appears to be sharing the interpretation of <i>Tosafot, Ketubot</i> 74b, that the reason the <i>kiddushin</i> are nullified if a woman has blemishes that a physician heals is that even after she is healed, the husband will still be repelled by the fact that at one time she possessed physical blemishes.<br>Rashi, by contrast, explains the difference between a wise man's nullification and a physician's healing as follows: The wise man nullifies the vow at its source, causing it to be considered as never having been taken. Thus, retroactively it is as if the woman had not been bound by a vow at the time of the <i>kiddushin</i>. A physician, by contrast, can heal a blemish only within the existence of a continuum of time. Thus, at the time of the <i>kiddushin</i>, the woman had physical blemishes. Therefore, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding.</i>
[The following rules apply when a man tells a woman:] "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this [item] on condition that I give you 200 <i>zuz</i> within 30 days." If he gives her [the money] within 30 days, she is consecrated. If 30 days pass without him giving it to her, she is not consecrated.<br>[If a man tells a woman,] "Behold, you are consecrated to me with these <i>zuz</i> after 30 days," she is consecrated after 30 days, even though she used the money within the 30 days. If either he or she change their minds [and decide to nullify the marriage] within these 30 days, she is not consecrated.
If another man comes and consecrates her within these 30 days, she is consecrated to the second man forever. [The rationale is] that at the time the second man consecrated her, she was not consecrated. Therefore, the second man's <i>kiddushin</i> are binding and make her a married woman. Thus, after the 30 days pass and the first man's <i>kiddushin</i> are fit to take effect, she is already a married woman. It is thus as if the first man consecrated a married woman, in which case the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's wording, it would appear that if her second husband died or divorced her within the thirty days, the first man's <i>kiddushin</i> are binding. The Rashba (in his gloss on <i>Kiddushin</i> 59b) does not accept this premise and states that the woman's acceptance of the second <i>kiddushin</i> clearly shows a change in her mind with regard to the first <i>kiddushin</i>. For this reason, they are nullified and can never be binding again (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). (See Ramah and <i>Tur</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 40:2.)</i>
[The following rules apply when a man] tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this <i>dinar</i> from this time onward, and after 30 days," and another person consecrates her within the 30 days. [There is doubt regarding the matter,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi, <i>Kiddushin</i> 59b, explains that the doubt is whether his statement is a conditional statement, and thus, after 30 days pass the original <i>kiddushin</i> will retroactively take effect, thus nullifying the <i>kiddushin</i> given her by the second man. Or perhaps by saying "after 30 days," the first man withdrew his initial statement, and his intent was that his <i>kiddushin</i> would not be effective until after 30 days. If this were so, the second man's <i>kiddushin</i> would be binding.<br>Significantly, if a person made a similar statement with regard to a sale, the Rambam rules (<i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 2:9) that this is a conditional statement. Thus, it appears that his ruling here is a stringency, accepted because of the severity of the laws of marriage and divorce.</i> and] both [men] are considered as having established <i>kiddushin</i> that may possibly be binding. Therefore, both are required to divorce her.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 40) states that this is necessary only when the woman wants to marry a third person. If she wants to marry either of the individuals who consecrated her, she may do so, provided the other divorces her. Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> does not quote this ruling, many later authorities do.</i> The divorce may be given within the [original] 30 days<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Although the <i>kiddushin</i> given by the first man do not take effect fully until after 30 days, it is possible for him to divorce her before that date. For when the <i>kiddushin</i> take effect, she will be consecrated retroactively from the time of the original <i>kiddushin</i>, and then these <i>kiddushin</i> will be nullified by the divorce.</i> or afterwards.<br>[Should one man tell a woman,] "Behold, you are consecrated to me from this time onward, and after 30 days"; and another man comes and tells her, "Behold, you are consecrated to me from this time onward, and after 20 days," and another man comes and tells her, "Behold, you are consecrated to me from this time onward, and after 10 days," [there is doubt regarding the matter, and] all [the men] are considered as having established <i>kiddushin</i> [that may possibly] be binding, and every one must divorce her. [Indeed, these rules apply] even when a hundred men consecrate her in this manner.
When [a man] tells a woman, "Behold, you are consecrated to me [and these <i>kiddushin</i> apply to everyone] with the exception of so and so" - i.e., that she should not be forbidden to have relations with him - with regard to everyone else she should be considered a married woman, but with regard to him she should be considered to be single - there is doubt regarding the status of the <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The doubt centers on whether it is possible to establish a bond of <i>kiddushin</i> that is incomplete. This is an unresolved issue. The latter clause states that if a person desires to establish a bond of <i>kiddushin</i>, but with a proviso, this is definitely unacceptable.
