As mentioned by the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 38:68, there are authorities who maintain that the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding at all.</i><br>If, however, he tells her, "Behold, you are consecrated to me on condition that you are permitted to so and so," she is consecrated, and she is forbidden to that person as she is forbidden to all others. [The rationale is that] he has made a condition that is impossible to fulfill.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">For the very nature of the marriage bond forbids relations with another man.</i>
When [a man] gives two <i>p'rutot</i> to a woman and tells her: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with one today, and with the other after I divorce you," she is consecrated. When he divorces her, she becomes consecrated to him again<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 40:7), Rav Yosef Karo rules that the status of these <i>kiddushin</i> is doubtful: the woman cannot marry another person until she is divorced, but she must be consecrated again before the marriage can be consummated.</i> until he divorces her a second time, because of the <i>kiddushin</i> established by the second <i>p'rutah</i>.<br>If, however, [a man] tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this [item] after I convert," "...after you convert," "...after I become freed [from servitude],"<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a Canaanite servant, who cannot marry a Jewish woman. Similarly, a male Jew cannot marry a female Canaanite servant.</i> "...after you become freed [from servitude]," "...after your husband dies," or "...after your sister dies,"<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the man proposing is married to the woman's sister. While his wife (her sister) is alive, he may not marry the woman. Afterwards, he may.</i> she is not consecrated. [The rationale is] that he cannot consecrate her now.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 62a explains that at the time the <i>kiddushin</i> were given, the possibility of marriage is "something that has not come into the world," for it is impossible for them to take effect. Therefore, even when the situation changes afterwards, they are not effective retroactively.</i>
When [a man] tells a <i>yevamah</i>: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this [item] after your <i>yavam</i> performs <i>chalitzah</i> for you,"<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when a woman's husband dies childless, she is obligated to marry his brother (referred to as a <i>yavam</i>) through the rite of <i>yibbum</i>, or be freed of her obligation to him through the rite of <i>chalitzah</i>. The Rambam is describing a situation in which another man gives her <i>kiddushin</i> with the expectation that <i>chalitzah</i> will be performed.</i> she is consecrated. [The rationale is] that even if he consecrated her at present, the <i>kiddushin</i> would be [at least] of a doubtful status.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 4, Halachah 14. Since the <i>kiddushin</i> a person gave her now would have some effect, <i>kiddushin</i> given with a conditional statement are binding totally.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 40:6) rules that even when a conditional statement is made, the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt. There are some manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> that indicate that the Rambam also shared that view.</i>
When a man tells a friend, "If your wife gives birth to a girl, [the girl] is consecrated to me with this [item]," his statements are of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For the object of the <i>kiddushin</i> does not yet exist.</i> If the friend's wife is pregnant, and the existence of a fetus has been recognized, [the girl] is consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad, the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> interpret the Rambam as stating that the <i>kiddushin</i> given for the fetus are definitely binding. In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Kiddushin</i> 3:5), however, the Rambam explicitly states that this is a Rabbinic stringency, enforced because of the severity of the laws of marriage.</i> [Nevertheless,] it appears to me that [the man] must consecrate [his bride] again via her father after she is born, so that she will enter a marriage bond about which there are no questions.
When [a man] tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with 100 <i>dinarim</i>," and gives her at least one <i>dinar</i>, she is consecrated, provided he gives her the entire sum. It is as if he told her, "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this <i>dinar</i> on condition that I give you 100 <i>dinarim</i>." [In such an instance,] the <i>kiddushin</i> take effect from [the time he gave her the first <i>dinar</i>].<br>When does the above apply? When he told her "with 100 <i>dinarim</i>" without specifying [any particular <i>dinarim</i>]. If, however, he is more explicit and tells her, "Behold, you are consecrated to me with these 100 <i>dinarim</i>," and begins counting them out into her hand, she is not consecrated until he gives her [all 100].<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Since he began counting them out for her, she is under the impression that she will receive the entire sum, and will not accept less (<i>Kiddushin</i> 8a).</i> Either of them may retract their consent until the very last <i>dinar</i> is given.<br>Similarly, if one of the <i>dinarim</i> was found lacking the standard weight, or one was a <i>dinar</i> of brass, she is not consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For she accepted the <i>kiddushin</i> under the impression that all 100 <i>dinarim</i> were of full value. Nor can he give her a different <i>dinar</i>, because he specified that the <i>kiddushin</i> would be with the coins he was giving her. Even if neither the man nor the woman retracts, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). (See Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 29:7.)</i> [The following rules apply when] one of the <i>dinarim</i> was inferior: If it would be accepted with difficulty, [the <i>kiddushin</i> are valid, provided]<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to this ruling, explaining that even though the man is obligated to exchange the <i>dinar</i>, the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding whether or not he does so. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 29:7) quotes the Ra'avad's ruling.</i> he exchanges it. If it would not [be accepted], the <i>kiddushin</i> are of no consequence.
[The following rules apply when a man] tells [a woman]: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with these clothes that are worth 50 <i>dinarim</i>." When they are silk or of similar fabrics that a woman would desire,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording appears to indicate that the reason no evaluation is necessary is that women usually desire silk, and because of this desire waive the need for evaluation. Implied is that other items that are not that desirable must be evaluated before the <i>kiddushin</i> are binding. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 31:1) does not follow this approach. (See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 31:1.)<br><i>Tosafot, Kiddushin</i> 7b, offer a different rationale for the mention of silk: Most people can make at least a rough evaluation of the value of silk. When, however, an object cannot be evaluated easily - e.g., a precious stone - a woman is not consecrated, because she is unsure of the value of the gem until she receives an expert's appraisal. This is one of the sources for the custom of consecrating a woman with a wedding ring that does not contain a stone.</i> and they are worth 50 [<i>dinarim</i>], the woman is consecrated from the time she took them onward. There is no need that they be evaluated in the market, and only afterwards, when the woman is assured [of their value], will she be consecrated. Instead, since they are worth the amount he states, she is consecrated from the time of the initial [exchange]. If they are not worth [that amount], she is not consecrated.
A man and a woman were discussing the subject of their consecration, he saying: "I will consecrate you with 100 <i>dinarim</i>," and she saying: "I will not be consecrated for less than 200 [<i>dinarim</i>]." [Since they did not agree,] they both went home.<br>[The following rules apply when] afterwards, [either the man or the woman requested the other [to reconsider], and the man consecrated her without specifying a sum. If the man made the request of the woman, the sum [originally] quoted by the woman is accepted. If the woman made the request of the man, the sum [originally] quoted by the man is accepted.
When a man appoints an agent to consecrate a woman, and the agent consecrates her on the basis of a conditional agreement, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">For by entering into a conditional agreement when he was not instructed to do so by the principal, the agent deviated from the instructions he was given. As such he is acting on his own initiative, and not as the agent of the principal.</i> Similarly, if [the principal] instructed the agent to consecrate the woman on the basis of a conditional agreement, and he consecrated her without making any stipulation whatsoever, or made another stipulation or changed the stipulation stated by the principal, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid.
When [the principal] tells the agent: "Consecrate her in this and this place," and the agent consecrated her in another place, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Here also, the reason is that the agent deviated from the instructions he was given.</i> [If the principal tells the agent:] "Consecrate her for me. She is in this and this place," and the agent goes and consecrates her in another place, she is consecrated; he is merely suggesting to him the place [where she might be found].<br>Similarly, if [the woman] tells her agent, "Receive <i>kiddushin</i> for me in this and this place," and the agent received them for her in another place, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not valid. [If she told her agent: "Receive <i>kiddushin</i> for me. My prospective] husband is in this and this place," and [the agent] receives the <i>kiddushin</i> in another place, she is consecrated; she is merely suggesting to him the place [where he might be found].
When [a man] consecrates a woman, but he or she desires to retract immediately - even if the retraction is made within a very short amount of time<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Here the intent is a specific measure of time, the amount of time it takes to say: <i>Shalom alecha, rabbi umori</i>.</i> - the retraction is of no consequence and the woman is consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nedarim</i> 87a states that with the exception of idol worship, marriage and divorce, a retraction made within the abovementioned span of time is reckoned with. Why are these three instances different? In general, a person is not precise with regard to what he says and may make statements, relying on the possibility of retracting them later. In these three instances, however, the severity of the matter is obvious, and a person would not make such statements unless he made them with full presence of mind (Rabbenu Nissim). (See also the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Temurah</i> 5:3, which mentions several other instances in which a person's retraction is of no consequence.)</i>
When [a man] consecrates [a woman] and attaches a condition [to the <i>kiddushin</i>], and after several days changes his mind and nullifies the condition, the condition is of no consequence and it is as if the woman had been consecrated without any condition ever having been made. [This law applies] even when he nullifies the condition in the presence of his intended bride alone, without this being observed by witnesses. Similarly, if the woman was the one who attached a condition to the <i>kiddushin</i>, and afterwards nullified it in the presence of her prospective husband alone, the condition is of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling, maintaining that the nullification of the condition must also be made in the presence of witnesses. (He does, however, accept the Rambam's decision that if a man brings the woman to the <i>chuppah</i>, without a condition, in the presence of witnesses, the condition is considered to be nullified. For his act is considered equivalent to nullifying the condition.)<br>The Rashba accepts the Rambam's ruling with regard to conditions involving money - e.g., "Behold, you are consecrated on condition that you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>." For a person may waive a debt owed him, and consider it as received. With regard to other conditions - e.g., "Behold, you are consecrated on condition that you are not bound by vows" - he does not accept the Rambam's position. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 38:35) quotes the Rambam's ruling.</i><br>Therefore, if [a man] consecrated [a woman] and attached a condition [to the <i>kiddushin</i>], and afterwards, brought her [to the <i>chuppah</i>] without mentioning the condition, or engaged in sexual relations with her without mentioning the condition, she must receive a divorce [before she marries another man]<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> originally given is doubtful. If the couple want to continue living together, they must establish <i>kiddushin</i> that are unquestionably binding. And if a second man consecrates her, she must receive a divorce from both men before marrying a third (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 38:35).</i> even though the condition was never fulfilled. [The rationale is that] perhaps [the man] nullified the condition when he brought her [to the <i>chuppah</i>] or when he engaged in sexual relations with her.<br>Similarly, when [a man] consecrates a woman with [an article] worth less than a <i>p'rutah</i> or with a loan, and then engages in sexual relations [with this woman] in the presence of witnesses, without making a statement of intent, the woman must receive a divorce [before she marries another man]. [The rationale is that] perhaps [the man intended to consecrate her through these relations] and relied on them, rather than on the <i>kiddushin</i> that are inadequate.<br>[The principle on which these rulings depend is:] It is an accepted presumption that no virtuous Jewish man will enter into sexual relations that are wanton when he has the potential to engage in these relations in a way that is a mitzvah.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In one of his responsa, the Rambam states that this principle cannot be extended without limit. When a man and a woman engage in sexual relations with a promiscuous intent, we do not say that he intends to consecrate her with these relations. The principle stated above is applied only when there is reason to presume that the man desired to establish a marriage relationship. (See also <i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 10:19.)</i>

Chapter 8

When [a man] tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me with this cup of wine," and the cup is discovered to contain honey [she is not consecrated]. [Similarly, if he tells her: "...Behold, you are consecrated to me with this cup] of honey," and the cup is discovered to contain wine;<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Even though honey is more valuable than wine, the <i>kiddushin</i> are not binding, because the stipulation was not fulfilled, and it is possible that the woman indeed desired wine rather than honey. Similarly, with regard to the sets that follow, <i>Kiddushin</i> (48b) explains reasons why it is possible to say that the woman favored either of the alternatives, and she is therefore not consecrated unless the stipulation that is made is met.</i> "...with this <i>dinar</i> of silver," and it is discovered to be gold; "...[with this <i>dinar</i>] of gold," and it is discovered to be silver; "...on condition that I am a priest," and he was discovered to be a Levite; "[on condition that I am] a Levite," and he was discovered to be a priest; "...[on condition that I am] a Givonite,"<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Who, like bastards, are forbidden to marry into the Jewish people. (See the notes on Chapter 1, Halachah 7.)</i> and he was discovered to be a bastard; "...[on condition that I am] a bastard," and he was discovered to be a Givonite; "...[on condition that I am] an inhabitant of a town," and he was discovered to be an inhabitant of a metropolis; "...[on condition that I am] an inhabitant of a metropolis," and he was discovered to be an inhabitant of a town; "...on condition that I am poor," and he was discovered to be rich; "...[on condition that I am] rich," and he was discovered to be poor; "...on condition that my house is close to the bathhouse," and it is discovered to be distant from it; "...[on condition that my house is] distant from the bathhouse," and it is discovered to be close to it; "...on condition that I have a maid," "...a daughter who knows how to braid hair," or "...who bakes," and [it is discovered that] he does not have one; "...on condition that he does not have [one of the above,] and [it is discovered that] he does; "...on condition that he has a wife and children," and [it is discovered that] he does not; "...on condition that he does not have [the above,] and [it is discovered that] he does - in all these and in any similar instance, the woman is not consecrated. The same rule applies if she [makes a condition based on] false information.
In all the above instances, she is not consecrated even though she says: "In my heart, I was willing to be consecrated to him even though he deceived me and gave me wrong information." Similarly, [if she gave him false information,] she is not consecrated even though he says: "In my heart, I was willing to consecrate her even though she deceived me." [The rationale is that] feelings in one's heart are not [the same as explicit] statements.
[When a man tells a woman:] "Behold, you are consecrated to me on condition that I am a perfumer," and it is discovered that he is both a perfumer and a leather craftsman;<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Animal feces were used in the processing of leather, and thus leather craftsmen were known for their unpleasant odor.</i> "...on condition that I am an inhabitant of a town," and he was discovered to be an inhabitant of both a town and a metropolis;<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he maintains two homes.</i> or "...on condition that my name is Yosef," and it was discovered that his name was Yosef and Shimon; she is consecrated.<br>If, however, he told her: "[Behold, you are consecrated to me] on condition that my name is only Yosef," and it was discovered that his name was Yosef and Shimon; "...on condition that I am solely a perfumer," and it is discovered that he is both a perfumer and a leather craftsman; or "...on condition that I am solely an inhabitant of a town," and he was discovered to be an inhabitant of both a town and a metropolis; she is not consecrated.
When [a man] tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me on condition that I know how to read," [for the stipulation to be fulfilled] it is necessary that he know how to read from the Torah and translate what he reads according to the translation of Onkelos the convert.<br>If he tells her: "...on condition that I am a reader," he must know how to read the Torah, the works of the Prophets and the Holy Writings with proper grammatical precision. [If he tells her:] "...on condition that I know how to study the Mishnah," he must know how to read the Mishnah. "...On condition that I am a sage of the Mishnah," he must know how to read the Mishnah, the <i>Sifra</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A compendium of halachic exegesis of the Book of Leviticus, composed by Rav, a student of Rabbi Yehudah HaNasi, who compiled the Mishnah. Rav was the leader of the first generation of Amoraim in Babylonia.</i> the <i>Sifre</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">A compendium of the halachic exegesis of the Books of Numbers and Deuteronomy, composed by Rav.</i> and the <i>Tosefta</i> of Rabbi Chiyya.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A collection of teachings intended to "explain the Mishnah and expound upon concepts that would require much effort to be derived from the Mishnah." Rabbi Chiyya was one of Rabbi Yehudah HaNasi's primary disciples.</i>
[When a man tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me] on condition that I am a student [of the Torah]," we do not say that [he must be a student] of the caliber of ben Azzai and ben Zoma.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Students of Rabbi Akiva, renowned for their scholarship in their youth. Their promising futures were effaced after they entered a mystical experience together with their master. (See <i>Chaggigah</i> 14b.)</i> Instead, it is sufficient that when one asks him a question regarding his studies, he is able to answer. [This includes] even the laws of the festivals that are studied in public; these are easy matters that are studied close to the festival, so that people at large will be familiar with them.<br>[When a man tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me] on condition that I am a wise man," we do not say that [he must be] like Rabbi Akiva and his colleagues. Instead, it is sufficient that when one asks him a point of logic with regard to any subject, he is able to answer. "...On condition that I am mighty," we do not say that [he must be] like Avner ben Ner<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The commander of King Saul's armies.</i> or Yoav.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Yoav ben Tz'ruyah, the commander of King David's armies.</i> Rather, it is sufficient that his colleagues fear him because of his might. "...On condition that I am rich," we do not say that [he must be as wealthy] as Rabbi Eleazar ben Azariah.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Who was renowned for his wealth. Each year he would give 12,000 calves as the tithes of his herd (<i>Shabbat</i> 54b). Another version of the text also mentions Elazar ben Chersom whose wealth is described in <i>Yoma</i> 35b.</i> Rather, it is sufficient that the inhabitants of his city honor him because of his wealth.<br>[When a man tells a woman: "Behold, you are consecrated to me] on condition that I am righteous," even if the person is known to be thoroughly wicked, there is doubt [regarding the status of the <i>kiddushin</i>, and] the woman is considered as consecrated. For it is possible that he had thoughts of repentance in his heart at that time.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The doubt exists only because we cannot be aware of what is happening within the person's heart or mind. If we could be sure that he had repented within his heart, he is considered a righteous man, regardless of his previous conduct. This demonstrates the power of <i>teshuvah</i>, how one thought of repentance can transform one's spiritual level from one extreme to the other.</i><br>"...On condition that I am wicked," even if the person is known to be thoroughly righteous, there is doubt [regarding the status of the <i>kiddushin</i>, and] the woman is considered as consecrated, since it is possible that he had thoughts of idol worship in his heart at that time. For the sin of idol worship is so great that even when a person thinks of serving [idols] in his heart,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In general, a person is not punished for a sin unless he commits a deed. The worship of false gods is different. (See <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 2:6, which states: "Whoever accepts a false god as true, even when he does not actually worship it, disgraces and blasphemes [God's] glorious and awesome name." See also <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 2:1,3.)</i> he is considered wicked, as [implied by Deuteronomy 11:16, which] states: "lest your hearts be tempted [and you go astray and serve other gods]," and [Ezekiel 14:5, which] states: "that I may detect the House of Israel in their hearts [for they are all estranged from Me because of their idols]."
[When a man] consecrates a woman and says, "I thought she was from a priestly family, and instead she is from a family of Levites," "...from a family of Levites, and instead she is from a priestly family," "...poor, and instead she is rich," or "...rich, and instead she is poor," she is consecrated, for she did not cause him to err.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since he did not explicitly state the matter as a stipulation, his misconception does not cause the <i>kiddushin</i> to be nullified.</i><br>Similarly, if she says, "I thought he was a priest, and instead he is a Levite," "...a Levite, and instead he is a priest," "...poor, and instead he is rich," or "...rich, and instead he is poor," she is consecrated, for he did not cause her to err.

Chapter 9

[When a man] consecrates two women whom he is forbidden to marry at the same time, because it creates a prohibited relationship, neither is consecrated. What is implied? When a man consecrates a woman and her daughter or two sisters at the same time, neither of them is consecrated.
[The following rule applies when a man] consecrates many women at the same time and says: "Behold, all of you are consecrated to me." If among [these women] were two sisters, a woman and her daughter or the like, none of the women is consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since all the women were included in the same statement without differentiating between any of them, none is consecrated.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> notes that <i>Kiddushin</i> 51a equates this law with the following ruling: When one person tells another, "You and this animal will acquire this object," the person acquires half. The correspondence is explained as follows. Although the animal is not fit to acquire the property, the person still acquires the half. Similarly, although some of the women are not fit to be consecrated, the others are, and they should be consecrated.<br>On this basis, the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> asks: Since in <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 22:12, the Rambam rules that the person acquires the property although the animal does not, seemingly he should agree that the women who are fit to be married should be consecrated. In resolution, he explains that monetary laws are governed by different principles from marital laws, and therefore there is no contradiction between the two rulings.<br>Because of this contradiction, although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 41:4) mentions the Rambam's opinion, it also mentions a view that states that the women who are not related are consecrated. It concludes that because of the conflicting opinions, the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is doubtful. The same applies with regard to the law mentioned in the second portion of this halachah.</i><br>If [the man] told [the women]: "Those of you who are fit to engage in marital relations with me are consecrated to me," they are all consecrated to him, except the sisters, the mother and her daughter or the like.<br>Similarly, if [a man] told [a group of women], "Behold, all of you are consecrated to me," and among [these women] was a Canaanite maidservant, a non-Jewish woman or a woman who is forbidden to this man as an <i>ervah</i> - e.g., a married woman, his daughter, his sister or the like - none of them is consecrated to him. If he says: "Those of you who are fit to engage in marital relations with me are consecrated to me," they are all consecrated to him, except the women with whom he cannot establish <i>kiddushin</i>.
[The following rule applies when] a man tells two sisters: "Behold, one of you is consecrated to me with this [article]," and gives them both a <i>p'rutah</i>, or one accepts it on behalf [of herself and] her sister. They both require a divorce from him, and it is forbidden for him to engage in marital relations with either of them, for the <i>kiddushin</i> are viable even though he is forbidden to engage in relations with either of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since the man was not specific as to which of the sisters he was consecrating, there is no way to determine which of them was intended. [Even if the man specifies his intent afterwards, his word is not accepted (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 37:16).] Therefore, there is doubt as to the status of both of the women.</i> The same [rule applies] when [a man] tells a father: "One of your daughters is consecrated to me," and the father accepts the <i>kiddushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 37:16) states that this rule applies even when the man consecrating the daughters was engaged to one of them. Since he did not specify his intent at the time of the <i>kiddushin</i>, all the daughters require a divorce.</i>
[The following rule applies when a man] appoints an agent to consecrate a particular woman, the agent went and consecrated her, the principal himself consecrated the woman's mother, daughter or sister, and it is not known which of them was consecrated first: They both require a divorce, and they both are forbidden [to have relations] with him.<br>A similar [rule applies] if a woman appointed an agent to consecrate her, he [fulfilled her charge], she herself consecrated herself to another man, and it is not known which consecration took place first. Both men are required to divorce her. If they so desire, one may divorce her and one may marry her.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In the previous instance, the man was not able to marry either of the women he consecrated, because they were related and it is forbidden to marry one's divorcee's mother, sister or daughter.</i>
When does the above apply? When [the two men who consecrated her] were not related. If, however, they were related, the agent consecrated the woman to a father, and she consecrated herself to his son, to his brother or the like: they both must divorce her, and they both are forbidden [to have relations] with her.
[The following rules apply when a man] tells an agent: "Go out and consecrate a woman for me," the agent dies, and it is not known whether or not he consecrated a woman on behalf of the principal. We accept the presumption that he consecrated [on his behalf], for it is an accepted presumption that the agent will carry out the mission with which he was charged.<br>[Accordingly,] since it is not known which woman he consecrated, the principal is forbidden to marry any woman who has a relative who might be forbidden because of the laws of <i>ervah</i> - i.e., a woman who has an [unmarried] daughter, mother, sister or the like.<br>[The rationale is] that if you say: "Let him marry this one," perhaps the agent had consecrated the woman's mother, sister or daughter. He is permitted [to marry] a woman who does not have relatives like these.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 35:11) quotes the opinion of Rabbenu Nissim, who maintains that if the relatives state that they were never consecrated and the man marries the woman, they are permitted to remain married.</i><br>If [the woman the man desires to marry] has a relative like this - e.g., a sister - and that relative was married at the time the agent was appointed, [the man] is permitted to marry her. [This applies] even if this relative was divorced before the agent died. We do not say that perhaps the agent consecrated her relative after she was divorced. For she was not fit to [marry him] at the time the agent was appointed, and a person does not appoint an agent to consecrate a wife for him if [the intended] is not fit to be consecrated at the time the agent is appointed.
[The following rules apply when] a person has five sons, they each appoint their father as an agent to consecrate a wife for them, and the father tells a colleague who has five daughters: "[Each] one of your daughters is consecrated to one of my sons." Should the father [of the girls] accept the <i>kiddushin</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If the girls are below the age of <i>bagrut</i>, their father has the privilege of consecrating them. If they are above the age of <i>bagrut</i>, it is possible that they appointed him as an agent to receive their <i>kiddushin</i>. Note, however, the following halachah.</i> each of the girls must be divorced by each of the five brothers. For they all gave their father the prerogative of consecrating a wife for them [and he did not specify which woman would be the wife for which of his sons].<br>If one of [the sons] dies, each of the women must be divorced by the four [remaining brothers] and must perform the rite of <i>chalitzah</i> with one of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For perhaps the brother who died was her betrothed.</i>
[There is, by contrast, no doubt in the following situation:] A father had [two daughters]: one a minor or a <i>na'arah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">A girl between the ages of twelve and twelve and a half, who has manifested signs of physical maturity.</i> whom he has the privilege [of consecrating], and one a <i>bogeret</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A girl past the age of twelve and a half, who has manifested signs of physical maturity.<br>In the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Kiddushin</i> 3:8), he states that if the man has several daughters below the age of <i>bagrut</i>, they all require a divorce, because of the doubt mentioned in the previous halachot, but the daughter above the age of <i>bagrut</i> does not require a divorce.</i> Even if the <i>bogeret</i> gives her father the privilege of consecrating her, when he consecrates one of his daughters without specifying which one, it is assumed that the <i>bogeret</i> is not the one intended unless he specifically states [that the <i>kiddushin</i> are for] "my older daughter, who is a <i>bogeret</i>, who appointed me as [her] agent."<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 51b explains the rationale for this ruling. A person will not abandon a mitzvah for which he is responsible (the consecration of his younger daughter) to fulfill a mitzvah for which he is not responsible (the consecration of his elder daughter).</i> Therefore, [in such a situation,] the <i>bogeret</i> is not consecrated,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 37:15) states that this law applies only when the daughter who is above <i>bagrut</i> does not specify the identity of a man she desires to marry. If, however, she makes such a specification, and the above situation occurs with regard to this individual, she also requires a divorce.</i> and her sister is consecrated.
[The following rules apply with regard to a father] who has two pairs of daughters from two different wives, and he has the prerogative [of consecrating all of them].<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., none of them has reached the age of <i>bagrut</i> (<i>Beit Yosef, Even HaEzer</i> 37). (See <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 37:42.)</i> If he consecrated one daughter, and at the time of the <i>kiddushin</i> told the husband: "I consecrated my oldest daughter" [there is no confusion with regard to his intent]. Although it is possible to say that perhaps he consecrated the older daughter in the older pair to him, or the older daughter in the younger pair or the younger daughter in the older pair - for she is older than the older daughter in the younger pair [- we do not entertain such doubts.] All [the daughters] are permitted [to marry other men] except the older daughter in the older pair; she alone is considered to be consecrated.<br>Similarly, if [the father says that] he consecrated his youngest daughter: Although it is possible to say that perhaps [he consecrated] the younger daughter in the younger pair to him, or the younger daughter in the older pair or the older daughter in the younger pair - for she is younger than the younger daughter in the older pair [- we do not entertain such doubts]. All [the daughters] are permitted [to marry other men] except the younger daughter in the younger pair; she alone is considered to be consecrated. For the phrase "my oldest daughter" implies the daughter whom none is elder than, and the phrase "my youngest daughter" implies the daughter whom none is younger than.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Although the Hebrew words גדולה and קטנה can mean both "older" and "oldest" and "younger" and "youngest" respectively, in this context the man's intent is clear, and the intent is the oldest and the youngest.</i>
A father's word is accepted with regard to [the status of] his daughter below the age of <i>bagrut</i>. [If] he states that she has been consecrated, she is forbidden to marry at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 64a derives this concept from Deuteronomy 22:16, "I gave my daughter to this man," the verse that teaches that the father has the prerogative of consecrating his daughter until she becomes a <i>bogeret</i>. From "I gave my daughter," we learn that because of her father's words, the woman is forbidden to marry anyone but her intended. From "this man," we learn that he can clarify the identity of the intended.</i>
When a father says, "I consecrated my daughter, but I do not know to whom I consecrated her," she is forbidden [to marry] any man forever unless the father says, "I became aware of the fact that I consecrated her to so and so." He alone must divorce her [before she can marry another person]. [Her father's word is accepted with regard to the identity of the person who consecrated her] even if he becomes aware after she reaches the age of <i>bagrut</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that since the prohibition comes on the basis of her father's statements, the license to marry is also granted on that basis.</i>
If a father says, "I don't know to whom I consecrated [my daughter]," and a person comes and says, "I am the one who consecrated her," his word is accepted. [Moreover, he is granted the prerogative of] consummating the marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., not only may he free the girl of the prohibition by divorcing her, he may consummate the marriage if he desires. We do not suspect that he is making this statement merely because he is attracted to the woman (<i>Kiddushin</i> 63b). The man is given this prerogative because we assume that he would not lie, lest the father protest and deny his claim.</i> He need not consecrate her a second time.
[In the above situation,] if two people come and both claim that they were the ones who consecrated her, they are both required to divorce her. If they desire, one may divorce her, and one may consummate the marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">He must, however, consecrate the woman again (Rashba, Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 37:22).</i><br>[If the latter option was taken, and] one consummated the marriage,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The Rashba states that this ruling applies even if the marriage was not yet consummated. As long as the license for the marriage was granted, the third person's claims do not cause it to be rescinded. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 37:23) quotes both views, but appears to favor that of the Rambam.</i> and afterwards a third person came and claimed that he was the one who had consecrated her [originally], his word is not accepted and he does not cause her to be forbidden to her husband.
[The following rules apply when] a woman states: "I was consecrated, but I do not know to whom I was consecrated," and a man comes and claims: "I was the one who consecrated her." His word is accepted and he may divorce her [so that] she is permitted to marry others, but not him. He is forbidden to consummate the marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, he consummates the marriage and the couple live together as man and wife, we do not force them to separate because of the suspicions mentioned (Rashba, <i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 37:24).</i><br>[This restriction was instituted out of suspicion that] perhaps the man's natural inclination overcame him [and he made his statement out of desire for her]. [And we fear that the woman] will encourage [his false statements] so that she will be permitted [to marry].
[The following rules apply when a man] tells a woman: "I consecrated you," and the woman denies the matter. He is forbidden [to marry] her close relatives,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the relatives he would be forbidden to marry if she were his wife. Since according to his statements they are man and wife, he must uphold any prohibitions that such a relationship would bring about.</i> but she is permitted [to marry] his close relatives.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">For she denies the matter.</i> If she says, "You consecrated me," and he denies the matter, he is permitted [to marry] her close relatives, but she is forbidden [to marry] his close relatives.<br>If he says: "I consecrated you," and the woman says: "It was my daughter, not me, whom you consecrated," he is forbidden [to marry] the close relatives of the mother; the mother is permitted [to marry] his close relatives; he is permitted [to marry] the close relatives of the daughter;<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., those who are not forbidden to him because of his statements regarding her mother. Since he does not acknowledge the consecration of the daughter, he is not bound by the mother's statements.</i> and the daughter is permitted [to marry] the man's close relatives.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">For the mother's statements are not binding for the daughter. Although Scriptural law gives a father the prerogative to make binding statements regarding his daughter's status, a mother's statements do not have this power even according to Rabbinic law (Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Kiddushin</i> 3:11).</i><br>[The following rules apply when the man says:] "I consecrated your daughter," and the woman says: "It was myself [not my daughter] whom you consecrated." He is forbidden [to marry] the daughter's close relatives; the daughter is permitted [to marry] his close relatives; he is permitted [to marry] the mother's close relatives; and the mother is forbidden [to marry] the man's close relatives.
All the claims of <i>kiddushin</i> [mentioned in the previous halachah] refer to a situation in which the person making the claim states that the <i>kiddushin</i> were given in the presence of witnesses, and the witnesses either journeyed overseas or died. If, however, they acknowledge that the <i>kiddushin</i> were given without witnesses observing, the <i>kiddushin</i> are of no consequence, as we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 4, Halachah 6.</i><br>Whenever a woman tells a man, "You consecrated me," and he denies the matter, we ask him to compose a bill of divorce so that she will be permitted to marry others, for [doing this] does not involve any loss to him.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Once the man divorces the woman, he is forbidden to marry her close relatives. Nevertheless, this is not considered a significant loss, since there are many other women he could marry. And yet, because of this factor, he cannot be compelled to divorce the woman (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>; Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 48:6). Others [Rabbenu Eliyahu Mizrachi and the Maharshal (Responsum 25)] differ and maintain that since he divorced the woman only as a favor to her, he is not forbidden to marry her relatives.</i> If he gives her a divorce on his own volition,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., without being asked to by others.</i> we compel him to give her [the monetary settlement, as stated in] the <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> notes that there are several points that require clarification with regard to the Rambam's statements. As stated in Chapter 10, Halachah 11, a man is not ordinarily obligated to pay a marriage contract until the second stage of the marriage (<i>nisu'in</i>). If merely <i>kiddushin</i> were given, he is not under such an obligation. It is only when he wrote the woman a <i>ketubah</i> and gave it together with the <i>kiddushin</i>, that he is obligated.<br>This, however, raises a question: If he wrote the woman a <i>ketubah</i>, how can he deny the <i>kiddushin</i>? The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that this law applies only in a place where it is customary to write the <i>ketubah</i> before the <i>kiddushin</i> are given. Because in most instances there is no obligation to give a <i>ketubah</i> after <i>kiddushin</i>, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> does not mention this law at all (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 48:2).</i>
When a man appoints an agent to consecrate a woman for him, and the agent goes and consecrates her for himself, the woman is consecrated to the agent. It is, however, forbidden to do such a thing. Whoever does this or performs a similar act with regard to business matters is considered to be wicked.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 7:10.</i>
[The following rules apply when a man] appoints an agent to consecrate a woman for him, the agent consecrates her [but a doubt arises whether the agent consecrated her for himself or for the principal]. When the agent says, "I consecrated [the woman] for myself," and the woman says, "I was consecrated to the principal," [the ruling depends on whether or not the appointment of the agent was made in the presence of witnesses].<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad maintains that the man must always appoint an agent in the presence of witnesses. He therefore interprets the phrase <i>huchzak hashaliach</i> to mean that the appointment of the agent was public knowledge.</i><br>If the agent was not appointed in the presence of witnesses, the agent is forbidden to marry the woman's close relatives, and she is permitted to marry [the agent's] close relatives.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">As in Halachah 15.</i> The woman is forbidden to marry the principal's close relatives, but the principal is permitted to marry her close relatives.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For his agent states that he did not consecrate the woman on behalf of the principal.</i> If the agent was appointed in the presence of witnesses, she is consecrated to the principal.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that even though the woman is considered to be consecrated to the principal, the agent is still forbidden to marry the woman's relatives. The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 35:15) quotes this ruling.<br>Rabbenu Asher does not accept the Rambam's ruling, stating that if the agent explicitly states that he did not consecrate the woman on behalf of the principal, there is no way that the <i>kiddushin</i> can be binding.</i>
[The following rules apply when] the woman says, "I do not know to whom I was consecrated, whether to the agent or to the principal." If the agent was not appointed in the presence of witnesses, she is consecrated to the agent. If he was appointed as his agent [in the presence of witnesses], they both are required to divorce her. If they desire, one may divorce her and one may consummate the marriage.
[The following rule applies when] a woman appoints an agent to consecrate her, he went and fulfilled his mission, but while he was in the process of doing so, she nullified his agency and rescinded his appointment, and it is not known whether she nullified his agency before he received the <i>kiddushin</i> or afterwards. The status of the <i>kiddushin</i> is doubtful. [She cannot marry another man without receiving a divorce, nor may the marriage be consummated unless she receives <i>kiddushin</i> again.] Similar rules apply when a man appoints an agent and retracts his appointment.
[The following rules apply when a man] consecrates one of five women but does not know which of them he has consecrated, and each of them says, "He consecrated me." He is forbidden to marry the [close] relatives of all these women and must divorce each of them. [With regard to the payment of the money due because of the marriage contract,] he should leave [the sum due because of] one marriage contract among all the women and depart.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the doubt, he is not required to pay each of them the sum required by the marriage contract.</i><br>If, however, [the man] had consecrated [his intended] through sexual relations, our Sages penalized him<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Because he violated our Sages' instructions not to consecrate with sexual relations.</i> [and required him] to give [the sum due because of] the marriage contract to each of the women.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 49:2 questions why this ruling differs from the ruling delivered (<i>Hilchot Gezelot</i> 4:9) when a man says that he stole from one of five people, and does not know from whom he stole. In such an instance, although the thief is required to reimburse each of the five, this is only when each of the recipients takes an oath that the money was stolen from him. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> leaves the question unresolved.</i><br>[When could such a situation apply?]<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">For, as stated in Chapter 10, Halachah 11, a man is not ordinarily required to pay the money due because of a marriage contract until <i>nisu'in</i>, the second stage of the marriage relationship. If the woman has merely been consecrated, this obligation is not incumbent upon him unless he wrote a marriage contract for her.</i> When it is known that he wrote a marriage contract for one of the women,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad states that there is no need for the marriage contract to have been written. If the man made a verbal agreement to that effect when consecrating the woman, he is obligated. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam would also accept the Ra'avad's law, and was merely giving one of several possible settings in which the law stated could be applied.</i> and the marriage contract was lost, and each of the women claims: "I was the one who was consecrated. He wrote the marriage contract for me and it was lost."
When a report is circulated that a woman has been consecrated to a particular man, we operate under the presumption that [the woman] is consecrated although there is no binding evidence to that effect.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">As the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 46:1) states, the woman is bound by the same rules as all those whose status of their <i>kiddushin</i> is in doubt. She may not marry any one other than the man to whom the report says she is consecrated, but before she may consummate the marriage with him, she must be consecrated again.</i> Whenever a report is not substantiated by a court, no attention is paid to it.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is possible for many rumors to be spread. When is credence given to a rumor? When it can be substantiated in court with testimony, as mentioned below. (See Rashi, <i>Gittin</i> 99b.)</i><br>What type of report when substantiated will cause a woman to be considered to be consecrated? Two [men] came [to court] and testified that they saw candles lit, couches spread, people coming in and out of the house, and women celebrating with her, saying "So and so was consecrated today."<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In this part of the halachah (in contrast to its beginning), the Rambam does not mention that the witnesses state to whom the woman was consecrated. The later authorities (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 46:1; <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 46:1) maintain that this information must also be stated; otherwise the woman is not considered to be consecrated.</i> If the women are heard saying: "So and so will be consecrated today," no attention is paid [to the report]; perhaps they assembled for the purpose of <i>kiddushin</i>, but the <i>kiddushin</i> were not given. It is only when [the report says that the woman] was [actually] consecrated [that the court considers her as such].<br>Similarly, if two [men] come and say, "We saw what looked like an <i>erusin</i> celebration and we heard sounds [of joy], and we heard from so and so<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., according to the Rambam, it is sufficient for two witnesses to hear from one person who originally heard the report from another person. The Rashba and Rabbenu Asher maintain that unless a report is spread by at least two individuals, it cannot be substantiated in court. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 46:2) quotes the Rambam's view and the Ramah quotes the other opinions.</i> who heard from so and so that this woman was consecrated in the presence of [two witnesses] and the witnesses went to another country or died" - this is a report that could cause a woman to be considered consecrated.
When does the above apply? When there is no rationale that offsets the report. If, however, there is a rationale that offsets the report, and that rationale is heard when [the report that] she was consecrated is heard, [the woman] is not considered to be consecrated.<br>What is [an example] of a rationale that offsets a report? "So and so was consecrated with a stipulation attached," or "[So and so was given] <i>kiddushin</i> whose status is in doubt."<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., there is a question whether the <i>kiddushin</i> were worth a <i>p'rutah</i>, or the youth consecrating her had reached the age of majority (<i>Gittin</i> 9:9; <i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 46:3).</i> [In such instances,] the woman is not considered [consecrated]. Instead, we ask her [for an account of the circumstances] and rely on her word, since there is no clear evidence nor firm report.
[The following rules apply when at first] a report spreads that [a woman] was consecrated to a particular man, and after a few days a rationale that offsets the report is stated. If it appears to the court that the rationale is true, they rely on it, and [the woman] is not considered to be consecrated. If not, since the rationale was not heard at the time the report of the <i>kiddushin</i> was heard, we do not take it into consideration.
An incident once occurred involving a report that a particular woman was consecrated to the son of so and so. After time passed, they asked [the husband's] father, who said, "There was a stipulation attached when she was consecrated to him, and the stipulation was not fulfilled." The Sages did not rely on his words. Instead, they ruled that the status of the <i>kiddushin</i> was in doubt, as if there were no rationale that offsets [the original report].
[The following rules apply when] a report is spread that [a woman] was consecrated to a particular man, and a second man came and consecrated her in our presence. We attempt to verify the report of the <i>kiddushin</i> of the first man. If witnesses come and give clear testimony that [the woman] was consecrated to the first man, the <i>kiddushin</i> given by the second are of no consequence.<br>If not, the first man, for whom there is merely a report of his <i>kiddushin</i>, must divorce the woman, and the second man, who definitely consecrated her, is allowed to consummate the marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">If the first man does not divorce her, the second is not allowed to consummate the marriage. If he does not heed this ruling and marries her before she receives a divorce, the Rabbis ruled that the woman is forbidden to him, and he is required to divorce her (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 46:5).</i> If the second man divorces her, the first should not consummate the marriage, lest people at large say, "He remarried the woman he divorced after consecrating her, after she had been consecrated by another man."<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This is forbidden, as explained in <i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 11:12. If the first man does not heed this ruling and marries her, the Rabbis are unresolved whether or not the marriage is allowed to stand (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 46:13).</i>
When a report is spread that a woman was consecrated to one man, and a second report is later spread that she was consecrated to another, one of the men should write her a bill of divorce, and the other - either the first or the last - may consummate the marriage.
In a place where it is customary for [a prospective groom] to send gifts to his [prospective] bride after consecrating her, and witnesses who had seen presents being brought to [a woman] come [and testify to that effect], we suspect that she has been consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., although the presents are worth more than a <i>p'rutah</i>, they themselves do not establish a bond of <i>kiddushin</i>, because they are not given with that intent. Nevertheless, the fact that the presents were given indicates that there is a possibility that <i>kiddushin</i> had been given previously.</i> [Because of this] suspicion, she must be divorced. [This ruling applies] even when the majority of the men in the city send presents before consecrating [their prospective brides].<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are a number of men who send presents after consecrating, we suspect that perhaps this person also followed that practice. Although rulings of Torah law are ordinarily determined by the practice of the majority, an exception is made in this case, because of the severity of the laws of marriage and divorce (<i>Tosafot, Kiddushin</i> 50b), or because this majority practice is not fixed and may change at the whim of the people (Rabbenu Nissim).</i><br>In a place where it is customary for all the men [of the locale] to send presents first and then consecrate, [the fact that witnesses] saw presents [being sent] is not a cause for suspicion.
[The following rules apply when] it was established that a marriage contract had been composed [for a specific woman]: If it is common for some of the people in that place to consecrate and then have [a marriage contract] composed, we suspect [that the woman was consecrated].<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the marriage contract, like the gifts mentioned in the previous halachah, are a sign that perhaps the woman was consecrated (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> [This law applies] even when there is no scribe in the locale. We do not say that because a scribe happened to be found [in the locale], [the man had the marriage contract] written before [he consecrated the woman].<br>If all the men in a locale have marriage contracts composed before consecrating [their wives], [the existence of a marriage contract] is not a cause for suspicion [that a woman has been consecrated].
[The following rule applies when there is a dispute between two pairs of witnesses:] two [witnesses] say: "We saw [a woman] consecrated on this particular day," and two [witnesses] say: "We did not see [this happen]." Although they are all neighbors, living in the same courtyard, [the woman] is considered to be consecrated; the claim "We did not see [this happen]" is of no consequence, for it is common for [a man] to consecrate [a woman] in private.
When one witness says, "This [woman] has been consecrated," and [the woman] herself says, "I have not been consecrated," she is permitted [to marry without restriction].<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, the testimony of one witness is not accepted when the woman denies his statements. If her words were not true, she would not dare to contradict the witness's testimony (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i><br>When one [witness] says, "[This woman] has been consecrated," and another [witness] says, "she has not been consecrated," she should not marry<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's ruling appears to be based on the rationale that since the woman has a witness who supports her, we suspect that she might contradict the other witness even when she is not telling the truth (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).<br>This rationale is not accepted by the Ra'avad and other authorities, who object to the Rambam's ruling. They maintain that since the woman's statements are accepted when she has no support, surely they should be accepted when they are supported by another witness. They explain that <i>Ketubot</i> 23a, the source for this halachah, is referring to an instance when a man threw <i>kiddushin</i> to a woman, one witness claims that the <i>kiddushin</i> were closer to the woman (and hence, she was consecrated), the other claims that they were closer to the man (and thus she was not consecrated), and the woman herself does not know. This is the view accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch (Even HaEzer</i> 47:3).</i> [anyone other than the person to whom the witness says she has been consecrated]. If she, nevertheless, marries another person, [there is no necessity to] terminate [the marriage], for she says, "I was not consecrated."<br>[The following rules apply when a woman herself] says, "I have been consecrated," and afterwards she accepts <i>kiddushin</i> [a second time]. If she can offer a rationale that explains her previous statements, explaining why she said she was consecrated, and the reason appears substantial [to the court],<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketubot</i> 22a gives an example of a rationale that the Sages accepted. A very attractive woman at first said that she was consecrated, and afterwards accepted <i>kiddushin</i>. When asked to account for her behavior, she explained that her suitors were originally not worthy people, and she therefore wanted nothing to do with them. When a worthy suitor came, she was happy to accept his offer.</i> she is permitted to [marry] the second man.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Note the ruling of the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 47:4), who states that this law applies only when the woman says "I was consecrated," but does not state to whom. If she mentions the name of a person who she claims to have consecrated her, her retraction is not accepted even when the rationale she offers appears to have substance.</i> If she cannot offer an explanation, or she offers one but it does not appear substantial, she is forbidden [to marry]. [Nevertheless, we also give certain consideration to] the <i>kiddushin</i> given by the second man, and [require] him to divorce her. She is forbidden to [marry] him or anyone else until the person who first consecrated her comes.<br>Similar [rules apply with regard to] a woman who comes [to a new community] and says that she is a married woman, and afterwards says that she is unmarried. If she gives a rationale that explains her statements, and it [appears] substantial, her word is accepted.

Chapter 10

According to Rabbinic law, a woman who has been consecrated (i.e., an <i>arusah</i>) is forbidden to engage in sexual relations<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, the two are forbidden to remain in privacy together. For the prohibition against <i>yichud</i>, being alone with a woman other than one's wife, applies until the marriage is consummated (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 55:1).</i> with her husband as long as she is living in her father's home.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This is alluded to by the wording of the blessing recited before consecrating a woman (Chapter 3, Halachah 24), which praises God "who has forbidden the <i>arusot</i> to us, and permitted to us those who are married by [the rites of] <i>chuppah</i> and <i>kiddushin</i>" (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> A man who has relations with his <i>arusah</i> in his father-in-law's home is punished with "stripes for rebelliousness."<br>Even when [the husband] consecrated [his <i>arusah</i>] by having sexual relations with her, he is forbidden to engage in sexual relations with her again until he brings her to his home, enters into privacy with her, and thus singles her out as his [wife].<br>[Their entry into] privacy is referred to as entry into the <i>chuppah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Popularly, the term <i>chuppah</i> is understood to refer to the wedding canopy. There are also sources for this definition (see <i>Sotah</i> 49b and Rashi's commentary; for other definitions, see the notes of the Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 55:1). Nevertheless, the common practice is to follow the Rambam's view as well. For this reason, after the ceremony under the wedding canopy, the bride and groom go to a private room, the <i>cheder yichud</i>. This constitutes the halachic definition of <i>chuppah</i>.</i> and it is universally referred to as <i>nisu'in</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned previously, in Jewish law, marriage is a two-stage process involving <i>erusin</i> and <i>nisu'in. Erusin</i> (also referred to as <i>kiddushin</i>) is the stage described in the previous chapters, that causes a woman to be designated as a man's wife and causes her to be forbidden to other men. It is not until <i>nisu'in,</i> however, that the couple begins living together as man and wife. At present, <i>nisu'in</i> follows directly after <i>erusin</i>; under the wedding canopy the groom consecrates the bride, and afterwards they go to a private room.</i><br>When a man has relations with his <i>arusah</i> for the sake of [establishing] <i>nisu'in</i> after he has consecrated her, the relationship is established at the beginning of sexual relations. This causes her to be considered his wife with regard to all matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., all the privileges and obligations of the <i>ketubah</i> (marriage contract) apply. He alone nullifies her vows, and if he is a priest, should his wife die, he must become impure when burying her.</i>
Once an <i>arusah</i> has entered the <i>chuppah</i>, her husband is allowed to have relations with her at any time he desires, and she is considered to be his wife with regard to all matters. Once she enters the <i>chuppah</i>, she is called a <i>nesu'ah</i>, although [the couple] has not engaged in sexual relations.<br>[The above applies when] it is fitting to engage in relations with the woman. If, however, the woman is in the <i>niddah</i> state [when relations are forbidden], the marriage bond is not completed and she is still considered to be an <i>arusah</i> although she entered the <i>chuppah</i> and remained in privacy [with her husband].<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling is not accepted by Rabbenu Asher and other authorities. They maintain that a <i>chuppah</i> conducted with a <i>niddah</i> is binding, despite the fact that the couple are forbidden to engage in sexual relations. (See <i>Tur</i> and <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> 61:1.) At present, every effort is made to schedule a wedding so that the woman will not be in the <i>niddah</i> state at that time. If, however, that is not possible, the wedding is held and is considered binding, despite the woman's condition.</i>
The marriage blessings must be recited in the groom's home<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This applied when the wedding celebrations were held in the groom's home. The intent is that the blessings be recited before the complete establishment of the marriage bond. Therefore, at present, these blessings are recited under the marriage canopy, before the couple goes to their private room.</i> before the marriage takes place. There are six blessings; they are:<br>Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who has created all things for His glory.<br>Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, Creator of man.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Ketubot</i> 7b) explains that this blessing is in praise of the creation of Adam, the first man.<br>In <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 10:11, where the text of the wedding blessings is mentioned, this blessing precedes the blessing "who has created all things for His glory." The order mentioned in these halachot is the sequence in which these blessings are recited today. It appears more appropriate, particularly according to Rashi's commentary (<i>ibid.</i>), which explains that the blessing "who has created all things..." is not directly connected to the wedding itself, but rather is recited in appreciation of the guests who have come to celebrate together with the new couple.</i><br>Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Despite the fact that this blessing follows two (or three) blessings that begin with "Blessed..." it also begins with "Blessed...." Among the explanations offered is that the first blessings are short, and if the phrase "Blessed..." were not mentioned, they would appear to be a single blessing (<i>Tosafot, Ketubot, ibid.</i>).</i> who created man in His image, in an image reflecting His likeness; [He brought forth] his form and prepared for him from His own Self a structure that will last for all time.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>ibid.</i>) interprets this as a reference to the creation of woman, who was created from man ("his own self"), and gives him the potential for reproduction ("a structure that will last for all time").</i> Blessed are You, God, Creator of man.<br>May the barren one rejoice and exult as her children are gathered to her with joy. Blessed are You, God, who makes Zion rejoice in her children.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">"The barren one" refers to Jerusalem. Psalms 137:6 states: "Let my tongue cleave to my palate if I do not place Jerusalem above my highest joy." Thus, at the height of the wedding celebration, we recall the holy city and pray that it be rebuilt.</i><br>Grant joy to these loving companions, as You granted joy to Your creation in the Garden of Eden long ago. Blessed are You, God, who grants joy to the groom and the bride.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is a prayer that the bride and groom enjoy the happiness experienced by Adam and Eve before the first sin.</i><br>Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the universe, who created joy and happiness, bride and groom, gladness, song, cheer and delight, love and harmony, peace and friendship. Soon, God, our Lord, may there be heard in the cities of Judah and the outskirts of Jerusalem, a voice of joy and a voice of happiness, a voice of a groom and a voice of a bride, a voice of grooms rejoicing from their wedding canopies and youths from their songfests.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Jeremiah 33:11. This blessing joins our wishes for the happiness of the particular couple with our hope for the Messianic redemption and the rebuilding of Jerusalem. The ultimate marriage relationship is the bond between God and the Jewish people, which will be realized in the Messianic age. Thus, the two themes, marriage and redemption, share an intrinsic link.</i> Blessed are You, God, who grants joy to the groom together with the bride.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi, <i>Ketubot</i> 8a, explains the difference between the last two blessings. The fifth of the blessings concludes with a request that the bride and groom enjoy a lifetime of happiness and success together. The sixth and final blessing concludes with a request that they find happiness in each other, that their wedding joy be extended throughout their lives. Alternatively, the final blessing is a blessing for the Jewish people as a whole, who find fulfillment in married life.</i><br>
If wine is available, a cup of wine should be brought, and the blessing over wine recited first. Afterwards, all the above blessings should be recited over the cup of wine; thus, one recites seven blessings.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">These seven blessings are also recited after grace at the festive meals held during the seven days of celebration after a couple's marriage. (See <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 2:9-11.)</i><br>In certain places, it is customary to bring a myrtle [branch] together with the wine. The blessing over the myrtle is recited after [the blessing over] the wine, and then the six blessings [mentioned above] are recited.
The wedding blessings are recited only in the presence of a quorum of ten adult free men.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketubot</i> 7b derives this from Ruth 4:2, "And he took ten men from the elders of the city," which is interpreted to refer to the marriage between Boaz and Ruth.<br>In the Guide for the Perplexed, Vol. III, Chapter 49, the Rambam explains that our Sages required ten men to be present to publicize all weddings, so that a man will live together with a woman only after their marriage has become public knowledge. Their intent was to make it socially unacceptable for couples to live together without marriage.</i> The groom is counted as part of the quorum.
When a man consecrates a woman, recites the wedding blessings, but does not enter into privacy with her in his home, she is still considered to be [merely] an <i>arusah</i>. For <i>nisu'in</i> are not established by the recitation of the wedding blessings, but rather by [the couple's] entry into the <i>chuppah</i>.<br>When [a man] consecrates [a woman] and [the two] enter a <i>chuppah</i>, but do not have the wedding blessings recited, the woman is considered to be married with regard to all matters. The wedding blessings may be recited even after several days have passed.<br>A woman in the <i>niddah</i> state should not marry until she is purified. The marriage blessings are not recited for her until she is purified.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the notes on Halachah 2, although all efforts are made not to schedule a marriage when the woman is in the <i>niddah</i> state, if this is unavoidable the wedding may be held and the blessings recited. Nevertheless, the consummation of the marriage is possible only when the woman is purified.</i> If a person transgresses, marries [a woman in this state] and has the blessings recited, they should not be recited again afterwards.
[A man] must write a marriage contract (a <i>ketubah</i>) [for his wife] before their entry into the <i>chuppah</i>; only afterwards is he permitted to live with his wife. The groom pays the scribe's fee.<br>How much does [the marriage contract require him to promise to have paid to her in the event of his death or his divorcing her]? If the bride is a virgin, no less than 200 <i>dinarim</i>. If she is not a virgin, no less than 100 <i>dinarim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Ovadiah of Bertinoro (<i>Pe'ah</i> 8:8) states that this is the sum of money required for a person to support himself for one year.</i> This amount is called the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i>.<br>If the groom desires to add to this amount he may, [promising any sum,] even a talent of gold. The laws pertaining to this addition and to the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i> are the same with regard to most matters. Therefore, every time the term <i>ketubah</i> is used without any additional explanation, it should be understood to include the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i> together with the additional amount [promised by the groom].<br>It was our Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">This point is a matter of debate, for there are certain opinions (among them that of Rabbenu Tam) that maintain that the obligation to pay the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i> stems from the Torah. Support for the latter opinion may be drawn from the wording commonly used in most Ashkenazic <i>ketubot</i> "200 silver <i>zuz</i> that are rightfully yours [as required by] the Torah." [Significantly, even the text of the <i>ketubah</i> in the standard printed texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> (<i>Hilchot Yibbum VaChalitzah</i> 4:34) includes this phrase. Nevertheless, many authorities maintain that this is a printer's addition and not the Rambam's own words.]<br>Most authorities (including Rabbenu Asher) agree that the obligation to pay the fundamental requirement of the <i>Ketubah</i> is Rabbinic in origin. Nevertheless, the abovementioned phrase is traditionally included in the <i>ketubah</i> to teach us the value of the silver to which we are referring, as is explained in the notes on the following halachah.</i> who ordained the requirement of [writing] a <i>ketubah</i> for a woman. [They instituted this obligation] so that it would not be a casual matter for [her husband] to divorce her.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when the man understands that divorcing his wife will cost him a significant sum of money, he will think twice before doing so.</i>
[Our Sages] did not require that these <i>dinarim</i> be of pure silver. Instead, [their intent was] the coin [commonly used] in the [Talmudic] period, which was seven parts copper and one part silver. Thus, a <i>sela</i> (a coin worth four <i>dinarim</i>) contained half a <i>zuz</i> of [pure] silver.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The Ashkenazic authorities (even those who agree with the Rambam with regard to the Rabbinic origin of the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i>) differ with him regarding the value the man is required to pay [<i>Tur,</i> Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 66:6)]. According to these authorities, our Sages ordained that a man pay his virgin bride 200 <i>zuz</i> of pure silver.
