With regard to a bride who is not a virgin, however, they differ and maintain that the obligation is 100 <i>zuz</i> of the Talmudic period. In practice, however, the custom is to give such a bride half the sum given to a virgin (<i>Beit Shmuel</i> 66:14).</i> And the 200 <i>dinarim</i> to be paid a virgin were equivalent to 25 <i>zuz</i> of pure silver, while the 100 <i>zuz</i> to be paid to a woman who had previously engaged in sexual relations was 12 and a half <i>zuz</i> [of pure silver].<br>The weight of each <i>zuz</i> is 96 barley corns, as explained at the beginning of <i>[Hilchot] Eruvin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 1, Halachah 12. According to most authorities, the equivalent of a <i>dinar</i> in contemporary measure is 4.8 grams. According to Rabbi Shneur Zalman of Liadi (<i>Piskei Siddur</i>), it is 5.1 grams.</i> A <i>dinar</i> is universally referred to as a <i>zuz</i>, regardless of whether it was of pure silver or of the coins used in the [Talmudic] period.
[A marriage contract] for a virgin may not be less than 200 [<i>zuz</i>], nor less than 100 [<i>zuz</i>] for a woman who is not a virgin. Whenever anyone [composes a marriage contract for] a lesser sum, the sexual relations [he conducts with his wife] are considered promiscuous.<br>[Marital relations] are permitted whether the husband composes a legal document [recording] the <i>ketubah</i>, or whether he has witnesses observe him making a commitment for either 100 or 200 <i>[zuz]</i> and [reaffirms that] commitment with a contractual act.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a <i>kinyan chalifin</i>, in which the recipient gives the seller a handkerchief and thus reaffirms his commitment. After this act, the transaction is binding. (See <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 5:5.)<br>Once a person reaffirms his commitment by performing a contractual act in the presence of witnesses, the witnesses have the right to draw up a document attesting to the obligation he accepted upon himself. They need not consult him before doing so (<i>Ketubot</i> 55a).</i> Similarly, if [a man] gives his wife possessions equivalent to the value of her <i>ketubah</i> [as security], he is permitted to engage in relations with her until he has the opportunity to [have the document] composed.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Implied by the Rambam's wording is that this is only a temporary measure, and that a <i>ketubah</i> must be composed at the earliest possible opportunity. (See Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 66:2.)</i>
When a man brings a woman [into a <i>chuppah</i>] without writing a <i>ketubah</i> for her, or he has written her a <i>ketubah</i> but it was lost, or the woman waived the <i>ketubah</i> in favor of her husband, or she sold her <i>ketubah</i> to him, he must compose a document [obligating himself] for [at least] the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he does not have to give her the full value of her original <i>ketubah</i>. He must, however, give her a <i>ketubah</i> in which he obligates himself for the minimal amount required by our Sages. Note the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 66:10, who questions whether he must write the <i>ketubah</i> for 100 or 200 <i>zuz</i>.<br>The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> (<i>op. cit.</i>) and the <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 66:14 state that the man is obligated for the minimal amount only in the latter two instances mentioned by the Rambam. When the woman loses her <i>ketubah</i>, her husband must write her a new <i>ketubah</i> for the initial amount.</i> if he desires to continue living with his wife. For it is forbidden for a man to continue living with his wife for even a single moment without [her having] a <i>ketubah</i>.<br>When, however, a woman sells her <i>ketubah</i> to others for the possible benefit,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., she sells the rights to her <i>ketubah</i> to a purchaser for a price below its face value. Should her husband die or divorce her, the purchaser receives the full value of the <i>ketubah</i>. If the woman dies before her husband, the purchaser does not receive anything.</i> [her husband] does not have to write another <i>ketubah</i> for her. For the <i>ketubah</i> was instituted solely so that it would not be a casual matter for [a man] to divorce [his wife]. In this instance, if [the woman's husband] divorces her, he must pay her <i>ketubah</i> to the purchaser in the same way that he would pay her if she had not sold it.
When [a man] consecrates a woman and writes her a <i>ketubah</i>, but does not enter into a <i>chuppah</i> with her, her status is that of an <i>arusah</i> and not that of a <i>nesu'ah</i>. For a <i>ketubah</i> does not bring about <i>nisu'in</i>. If [the husband] dies or divorces her, she may collect the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i> from property possessed by the man or his estate.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., after a marriage has been consummated, a woman may collect her due even from property that has been sold, for all her husband's property is on lien to her <i>ketubah</i>. Before the marriage bond is consummated, however, she does not have this right (<i>Ketubot</i> 43b).<br>Rabbenu Asher and Rabbenu Nissim differ with the Rambam in this regard and maintain that the woman should be able to collect her <i>ketubah</i> from property that has been sold as well. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 55:6) follows the Rambam's view.</i> She does not collect the additional sum [that he attached to the <i>ketubah</i>] at all, for they did not enter [a <i>chuppah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketubot</i> 54b explains that the additional amount was granted the woman in consideration of the couple's sexual relationship.</i><br>If, by contrast, a man consecrates a woman and does not write a <i>ketubah</i> for her, and he dies or divorces her while she is still an <i>arusah</i>, she has no claim against him, not even for the fundamental [requirement of the <i>ketubah</i>]. For our Sages did not grant [a woman] the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i> until the marriage is consummated or until the husband writes a document for her.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In this matter as well, Rabbenu Asher differs with the Rambam and maintains that a woman is entitled to a <i>ketubah</i> from <i>erusin</i> onward. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>ibid.</i>) quotes the Rambam's view. Although the Ramah mentions Rabbenu Asher's opinion, he states that it is customary to follow the Rambam's ruling.</i><br>When a man consecrates his daughter, and [her intended husband] writes her a <i>ketubah</i> and dies or divorces her while she is a <i>na'arah</i>, her father receives [payment for] her <i>ketubah</i>, as explained in Chapter Three<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 11.</i> above.
Similarly, our Sages ordained that whoever weds a virgin should celebrate with her for seven days.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Eivel</i> 1:1 states that Moses ordained the seven days of mourning and the seven days of wedding celebrations for the Jewish people.</i> He should not pursue his occupation, nor should he involve himself in commercial dealings; he should eat, drink and celebrate.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">During these days, it is customary for the friends and family of the bride and groom to host them at celebrations referred to as <i>sheva berachot</i> ("seven blessings") for the seven wedding blessings recited after the meal at these celebrations, as explained in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i>, Chapter 2.</i> [This ruling applies] regardless of whether the groom had been married before or not.<br>If the bride is not a virgin, [he should celebrate with her] for no less than three days. For it is an ordinance of our Sages that a husband - regardless of whether he was married before or not - should celebrate with a non-virgin bride for three days.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that if the groom was not married previously, he should celebrate with his bride for seven days even when she had been married before. He draws support from <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 2:9, which states that in such an instance the seven wedding blessings are recited for the week following the wedding.</i>
A man may wed several women at one time on one day and recite the marriage blessings for all of them at the same time. With regard to the celebrations, however, he must rejoice with each bride the time allotted to her: seven days for a virgin, three days for a non-virgin. One celebration should not be allowed to overlap with another.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 14, which explains the source for this ruling.</i>
It is permitted to consecrate a woman on any weekday,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Even during Chol Hamo'ed (<i>Hilchot Sh'vitat Yom Tov</i> 7:16).</i> even on Tish'ah B'Av,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The mourning customs of that day do not prevent one from consecrating a wife. The rationale: another man may consecrate the woman instead of him (Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Ketubot</i> 1:1).</i> whether during the day or during the night. With regard to weddings, by contrast, a wedding is not conducted on a Friday<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Although many of the early Sephardi authorities object, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 64:3, based on the ruling of the <i>Tur</i>) states that in the present age, it is customary to hold weddings on Friday, provided the groom spends three days preparing for the wedding feast.<br>(A wedding may be held on Sunday or Monday. The fact that the Sabbath is interposed in between does not mean that three days were not spent in preparation for the wedding.)</i> or a Sunday. [This is] a decree, [ordained] lest conducting the wedding feast lead to the desecration of the Sabbath, for a groom is preoccupied with the wedding feast. Needless to say, a wedding is not conducted on the Sabbath.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to weddings on the Sabbath, there is an additional reason for the prohibition. A wedding involves a <i>kinyan</i>, the acquisition of the rights of the marriage contract, and it is forbidden to make a <i>kinyan</i> on the Sabbath (<i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 23:14).</i><br>Even on Chol HaMo'ed weddings are not held, as we have explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Sh'vitat Yom Tov, loc. cit.</i></i> for one celebration should not be mixed with another, as [implied by Genesis 29:27]: "Complete the week [of celebration] of this one and then I will give you this other one."<br>On other days, it is permitted to wed a woman on any day one desires, provided one spends three days preparing for the wedding feast.
In a locale where the court holds session only on Monday and Thursday, a virgin bride should be wed on Wednesday. Thus, if her husband has a claim with regard to her virginity,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This subject is discussed in detail in the following chapter.</i> he can take it to the court early the next morning.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages desired that he take the matter to court so the matter be investigated, lest the bride had in fact committed adultery (for in the Talmudic age, <i>erusin</i> preceded <i>nisu'in</i>). If the wedding were held on another day, our Sages feared that in the time the husband was waiting for the court to hold sessions, his wife would soothe his anger (Rashi, <i>Ketubot</i> 2a).</i><br>It is the custom of the Sages that a man who weds a non-virgin bride should wed her on Thursday, so that he will celebrate with her on Thursday, Friday and the Sabbath.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since the wedding blessings are recited for only one day when both the bride and the groom have been married before, our Sages feared that the man would ignore his wife on the day following their wedding and immediately return to work. To prevent this, they suggested that the wedding be held on Thursday. For the husband will not consider going to work on Friday and the Sabbath (<i>Ibid.</i>).</i> On Sunday, he will go back to work.
When a man consecrates his daughter while she is below the age of majority, both she and her father may object and delay the wedding until she comes of age and becomes a <i>na'arah</i>. If [the husband] desires to wed her, he may.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if the husband forces the bride to agree, the wedding is binding. The <i>Drishah</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 56) interprets the Rambam's wording to mean that the father desires to have his daughter wed before she comes of age. Some maintain that there is a slight printing error in the standard text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, and the proper version is "if they desire" - i.e., the bride and her father. (See <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 56:6.)</i> It is not proper, however, to do so.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Instead, the father should wait until his daughter comes of age and willingly agrees to marry her spouse. (See Chapter 3, Halachah 19.)</i>
If a man consecrated [a girl], delayed several years, and seeks to wed her while she is a <i>na'arah</i>, the girl is given twelve months from the day he makes his request, to outfit herself<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., to buy garments and jewelry (<i>Ketubot</i> 57b).</i> and prepare what she needs for him. Only afterwards, must she wed.<br>If he makes his request after she becomes a <i>bogeret</i>, she is given twelve months from the day she becomes a <i>bogeret</i>. Similarly, if he consecrates her on the day on which she becomes a <i>bogeret</i>, she is given twelve months from the day of the <i>kiddushin</i> - i.e., the day on which she became a <i>bogeret</i>.<br>When he consecrates her after she has become a <i>bogeret</i>, if more than twelve months have passed from the time she became a <i>bogeret</i> until he consecrates her, she is given only 30 days from the day he requests to wed her [to prepare]. Similarly, when a man consecrates a non-virgin bride,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even if she is not yet a <i>bogeret</i>. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and others explain that the Rambam is referring to a widow who is consecrated. (Therefore, she is given only 30 days, for she had already prepared herself for her first marriage.) He uses the term "non-virgin" to exclude a bride who had previously been widowed after consecration, but had never wed.</i> she is given 30 days [to prepare] from the day he requests to wed her.
Just as a woman is given time to outfit herself after her groom requests to wed her and then the wedding is held, so too, time is granted to the man to prepare himself<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., to prepare for the wedding celebrations and to prepare a home and furnishings.</i> if the woman requests the wedding to be held.<br>How much time is granted him? The same as is granted her. If [she would be granted] twelve months, [he is granted] twelve months. If [she would be granted] thirty days, [he is granted] thirty days.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that the Rambam's wording implies that everything depends on the woman's status. If she would be given twelve months to prepare herself, her husband is given that amount of time. The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Ketubot</i> 5:3) states that it is his status that is the determining factor: if he has never been married, he is given twelve months. If he is a widower, he is given thirty days. The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 56) follows that position.</i>
When the time allotted to the man passes and he still has not wed his <i>arusah</i>, he is obligated to provide her livelihood, although they have not wed. [Nevertheless,] if [the final day in] the time allotted him falls on Sunday or Friday, he is not liable for her livelihood on that day, for the wedding cannot be held then.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 14. With regard to this and the other examples that follow, the rationale is that since he is prevented by forces beyond his control from wedding her, he is not liable. Note the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 56:3), who states that if the man voluntarily delays the wedding and thus, becomes obligated to support his <i>arusah</i>, he must continue to support her even if she falls ill, and the wedding must be postponed because of her illness.</i> Similarly, if he or she falls ill or she enters the <i>niddah</i> state at the conclusion of the time allotted him, he is not obligated to provide her with her livelihood. For she is not fit to wed until she purifies herself,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 2.</i> or until she becomes healthy. Similarly, he is not able to wed a woman until he regains his health.

Chapter 11

[The following laws apply when a man] weds a virgin who was widowed or divorced or who underwent the rite of <i>chalitzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the woman had been consecrated or wed, but before she and her husband engaged in marital relations, she was either widowed or divorced.</i> If she was widowed or divorced or underwent the rite of <i>chalitzah</i> after <i>erusin</i> alone, the <i>ketubah</i> [to which she is entitled from her second husband] is 200 <i>zuz</i>. If, however, she had been wed, the <i>ketubah</i> [to which she is entitled from her second husband] is 100 <i>zuz</i>. Once she is wed, she is considered to be a non-virgin.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Even if there are witnesses to the fact that her husband died directly after they entered the <i>chuppah</i> (<i>Ketubot</i> 11a).</i><br>Similar [rules apply when a man] weds a virgin [bride] who is [a Canaanite maidservant] who has been freed, who is a convert, or who was held captive [by gentiles and freed]. If the maidservant had been freed, the convert had converted, or the women held captive had been redeemed before they reached the age of three years and one day,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that even if a woman engaged in sexual relations before the age of three, her hymen will grow back, as stated in Halachah 3, based on <i>Ketubot</i> 11b.</i> they are entitled to a <i>ketubah</i> of 200 <i>zuz</i>. If [this took place after they reached that age, their <i>ketubah</i> is [only] 100 [<i>zuz</i>].
Why did our Sages ordain that these women receive a <i>ketubah</i> of [only] 100 [<i>zuz</i>] even though they are virgins? Because it is a presumption that can be accepted as fact that a woman who is wed will engage in marital relations, and similarly, that a maidservant, a gentile woman and a woman held captive by gentiles will have engaged in relations. Hence, they ordained that such women would be entitled to [only] 100 [<i>zuz</i>],whether they engaged in relations or not. With regard to all matters, they are considered to be non-virgins.
A <i>mukat etz</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Literally, "one struck by a piece of wood," a woman who claims that she did not have hymenal bleeding at the time of her first sexual experience, because she had previously been "struck by a piece of wood" and caused to bleed at that time. As mentioned in Halachah 10, the term is used to refer to any woman who claims that her failure to have hymenal bleeding resulted from causes other than intercourse.</i> [is granted] a <i>ketubah</i> of 100 [<i>zuz</i>]. Even if [her husband] wed her under the presumption that she was a virgin and then he discovered that she was a <i>mukat etz</i>, she is entitled to a <i>ketubah</i> of 100 [<i>zuz</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Although one might think that the marriage would be annulled, because the husband was operating under a misconception (מקח טעות), <i>Ketubot</i> 11b rules that this is not so. As long as she had not engaged in sexual relations previously, their marriage is binding.</i><br>When a girl of less than three years of age engages in sexual relations, even when her partner is an adult male, she [is entitled to] a <i>ketubah</i> of 200 [<i>zuz</i>]. Ultimately, she will heal and be a virgin like all others.<br>Similarly, when a boy below the age of nine engages in sexual relations with an adult woman, she [is entitled to] a <i>ketubah</i> of 200 [<i>zuz</i>], as if she had never engaged in relations.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 67:4) follows the ruling of <i>Tosafot, Ketubot</i> 11b, who explain that this law applies only when the woman's hymen remains intact despite these relations.</i> For it is only after a boy reaches the age of nine years and one day that relations with him are of consequence. Before that age, they are of no consequence.
Whether a virgin is a <i>bogeret</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This point is necessary to mention because of the factors stated in Halachah 12.</i>, blind,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketubot</i> 36b explains that we are afraid that such a woman might have suffered hymenal bleeding from causes other than intercourse, but will not have noticed the fact.</i> or an <i>aylonit,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Ketubot</i> 36a) explains that since an <i>aylonit</i> is considered a <i>bogeret</i>, this point must be clarified, as it must with regard to a <i>bogeret</i>. The above ruling applies only when the husband was aware that the woman was an <i>aylonit</i>. If he was not aware of that fact, the woman is not entitled to a <i>ketubah</i> at all, as explained in Chapter 24, Halachah 2.</i> she [is entitled to] a <i>ketubah</i> of 200 [<i>zuz</i>]. By contrast, no provision was made for a <i>ketubah</i> for a woman who is a deaf mute or mentally incompetent. [The rationale is] that no provision has been made for the marriage of a mentally incompetent woman at all.<br>With regard to a woman who is a deaf mute, although our Sages made provision for her marriage, they did not entitle her to a <i>ketubah</i>, so that a man would not refrain from marrying her. Just as she is not entitled to a <i>ketubah</i>, so too, [her husband] is not [obligated to provide] her with her livelihood or grant her any other [of the ordinary] conditions of the marriage contract.<br>If one wed a woman who was a deaf mute and her difficulty was remedied, she is entitled to a <i>ketubah</i> and to the other conditions of the marriage contract. [The amount of] her <i>ketubah</i> is 100 <i>zuz</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Even if she was a virgin at the time of their original marriage, at present she is not a virgin.</i>
When a man marries a woman who is a deaf mute or mentally incompetent and writes her a <i>ketubah</i> for 10,000 [<i>zuz</i>], the obligation is binding; it was he who desired to diminish his assets.
[The following rules apply when] a deaf mute or a mentally incompetent man married a woman who was mentally competent. Even if afterwards the deaf mute's disability disappears and the mentally incompetent person gains stability, they are under no obligation to their wives. If, however, [the men] desire to remain [married] to [the women] after their own wellbeing has been restored, [the wives] are entitled to a <i>ketubah</i>, and its value should be 100 <i>zuz</i>.<br>If the deaf mute's marriage was made by the court, and they write [his wife] a <i>ketubah</i> against his assets, she is entitled to everything that the court has prescribed for her. A court will not arrange a marriage for a mentally incompetent person at all. Since the sages' injunction will not be maintained in his instance,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a marriage between a mentally incompetent man and an ordinary woman will constantly be pained by strife and will not last. In contrast, a deaf mute is more passive, and his household will not necessarily be characterized by friction (<i>Yevamot</i> 112b).</i> they did not ordain marriage for him at all.<br>Similarly, our Sages did not ordain marriage for a male below the age of majority; [the rationale is that] ultimately he will gain the potential to enter into a comprehensive marriage bond.<br>Why then did they ordain marriage for a girl below the age of majority<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a girl who has been orphaned of her father, or who was divorced after being wed. The Torah - and not our Sages - gives a father the right to consecrate his daughter before she becomes a <i>na'arah</i>.</i> although she too will ultimately gain the potential for a comprehensive marriage bond? So that she will not be treated in a wanton manner.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If the girl remains unmarried, the prohibitions against relations with her are not as severe, and the Sages feared that they would not be upheld. If she were allowed to marry, the prohibition against adultery would be respected, and she would be treated differently. Moreover, her husband will guard against her association with other men.</i><br>A youth should not be [allowed to] marry until he has been examined, and it has been determined that he has manifested signs of physical maturity.
When a male below the age of majority marries a woman, she is not entitled to a <i>ketubah</i>, even if he is already nine years and one day old. If he attains majority and remains [married] to her, she is entitled to the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., only the fundamental requirements of the <i>ketubah</i>, but not any additional amount that the youth added to the marriage contract, unless he renews that commitment after he reaches majority. Otherwise, that commitment - like any commitment made by a minor - is of no substance. Moreover, he is obligated for the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i> only when he engaged in marital relations with his wife after he attained majority. If not, the marriage - and thus the marriage contract - is of no consequence.<br>With regard to the fundamental requirements of the <i>ketubah</i>, the Rambam writes in his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Ketubot</i> 9:8) that she is entitled to either 200 or 100 <i>zuz</i>, depending on her status at the time of the wedding.</i><br>Similarly, when a man converts together with his wife, she is entitled to a <i>ketubah</i> [of 100 <i>zuz</i>]. It was with this intent that he maintained their marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher differs and maintains that the laws applying to a convert are the same as those applying to a minor. Both opinions are alluded to by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 67:11). (See the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 67:12, which explains the Rambam's position: Even if a convert made a commitment of more than 100 <i>zuz</i> to his wife, any sum above 100 <i>zuz</i> is considered to be an addition to the <i>ketubah</i> and is therefore no longer binding when the convert accepts his new status as a Jew.)</i>
Whenever a virgin bride is entitled to a <i>ketubah</i> of 200 [<i>zuz</i>], there is [the possibility of issuing] a claim against her, [denying] her virginity. Whenever, by contrast, a bride is entitled to a <i>ketubah</i> of [only] 100 [<i>zuz</i>],<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even a woman who was widowed after the wedding, before engaging in relations with her husband. Even though her second husband marries her under the impression that she is a virgin, there is no possibility of issuing such a claim against her.</i> or the Sages did not entitle her to a <i>ketubah</i> at all,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation to grant a virgin bride a <i>ketubah</i> of 200 <i>zuz</i> is Rabbinic in origin. At the same time that our Sages instituted that obligation, they granted the husband a safeguard: that his word would be accepted with regard to a claim denying the woman's virginity. In these instances, since the woman was not granted the additional money, the safeguard provided by the Sages also does not apply (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> there is no [possibility of issuing] a claim against her [denying] her virginity. [Similarly,] if [a groom] enters into privacy with his <i>arusah</i> before their wedding, there is no [possibility of issuing] a claim against her [denying] her virginity.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">We suspect that the groom had relations with her and later forgot the matter (Rashi, <i>Ketubot</i> 9b). See also note 30.</i>
What is meant by a claim [denying a woman's] virginity? [A man] married a woman on the assumption that she was a virgin, and [after the wedding] claims that he did not find signs of virginity. For there are two signs of virginity: a) [hymenal] bleeding at the conclusion of her first sexual experience; b) tightness that is felt during sexual relations at that time.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in the following halachot, unless there are other factors that support the woman's position, as will be explained, the husband's claim is accepted. We assume that the husband would not go to the time and expense of preparing a wedding feast and then mar the celebration by denying his wife's virginity unless the claim were true (<i>Ketubot</i> 10a).</i>
When [a man] weds a virgin who is granted a <i>ketubah</i> of 200 [<i>zuz</i>], and claims that he did not discover signs of her virginity, the woman is questioned [regarding the matter]. If she says, "It is true that he did not find me a virgin, but this is because I fell, and I was struck by a piece of wood or the ground, and my hymen was damaged," her word is accepted and she is entitled to a <i>ketubah</i> of [100 <i>zuz</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Unless there are witnesses who can testify that the woman engaged in relations previously, the only question before the court is the amount of the woman's <i>ketubah</i>. She is permitted to remain married to her husband, because there is no proof that she willingly engaged in sexual relations with another person after she was consecrated. (See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 18:10.)<br>An exception to the above is a woman married to a priest. Issuing a claim questioning her virginity places the entire foundation of their marriage in doubt.</i><br>Although [her husband] claims: "Perhaps you engaged in intercourse, and I am under no obligation to you,"<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the husband claims that he has entered into a <i>mekach ta'ut</i>, an agreement based on false premises. He had desired to marry a virgin, and he was not prepared to marry a woman who had had relations with another man. Therefore, he desires to have the marriage annulled entirely.</i> his claim is not accepted, for his claim is not absolute.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he is not certain that she had engaged in relations with another man. In all matters of Torah law, whenever one person has a claim that is absolute (<i>bari</i>, in this instance the woman's claim that her hymen was damaged by factors other than intercourse) and one that is not absolute (<i>shema</i>, the man's claim), the claim that is absolute is accepted.</i> He may, however, have a ban of ostracism issued, conditional on her having engaged in relations with another man.
If [the woman] says, "It is true that he did not find me a virgin, for another man raped me after I had been consecrated by him," her word is accepted, and she is entitled to a <i>ketubah</i> of 200 [<i>zuz</i>] as before.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since she was raped against her will, she is not forced to suffer a loss and is entitled to the full amount of the <i>ketubah</i>.</i><br>If [her husband] claims: "Perhaps you were raped before you were consecrated, and the agreement I entered was based on false premises. Or perhaps you willingly engaged in relations after you were consecrated" [his claim is not accepted]. He may, however, have a ban of ostracism issued conditionally against anyone who makes a false claim to have him incur a financial obligation for which he is not liable.
If he claims, "I did not find her a virgin," and she claims, "He has not had intercourse with me and I am still a virgin," she should be examined. Alternatively, he should have relations with her under the surveillance of witnesses [and the truth will be clarified].<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The intent is not that witnesses should observe the couple engaging in relations. This is forbidden, as stated in Chapter 14, Halachah 16. Instead, the intent is that they should inspect the sheet before and after the couple engage in relations for signs of hymenal bleeding.</i><br>If she claims, "He had relations with me and he found me a virgin like all others, and his claim is false," he is questioned [and asked to clarify his statements]. We ask him: "Why do you say that she was not a virgin?" If he answers: "Because she did not have hymenal bleeding," we check her family [history]. Perhaps [the women of] this [family] are known not to have [vaginal] bleeding at all: neither menstrual bleeding nor hymenal bleeding. If this was found to be true, we presume [that she was a virgin, and she is entitled to a <i>ketubah</i> of 200 <i>zuz</i>].<br>If the women in her family are not known to have such a condition, we check her [physical state]; perhaps she is afflicted by a serious infirmity that has parched her body's natural fluids, or [perhaps] she was afflicted by hunger. Therefore, we have her bathe, eat and drink until she becomes healthy. At which point, [the couple] engage in relations again to see if she manifests hymenal bleeding or not.<br>If she is not hampered by sickness, hunger or the like, the [husband's] claim that she was not a virgin [is accepted]. [This applies] even if he felt tightness during relations. Since there was no hymenal bleeding, her hymen was not intact. For every virgin will manifest hymenal bleeding, whether she is a minor or above the age of majority, whether a <i>na'arah</i> or a <i>bogeret</i>, unless [this is prevented by an external factor,] illness or the like, as explained.<br>If [the husband] said: "[I claim that she was not a virgin,] because I did not feel tightness [during intercourse]. Instead, I found an open passageway," we inquire with regard to [the woman's] age. Perhaps she is a <i>bogeret</i>, and most <i>bogrot</i> do not have tightness that can be felt substantially [during intercourse], for as she grew older [the adhesion of] her limbs lessened, and the virginal [tightness] disappeared.<br>If she had not become a <i>bogeret</i> yet, we ask him: "Perhaps you leaned on the side or [entered] gently<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Other authorities (and their opinion is quoted in the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 68:6) state: "Maybe you did not enter gently?" - i.e., because of the husband's hurry to complete the sexual act, he did not feel the tightness.<br>The Ramah (<i>loc. cit.</i>) quotes the opinion of Rabbenu Asher, who states that the claim: "I discovered an open passageway," can be made only by a man who has been married before. If he was not married before, he would not have the experience to know the difference between virginal tightness and a non-virgin's state.</i> during intercourse, and therefore you did not feel any tightness?" If he replies: "No. I found an open passageway," [his] claim that she was not a virgin [is accepted] with regard to any woman who has not reached the age of <i>bagrut</i>, regardless of whether she was a minor or a <i>na'arah</i>, or whether she was healthy or sick. For the vaginal channel of every virgin is closed. Even if she manifests hymenal bleeding, she is not considered to be a virgin, because the vaginal channel was open.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramban and the Rashba state that the claim that the woman's vaginal channel was open can be made only in an instance in which the sheet on which the couple had relations was lost. If, however, the sheet is available, it should be inspected. If it has signs of blood, she is considered a virgin; and if not, she is not. This opinion is mentioned in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), but does not appear to have been accepted.</i>
There are <i>geonim</i> who rule that for a <i>bogeret</i>, the claim that she did not have hymenal bleeding is not valid, but the claim that her vaginal channel was open is valid. This does not appear [to be based on the proper text of] the Talmud. They had inaccurate versions of the text. I have investigated many texts, including those of an early era,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 15:2, where the Rambam states that he had available texts of the Talmud that were almost 500 years old. These would have been written approximately 200 years after the time of the Talmud's composition.</i> and I have discovered the version to be as I ruled. For a <i>bogeret</i>, the only valid claim is [that she did not manifest] hymenal bleeding.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's ruling is substantiated by our text of the Talmud (<i>Ketubot</i> 36b) and the ruling of the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 68:3). The differing opinion mentioned by the Rambam is that of Rabbenu Chanan'el.</i>
Our Sages were those who instituted the fundamental requirement of a marriage contract for a woman and they also instituted [the following consideration]: Whenever [a man] makes a claim that his wife was not a virgin, and the woman disputes his claim, [the husband's claim] is accepted. It is the woman's responsibility to bring support for her claim, not the man's. [The rationale is] that we assume that a man will not labor to prepare a [wedding] feast and then mar it, turning his celebration into mourning.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Based on this rationale, the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> mentions opinions that state that the man's word is accepted only when he prepared the wedding feast. If he did not, the woman's word is accepted.</i>
Until when may a husband issue a claim denying his wife's virginity? If [the couple] went into privacy, only immediately [thereafter].<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that the couple had relations and he discovered her to be a virgin. The fact that he issued a claim against her afterwards stemmed from discontent for other reasons, without any connection to her personal state.</i> If they did not enter into privacy, he has this option even after 30 days.
All the <i>geonim</i> have ruled that our Sages' statement that the husband's statements are accepted even though his wife disputes his claim applies only with regard to nullifying the obligation for the fundamental requirement of the marriage contract. Nevertheless, the woman is entitled to the additional amount [to which her husband committed himself]<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Although there are authorities (among them Rabbenu Asher) who offer reasons why the husband's word should be accepted in this instance as well, the prevailing view (and the ruling of the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 68:8) follows the Rambam's decision. The rationale is that the fundamental requirement of the marriage contract is a Rabbinic injunction, and the same authority that obligated the husband to meet this requirement rescinded it when he lodged a claim denying her virginity. The additional amount, by contrast, is a present to which the husband voluntarily obligated himself, and that obligation may be nullified only if it is proven that it was made under false premises.</i> unless there is clear proof that she was not a virgin, or she admitted that she was not a virgin before she was consecrated and that she deceived him.<br>Therefore, [the husband] may require her to take an oath while holding a sacred article,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Sh'vuot</i> 11:8, which states that such an oath is administered while the person is holding a Torah scroll. Significantly, the Rambam's ruling here represents a change of mind from his statements in his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Ketubot</i> 1:3), where he states that in such a situation the woman is required to take merely a <i>sh'vuat hesset</i>, a less severe oath.</i> as must be done by all others who must take oaths before they collect [the money due them].<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the situation is analogous to a person who holds a promissory note and may be asked to take an oath that it is valid before he can collect it, as explained in <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 14:2-3.</i> Afterwards, she may collect the additional sum.<br>She, by contrast, does not have the option of requiring him to take an oath that he did not discover her to be a virgin, before she must forfeit the fundamental requirement of the marriage contract, for it is a presumption accepted as fact that a person will not labor to prepare a [wedding] feast and then mar it. She may, however, have a ban of ostracism issued conditionally, applying to anyone who lodges false claims against her.
If [the husband] desires to remain married to [his wife] after causing her to forfeit the fundamental requirement of the marriage contract, he must write her [a new <i>ketubah</i> for] 100 [<i>zuz</i>]. For it is forbidden for a man to live with his wife for even one moment without a <i>ketubah</i>, as we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 10, Halachah 10.</i>

Chapter 12

When a man marries a woman, whether she is a virgin or a non-virgin, whether she is above the age of majority or a minor, and whether she was born Jewish, is a convert or a freed slave, he incurs ten responsibilities toward her and receives four privileges.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">These ten responsibilities and four privileges are all explained in detail in the chapters that follow, through Chapter 23.</i>
With regard to his ten responsibilities: three stem from the Torah. They include <i>sha'arah, kesutah v'onatah.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>These requirements are mentioned in Exodus 21:10. The verse forbids a husband from denying his wife these rights. Sefer HaMitzvot (Negative Commandment 262) and Sefer HaChinuch (Mitzvah 46) consider this to be one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i></i> Sha'arah</i> means providing her with subsistence.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Note the commentary of the Ramban on Exodus (<i>loc. cit.</i>), which interprets <i>sha'arah</i> and <i>kesutah</i> as also referring to conjugal rights and maintains that the obligation to provide a wife with her subsistence and with garments is Rabbinic. Most authorities, however, follow the Rambam's understanding.</i> <i>Kesutah</i> means supplying her with garments, and <i>onatah</i> refers to conjugal rights.<br>The seven responsibilities ordained by the Rabbis are all conditions [of the marriage contract] established by the court. The first is the fundamental requirement of the marriage contract. The others are referred to as <i>t'na'ei ketubah</i>, the conditions of the marriage contract. They are:<br>a) to provide medical treatment if she becomes sick;<br>b) to redeem her if she is held captive:<br>c) to bury her if she dies;<br>d) to provide for her from his possessions;<br>e) the right for her to continue living in his home after his death as long as she remains a widow;<br>f) the right for her daughters to receive their subsistence from his estate after his death until they become consecrated;<br>g) the right for her sons to inherit her <i>ketubah</i> in addition to their share in her husband's estate together with their brothers [borne by other wives, if she dies before her husband does].
The four privileges that the husband is granted are all Rabbinic in origin. They are:<br>a) the right to the fruits of her labor;<br>b) the right to any ownerless object she discovers;<br>c) the right to benefit from the profits of her property during her lifetime;<br>d) the right to inherit her [property] if she dies during his lifetime. His rights to her property supersede [the rights of] all others.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad and others maintain that the husband's right to inherit his wife's property stems from the Torah itself. The matter is the subject of a difference of opinion between our Sages (<i>Ketubot</i> 83b), and there is no explicit resolution of the question in the Talmud. Rav Kapach maintains that the early manuscripts of the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Ketubot</i> 9:1; <i>Bava Batra</i> 8:1) indicate that the Rambam himself originally subscribed to the view mentioned by the Ra'avad and changed his mind later in life. (See also Halachah 9.)</i>
Our Sages also ordained that the fruits of a wife's labor should parallel her subsistence, [the obligation to] redeem her should parallel [the right to] the benefit from her property, and [the obligation to] bury her should parallel [the right to] inherit [the property mentioned in] her <i>ketubah</i>.<br>Therefore, if a woman says: "I will not [hold you obligated for] my subsistence, but I will not work,"<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to the other two matters that are linked the husband's obligation to redeem her and to bury her, the woman does not have this option. Although this arrangement was instituted for the woman's benefit, our Sages did not give her a choice regarding these matters, because they desired to ensure that the woman would not be forced to remain in captivity among the gentiles and that she would be buried (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> and Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 69:5).</i> she is given this option, and she cannot be compelled to work.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages instituted this arrangement for the woman's benefit, since a woman's income could not ordinarily provide for her subsistence. Accordingly, the option of whether or not to forego the arrangement is in the woman's hands. If a woman can earn more than her subsistence, she is also entitled to forego the above arrangement.<br>Even in such a situation, the woman is still responsible for taking care of the household tasks (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> If, however, her husband says: "I will not provide for your subsistence, and I will not receive the right to the fruits of your labor," he is not given this option, lest the woman be unable to earn her subsistence.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The husband may, however, tell his wife: "Endeavor to earn your subsistence, and I will compensate for whatever deficiency remains" (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 69:4).</i> Because of this institution, [the obligation to provide for a woman's] subsistence is considered to be one of the <i>t'na'ei ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., although the <i>t'na'ei ketubah</i> are rabbinic in origin, and the obligation to provide for the woman's subsistence is from the Torah, since the linkage of it with her wages is rabbinic, the obligation is considered to be part of the <i>t'na'ei ketubah</i>.</i>
Whether or not these matters were written in the marriage contract - indeed, even if a marriage contract was not written and the couple merely married - once they marry, the husband is granted the four privileges mentioned, and the woman is granted the ten rights mentioned. There is no need to state them explicitly.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they are obligations that apply universally and are not dependent on the consent of a particular couple.</i>
If the husband made a stipulation that he would not be responsible for one of these obligations - or the wife made a stipulation that [her husband] would not be granted one of these privileges - [and the other party agreed,] the stipulation is binding,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The principle upon which this statement is based is that any stipulation to which both parties agree that concerns monetary rights - even those that are granted to a person by the Torah - is binding (<i>Kiddushin</i> 19b). For a person has the option to waive his right to property or privileges that justly belong to him (Rashi, <i>loc. cit.</i>). Therefore, a woman may waive even the rights to her subsistence or clothing that the Torah itself grants her.</i> with the exception of three matters with regard to which it is impossible for a stipulation to be made. Indeed, if a stipulation is made with regard to these three matters, it is of no consequence. These [three] are: [the woman's] conjugal rights, the fundamental requirement of the marriage contract and [the husband's right] to inherit [his wife's property].
What is implied? If [the groom] made a stipulation with his bride that he is not obligated to give her conjugal rights, his stipulation is of no substance. For he has made a stipulation against what is written in the Torah, and the stipulation does not concern financial matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Instead, the failure to provide a woman with conjugal rights is considered to cause her physical anguish (Rashi, <i>loc. cit.</i>). Although the Mordechai maintains that conjugal rights can also be considered monetary matters, for it is possible to give a woman enough money that she would be willing to forego her rights, the Rambam's view is accepted by most authorities.</i>
When a man makes a stipulation to reduce the amount of the fundamental requirement of the marriage contract - or he writes a <i>ketubah</i> for either 200 or 100 [<i>zuz</i>], but she writes that she has already received a portion of the sum, when in fact she did not<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the woman writes a receipt for part of the sum on her <i>ketubah</i>.<br>With regard to this instance, the <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 66) differs and maintains that the man is not obligated to pay her the full sum.</i> - his stipulation is of no substance.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Although this is a situation that concerns financial matters, our Sages desired that the fundamental requirement of the marriage contract be a binding institution, and therefore did not allow any modification of this obligation. Hence, the stipulation is nullified.<br>Note the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, who mentions views that differ with that of the Rambam and maintains that if the man desires to divorce the woman, he is not obligated to give her the sum for which the Sages obligated him. It is only when he wants to remain married to her that our Sages enforced their requirement.</i> For whenever a person establishes a marriage contract with a virgin for less than 200 [<i>zuz</i>] or with a non-virgin for less than 100 [<i>zuz</i>], the sexual relations [he conducts with his wife] are considered promiscuous.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 66:9) states that even though the man's stipulations are of no consequence, the sexual relations he conducts with his wife are considered promiscuous, because she may be unaware of the law and not know the amount due her.</i>
If he makes a stipulation after he weds her<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A different ruling applies if the stipulation is made between <i>erusin</i> and <i>nisu'in</i>, as explained in Chapter 23, Halachah 6.</i> that he will not inherit her property, his stipulation is of no consequence. Although the husband's [right to] inherit [his wife's property] is a rabbinic institution, [our Sages] reinforced their edict, [giving it the power of a statute of] the Torah.<br>With regard to [the Torah's statutes of] inheritance, all stipulations that are made are of no consequence, despite the fact that financial matters are concerned, as [derived from Numbers 27:11]: "the statutes of judgment."<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 6:1.</i><br>With regard to other [aspects of the marriage contract], a stipulation [made by the husband and accepted by his wife] is binding. For example, if he made a stipulation that he is not obligated to supply her with her subsistence or with clothing, or that he would not receive the benefits from her property, his stipulation is binding.
What is the amount that is designated for a woman's subsistence? We allot her bread for two meals every day, according to the norm of the people of her town, for a person who is neither sick nor a glutton.<br>The allotment is also made according to the type of bread eaten as a staple in that locale, be it wheat or barley, or rice, millet, or other grains, as is customary [in that locale]. Similarly, she is allotted other foods that are eaten together with bread - i.e., legumes, vegetables and the like. [She is also allotted] oil for food and to light a lamp and also fruit. She is also [allotted] a small amount of wine, if it is the local custom for women to drink wine.<br>On the Sabbath, she is allotted three meals,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">For a woman is obligated to eat three meals on the Sabbath as a man is (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim</i> 291:6).<br>Note the slight difference between the Rambam's statements here and those in <i>Hilchot Matnot Aniyim</i> 9:13.</i> and meat or fish according to the local custom. And she is given a <i>me'ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>me'ah</i> is one sixth of a <i>dinar</i> (<i>Kiddushin</i> 12a). Based on the Rambam's statements in <i>Hilchot Shekalim</i>, ch. 1, it is evident that this is a coin of relatively small value, approximately 1.5 grams of pure silver.</i> of silver for her private needs - e.g., a <i>p'rutah</i> for laundry, or for the bath and the like.
To whom does the above apply? To a poor Jewish man. But if the husband is wealthy, [the support he is required to provide his wife is apportioned] according to his wealth. If he is wealthy enough to provide her with several dishes of meat each day, he is compelled to do so, and she is allotted [subsistence] commensurate with his wealth.<br>If he is extremely poor and is unable to provide his wife with even the bread that she requires,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 70:7 states that if he can provide her with bread, even if he cannot provide her with other food, he is not obligated to divorce her. (See, however, <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 70:12.)<br>The <i>Chatam Sofer</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i>, Responsum 131) states that the Rambam's words imply that if the husband cannot support his wife from his own earnings, he is compelled to divorce her, even if she herself has the means to provide herself with subsistence.</i> he is compelled to divorce her.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that since he cannot provide her with subsistence, he is obligated to give her the opportunity to find another husband who can.<br>The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> question whether the husband can be compelled to seek to hire himself out as a laborer, or the court's only resort is to compel him to divorce his wife. Although that text does not favor either approach, the latter opinion is quoted by the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 70:3). The Ramah also mentions the opinion of <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Ketubot</i> 63a), which states that a husband who has no resources is not compelled to divorce his wife.</i> He remains indebted for her <i>ketubah</i> until he finds the means to provide payment for it.
When a husband desires to provide his wife with subsistence as befits her, on condition that she should eat and drink alone,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even in a separate dwelling (Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Ketubot</i> 5:9).</i> and that he should eat and drink alone, he is given this prerogative, provided he eats together with her on Friday night.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>), the Rambam states that this prerogative may in no way infringe on the husband's obligation to provide his wife with conjugal rights. In addition, he must share the Friday night meal with her, implying that this is for the sake of communication, not only as preparation for marital relations, as understood by some commentaries.<br>Note the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 70:2), who objects to the Rambam's ruling, and states that a man is given this prerogative only if his wife consents.</i>
When a woman has been allotted subsistence, and [the entire allotment was not used], the remainder belongs to her husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Note <i>Mishneh LaMelech</i> and the <i>Dagul MeRevavah</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 70), which state that this applies only when the woman purchased her food at a lower price than was originally estimated. If, however, she starved herself and consumed less than was allotted her, she, and not her husband, is entitled to the remainder.</i><br>If her husband is a priest, he is not entitled to provide her with all her provisions from <i>terumah</i>. [He is not given this option] because it is very difficult for her to protect [the <i>terumah</i>] from contacting ritual impurity, and to eat it while ritually pure [herself].<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Food that is <i>terumah</i> may not be eaten if it contracts ritual impurity, nor may it be eaten by a person who is himself ritually impure.</i> Instead, he should give her half her provisions from ordinary [food] and half from <i>terumah</i>.
Just as a man is required to provide his wife with her subsistence, he is required to provide for the maintenance of his children, both male and female, until they reach the age of six.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Nissim maintains that this obligation is incumbent on a father from the Torah itself, as an extension of his obligation to provide for his wife. Rabbenu Asher, however, maintains that the father's obligation is independent of the marriage bond. Even if he fathers children outside marriage, he is liable for their support.</i> Afterwards, he should continue to provide for their maintenance until they reach majority, as ordained by our Sages.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation to provide for one's children's subsistence until majority was one of the enactments instituted by the <i>Sanhedrin</i> after this body was relocated in Usha in the Galilee after the destruction of Jerusalem. At that time, several enactments were passed to direct the functioning of the Jewish community in this new phase. (See <i>Ketubot</i> 49b.)<br>Today, most rabbinic authorities maintain that because of changes in the socio-economic system, it is proper for a father to continue supporting his children well past the age of Bar or Bat Mitzvah.</i><br>If, however, he does not, he should be rebuked and embarrassed publicly, and appeals should be made to him. If he [persists in his] refusal, a public announcement is made with regard to him: "So and so is cruel and does not desire to provide for the maintenance of his children. He is worse than an impure bird, which does provide for its chicks." Nevertheless, he should not be compelled to provide for the maintenance [of children] six and older.
To what does the above apply? To a person who is not known to have resources, and it is not known whether or not he is capable of giving charity. If, however, he has resources and he possesses the means to give an amount to charity that would provide for [his children's] needs, his property is expropriated against his will<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">As evident from Halachah 17, this applies only when the father is present. The Rambam maintains that a person's property may not be expropriated for this purpose outside his presence.</i> for the purposes of charity,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Matnot Aniyim</i> 10:16, which states:<br>Although he is not obligated, when a person provides subsistence for his older sons and daughters, so that the males can study the Torah and the females will follow the straight path, . . . it is an act of charity, and indeed, a great act of charity.<br>And Chapter 7, Halachah 10, of that source, states:<br>When a person does not desire to give charity, . . . the court compels him, and administers stripes for rebelliousness until he meets the assessment made for him. [Moreover,] when he is present, his property is expropriated [for this purpose].<br></i> and [his children's] needs are provided for until they reach majority.
When a person travels to another country [and leaves his wife behind], [the following rules apply] should his wife come to court to place a claim [against her husband] for her subsistence. For the first three months from the day her husband departed, she is not given an allotment for her subsistence. [The rationale is that] it is an accepted assumption that a person does not depart without leaving provisions for his household.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 70:5) quotes opinions that state that this ruling applies only when the husband left home in an atmosphere of peace. In such a situation, we can be sure that he has provided for his family. If, however, he left home annoyed with his wife, it is plausible to assume that he did not provide for her needs.</i><br>Afterwards,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., after three months, or after she approaches the court. If she waits longer than three months, she is not given any payment for the previous period (Ramah, <i>ibid.</i>).</i> an allotment is made for her subsistence. If her husband owns property, the court expropriates his property and sells it to provide for his wife's subsistence. [When doing so,] no account is made for his wife's earnings until her husband comes.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher differs and maintains that the court should consider the amount the woman can earn when deciding on the size of her allotment. His rationale is that before expropriating a person's property, we should try to act in his interests. Although many authorities speak in favor of Rabbenu Asher's logic, they rule according to the Rambam's decision. (See <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 70:20.)<br>The <i>Avnei Milu'im</i> 70:3 explains the Rambam's position, stating that the husband is granted the right to his wife's earnings only when he provides for her subsistence willingly. When he forces her to approach the court to receive her subsistence, he has no claim on her earnings.</i> If it is discovered that she earned [money during the time that he was away], he is granted that sum.<br>Moreover, even if the matter is not taken to court, and instead the woman sells [her husband's property] on her own<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">There is a debate among the authorities whether or not she must consult experts with regard to the evaluation of the object. (See <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 70:21.)</i> in order to pay for her subsistence, the sale is binding. There is no need for a public announcement [regarding the sale of the property].<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, when property is sold by the court, it is necessary that a public announcement be made informing people of the sale, to attract customers and assure competitive bidding. (See <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 22:6.) In this instance, no such requirement is made, in order that the woman will not have to wait to receive the funds she requires.</i> Similarly, the woman is not required to take an oath [that her husband did not leave her money] until her husband comes and lodges a claim [against her], or until she comes to claim [the money due her, as stated in her] <i>ketubah</i> in the event of her husband's death. [In the latter instance, together with the oaths she is required to take to collect her <i>ketubah</i>,]<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 16, Halachah 4.</i> on the basis of the principle of <i>gilgul shevu'ah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Whenever a person is required to take an oath, the plaintiff can obligate him to take an oath on another claim. In this instance, since the woman is obligated to take an oath to her husband's heirs to collect the money due her for her <i>ketubah</i>, she can be required to take an additional oath regarding the sale of his property for her subsistence.</i> [she is also required to take an oath] that she did not sell [any more of her husband's property than] was necessary for her subsistence.
Just as the court [expropriates and] sells [the property of] a husband who travelled [to another country to provide for] the subsistence of [his] wife, so too, it [expropriates and] sells property to provide for the subsistence of his sons and daughters who are six years old or less. If, however, they are more than six [years old], [the court] does not provide for their subsistence from his property when he is not present, even when he is reputed to have means.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">We do not expropriate his property and provide for his children as an act of charity, because it is possible that he is giving charity in the place to which he has journeyed.<br>The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 71:2) states that if the person had supported his older children before leaving on his journey, provisions should be made for his children while he is away. It can be assumed that this would be his desire. The Ramah also mentions the opinion of Rabbenu Nissim, which states that if he possesses means, support should be provided for his children from his property as an act of charity. This view is not, however, accepted by most later authorities.</i><br>Similarly, when a person loses his mental faculties, the court expropriates his property and sells it to provide subsistence and other necessities for his wife and his children below the age of six.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that the Rambam's wording appears to imply that no provision is made for his older children, even when he has the means to support them. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, however, refers to <i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 11:11, which states that when a person who has means loses control of his faculties, the court levels an assessment for charity on his estate. Accordingly, it would appear that if the man has the means to give charity, his property is expropriated to pay for his children's subsistence, even if they are over six.<br>The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 71) states that in such an instance, the court should expropriate funds for the subsistence of the person's older children even if the person's estate is not large enough for an assessment for charity to be leveled against it. The rationale is that we assume that, like the majority of people, this person would also desire to support his children. The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 71:6 maintains that the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> follows this view, and not that of the Rambam.</i>
Some <i>geonim</i> ruled that an assessment should not be made for the subsistence of a woman whose husband journeyed overseas, or who died, unless she evinces possession of her <i>ketubah</i> document. If she does not evince possession of her <i>ketubah</i>, she is not entitled to subsistence. Perhaps she has already received payment for her <i>ketubah</i> from her husband, or perhaps she forfeited her <i>ketubah</i> in his favor, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 17, Halachah 19.</i> Others maintain that an assessment is made on her behalf for her subsistence, for we accept it as a presumption that she neither received payment for nor forfeited [her <i>ketubah</i>]. Hence, she is not required to show her <i>ketubah</i> [when presenting her claim].<br>I favor [the latter view] with regard to [a woman] whose husband has departed,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher and others do not accept the Rambam's distinction, and maintain that the court should also protect the interests of a person who is in another country and cannot defend himself. Nevertheless, in his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Karo defends the Rambam's decision, explaining that in contrast to an heir, the husband has the potential to take his claim to court when he returns. In his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 70:5), he quotes the Rambam's ruling. This ruling is also accepted by the later authorities.</i> since her claim to her subsistence stems from the Torah itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 2.</i> With regard to a woman whose husband died, however, she is not entitled to her subsistence until she brings her <i>ketubah</i>, for she [derives her subsistence] by virtue of a rabbinic enactment. Furthermore, her subsistence is paid from property belonging to [her husband's] heirs, and [the court] always advances claims in support of the interests of an heir.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since the heir himself was not aware of the details of his benefactor's affairs, he cannot necessarily advance claims in his own interests. Therefore, the court acts to protect them. (See <i>Bava Batra</i> 23a.)</i>
If [a woman's] husband departed on a journey, and she borrowed money for her subsistence, [her husband] is required to pay [the debt] when he returns.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 70:8) states that the benefactor must lodge a claim against the wife, who in turn must lodge a claim against her husband.</i><br>If a person voluntarily took the initiative of providing for her subsistence, when [her husband] returns the husband is not required to pay [that person]. The other person forfeited his money, [the rationale being] that [the husband] did not instruct him to provide for her, nor did she [request the assistance] as a loan.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Although the husband is obligated to pay for his wife's subsistence, our Sages rule that when a person pays a debt on behalf of a colleague without being instructed to do so, the debtor is not at all obligated to his patron.</i>
When a husband [who plans to] depart on a journey tells his wife: "Use your earnings to purchase your subsistence," she has no [right to demand] her subsistence [from him afterwards]. For if she had not accepted this agreement, and she had not felt confident, she could have issued a claim against him, or told him, "My earnings are not sufficient for me."<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Although our Sages associated a woman's earnings with her subsistence, they made this association for the woman's sake and gave her the prerogative of accepting or declining such a request. In a responsum, the Rambam writes that if it is not logical to assume that she could earn the funds required for her subsistence, for her to forfeit her rights, she must explicitly consent to her husband's stipulation.</i>
[The following rule applies if] the woman took the matter to court and was awarded an assessment for her subsistence, the court sold [her husband's landed property] and gave her [the proceeds] - or she sold [the property] herself - and afterwards, the husband came and claimed that he left provisions for her. She is required to take an oath, while holding a sacred article, that he did not make provisions for her [and then she is not held liable].<br>[The following rule applies when a husband departed on a journey, and the woman] did not take the matter to court, nor sell his property, but instead waited until he returned. [If upon his return there is a dispute,] he claims: "I made [provisions for you]," while she claims, "You did not make provisions. Instead, I borrowed money from this person to provide for myself," he is required to take a rabbinic oath<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a less severe oath instituted by the Rabbis. (See <i>Hilchot To'en V'Nit'an</i> 1:3.)</i> that he left provisions for her, and then he is not held liable. She remains responsible for the debt.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">She, however, does not have the opportunity of paying the debt until she is divorced or becomes widowed, because all her property is under lien to her husband, and he is entitled to her earnings.</i>
[In the above instance,] if she sold movable property, claiming that she sold it to provide for her subsistence, and her husband claimed that he had left provisions for her, she is required to take a rabbinic oath that he did not leave her any provisions.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was movable property and not landed property that was sold, the oath that the woman is required to take is more lenient than that mentioned in the previous halachah. The rationale is that had she desired to lie, she could have claimed that the goods were stolen or lost.</i><br>If she did not issue a claim against him, did not borrow money, and she did not sell his property, but instead strained herself during the day and during the night and earned her livelihood, she is not entitled to any recompense.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., she cannot demand reimbursement for the difference between her earnings and the amount she would ordinarily be entitled to for her subsistence (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 70:41). If she earned more than her subsistence, the additional funds belong to her, not to her husband (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 70:11).</i>
[The following rules apply when] a man takes a vow that his wife should not derive any benefit from him [or his property]. Whether he specified the span of the vow or did not specify the span of the vow, we grant him an interval of thirty days.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <i>Ketubot</i> 59b, Rabbenu Asher and Rabbenu Nissim object to the Rambam's ruling. Since the husband is liable to provide for his wife's subsistence, the vow he takes cannot override that obligation, except in specific instances. In both the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 235:2), Rav Yosef Karo follows these views.</i> If the span of his vow is concluded, or even though it is not concluded, but he has his vow annulled, this is acceptable. If not, he must divorce his wife,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">After thirty days, the matter will become public knowledge and the woman will suffer ridicule. Therefore, her husband is obligated to divorce (Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah, <i>Ketubot</i> 7:1).</i> and pay her [the money due her because of] her <i>ketubah</i>.<br>During those thirty days, she should work and [attempt to] sustain herself [through her labor]. One of [her husband's] friends should provide her<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Ketubot, op. cit.</i>) states that her husband should appoint a person to provide for her. As the Talmud explains (<i>Ketubot</i> 71a), this does not mean that he should appoint this person as an agent, for this is forbidden by his vow. Instead, he should say, "Whoever provides for my wife will not suffer a loss."</i> with those things she needs that she cannot purchase through the fruits of her labor, if the fruits of her labor are not sufficient for her.
When a person makes a vow [preventing] his wife from tasting one of the species of produce,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">As the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains, this refers to a situation in which the husband took a vow that if his wife partakes of a particular species of produce, she will be forbidden to benefit from his property (or according to the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 235:3, that sexual relations between them will be forbidden). If, however, the husband takes a vow that his wife may not eat a particular type of produce, that vow is nullified. For a person cannot take a vow to restrict the actions of another person.</i> he should be given an interval of thirty days. [If he prolongs the situation] beyond this time, he is required to divorce [his wife] and pay [her the money due her by virtue of her] <i>ketubah</i>. [This ruling applies] even when his vow prevents her from eating undesirable food, or a species that she has never tasted in her life.<br>[The following rules apply when a woman] took a vow not to partake of a particular species of produce, and [her husband] allowed the vow to stand, or she took a Nazarite vow and he did not annul it.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For, as Numbers 30:8-9 relates, a husband has the right to nullify or uphold the vows his wife takes.</i> If he desires to remain married to her and for her not to partake of this species or to be a Nazarite, he may.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">As Rav Yosef Karo mentions in both the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), other opinions require the husband to divorce his wife in such a situation.</i> If, however, he says: "I do not desire a woman with vows," he may divorce her, but he is required to pay her the money due her because of her <i>ketubah</i>. [The rationale is that] he had the option to nullify [her vow], and instead, he willingly allowed the vow to stand.

Chapter 13

To what extent is he required to provide her with garments? Annually, he must purchase for her clothes that were worth 50 <i>zuz</i> in the coinage prevalent [in the Talmudic period], these being worth six and one fourth <i>dinarim</i> of pure silver.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For a <i>zuz</i> was only one eighth pure silver.</i><br>He should provide her with new [garments] during the rainy season. After these garments become worn, she should wear them in the summer.
