The matter is not, however, entirely clear cut and should be discussed with a competent Rabbinic authority with regard to one's actual conduct.</i> For anyone who adds a soul to the Jewish people is considered as if he built an entire world.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, having children leads to the coming of the Redemption. <i>Yevamot</i> 63b states that the <i>Mashiach</i> will not come until all the souls destined to be conceived are born.</i><br>Similarly, it is a mitzvah of our Sages that a man should not live without a wife,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 1:8) explains that this commandment applies when the husband cannot support a wife who can bear children and increase the size of his family.</i> so that he will not be prompted to [sexual] thoughts.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yevamot</i> 62b, which states: "Any man who is unmarried is left without happiness, without good and without blessing."<br>Rav David Cohen quotes Rav Yitzchak Alfasi as maintaining that the requirement to marry stems from the Torah and not from our Sages, as the Rambam maintains.</i> Similarly, a woman should not live without a man,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 21:26, which states that it is permissible for a woman never to marry.</i> so that she will not be suspected [of immoral conduct].
It is an obligation for a man to admonish<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Here the term לקנאת, translated as "admonish," has a specific meaning: to warn one's wife not to enter into privacy with another man. If this warning is disobeyed, the woman must undergo the rites of a <i>sotah</i> to continue her marriage.</i> his wife. Our Sages declared:<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sotah</i> 3a. Although this is the subject of a difference of opinion among our Sages, the Rambam follows the opinion of Rabbi Akiva.</i> "A man will not admonish his wife unless a spirit of purity enters his being." [Nevertheless,] he should not admonish her more than necessary.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">See the conclusion of <i>Hilchot Sotah</i> for a more detailed treatment of this subject.</i><br>[A man] should never compel [his wife] to engage in sexual relations against her will. Instead, [relations] should be with her agreement, [preceded by] conversation and a spirit of joy.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot De'ot</i> 5:4-5.</i>
Similarly, our Sages commanded a woman to conduct herself modestly at home, not to proliferate levity or frivolity before her husband, not to request intimacy verbally,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Eruvin</i> 100b states that a woman requests intimacy with her heart.</i> nor to speak about this matter.<br>She should not deny her husband [intimacy] to cause him anguish, so that he should increase his love for her. Instead, she should oblige him whenever he desires. She should keep her distance from his relatives and the members of his household so that he will not be provoked by jealousy and should avoid scandalous situations - indeed, any trace of scandal.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 24, Halachah 15ff.</i>
Similarly, our Sages commanded that a man honor his wife more than his own person, and love her as he loves his own person. If he has financial resources, he should offer her benefits in accordance with his resources. He should not cast a superfluous measure of fear over her. He should talk with her gently, being neither sad nor angry.
And similarly, they commanded a woman to honor her husband exceedingly and to be in awe of him. She should carry out all her deeds according to his directives, considering him to be an officer or a king. She should follow the desires of his heart and shun everything that he disdains.<br>This is the custom of holy and pure Jewish women and men in their marriages. And these ways will make their marriage pleasant and praiseworthy.

Chapter 16

The property that a woman brings to her husband's [resources] - be it landed property, movable property or servants - is not referred to with the term <i>ketubah</i>, but rather with the term <i>nedunyah</i>.<br>[More particularly, there are two subdivisions within this category.] When the husband accepts responsibility for the <i>nedunyah</i> and it is considered to be his property<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the article or land that the woman brings to the household is evaluated, and the husband takes responsibility for the value of the article. From this time onward, it is as if the article were his, and he is obligated to pay his wife a fixed amount if he divorces her or she is widowed.</i> - i.e., if it decreases in value he suffers the loss, and if it increases in value the gain is his - the property is referred to as <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This term literally means "property [that is like] iron sheep." The term iron is used to indicate that the husband's obligation is unchanging, like iron. The reference to sheep stems from the fact that during the Talmudic period in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, a similar agreement was frequently made with a shepherd with regard to the sheep entrusted to him. He was given a herd that was evaluated at a given price, and he was obligated to return either sheep of that value, or payment for them to their owner. (See the commentary of Rav Ovadiah of Bertinoro, <i>Yevamot</i> 7:1.)</i><br>If the husband did not accept responsibility for the <i>nedunyah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">With this statement, the Rambam indicates that - in contrast to the opinion of certain authorities - the property belonging to a woman does not automatically become <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>. For it to be placed in that category, the husband must explicitly accept responsibility for it (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). (See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 85:3.)</i> and it instead remained the property of the woman<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to this type of property as well, the husband has the privilege to manage the use of the property and reap its benefits during the time he remains married to the woman, but the property itself belongs to her.</i> - if it decreases in value she suffers the loss, and if it increases in value the gain is hers - the property is referred to as <i>nichsei m'log</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>m'log</i> means "to pull out hairs" (Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Yevamot</i> 7:1). Just as a person pulls out the hairs from a head, leaving it uncovered, so too, the husband continues to use his wife's property even though its value depreciates.</i>
Similarly, all the property that a woman owns that she did not bring to her husband's household, nor had written in her <i>ketubah</i>, but rather left as her own, or property that came to her as an inheritance, or that was given to her as a present - all of this is referred to as <i>nichsei m'log</i>, for it is all in her possession.<br>The term <i>ketubah</i>, by contrast, refers only to the fundamental requirement of the marriage contract - i.e., 100 [<i>zuz</i> for a non-virgin] or 200 [<i>zuz</i> for a virgin] and the additional amount that [the husband promised].<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">By making a distinction between the money due a woman by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> and her <i>nedunyah</i>, the Rambam is emphasizing that they are governed by different laws. With regard to the money of the <i>ketubah</i>, the husband or his estate is granted certain leniencies. But with regard to the <i>nedunyah</i>, by contrast, the woman is considered the same as any other of her husband's creditors (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). The <i>Beit Yosef</i> and the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 100:2) quote this explanation.</i>
We have already explained that our Sages established the fundamental requirement of the marriage contract, and that the laws governing the additional amount [promised by the husband] are the same as those governing the fundamental requirement.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 10, Halachah 7.</i><br>[Our Sages] did not grant a woman the option of collecting [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> whenever she desired. Instead, it is like a debt, which is not payable until a given date. For a <i>ketubah</i>, the time when payment is due is not until after the woman's husband dies or divorces her.<br>Similarly, our Sages ordained that if a husband has fields [of varying quality] - good, bad and intermediate - when the woman comes to collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> from this property, she is entitled to collect only from the inferior fields.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Although a creditor has the right to collect his due from the properties of intermediate value, the woman is given this disadvantage. The rationale is that a woman desires to marry and therefore is willing to accept this stipulation (<i>Gittin</i> 50a).</i> They are referred to as <i>ziboorit</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Aruch HaShalem</i> interprets this term as being derived from an Arab word meaning "a rocky field." The Ramah (<i>Choshen Mishpat</i> 101:5) states that it refers to a bee that stings and is therefore considered one of the lower forms of life.</i>
Similarly, our Sages ordained that when a woman comes to collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> after her husband's death, she may not collect [this sum] until she takes an oath while holding a sacred article,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a Torah scroll, as mentioned in <i>Hilchot Sh'vuot</i> 11:8. An oath is required because the woman is seeking to collect money from an estate bequeathed to heirs, and whenever payment is to be collected from an estate, an oath is necessary (<i>Ketubot</i> 87a; <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 14:1).</i> that her husband did not leave any property in her possession,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 96) states that the woman must also take an oath that she did not seize any of her husband's property. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 96:2) quotes this view.</i> that she had not sold her <i>ketubah</i> to him, nor waived payment of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the opinion of the <i>Tur</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) and the <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i>, the Rambam maintains that even if a woman is in possession of her <i>ketubah</i>, she is required to take these oaths. (See Halachah 21.) The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) mentions the Rambam's view, but appears to favor that of the <i>Tur</i>.</i> [Her wardrobe, even] the garments she is wearing should be evaluated and the sum deducted from [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a trousseau given to the woman by her husband. Although the husband gave his wife these clothes as a gift, he did not give them to her with the intent that she take them and leave his household (<i>Ketubot</i> 54a).</i><br>If, however, he voluntarily divorces her, she may collect [the money due her] without taking an oath,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In the first half of this halachah, the oath is instituted by the court to protect the interests of the heirs. In this instance, however, unless the husband himself issues a claim requiring an oath (see Halachah 19), no oath is required.</i> nor should [her wardrobe] be evaluated.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">From <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 1:5, it appears that this does not apply to a woman's Sabbath and festival clothing, or to her jewelry. Similarly, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 99:1) states that those articles should be evaluated and deducted from the sum due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>.</i> [The rationale is that] he bought them for her, she acquired them, and it is he who desires to divorce her, and not the reverse.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the husband is compelled to divorce his wife, her wardrobe should be evaluated and deducted from the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>; see <i>Ketubot</i> 77a).</i>
Similarly, [our Sages] ordained that a widow<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This restriction applies to a widow, but not to a divorcee. The Rashba and other authorities differ and maintain that the same ruling applies to a divorcee. It appears that it is their opinion that is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 100:1).</i> should collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> from landed property only.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., and not from movable property. This ruling also applies to other creditors, as stated in <i>Hilchot Nizkei Mammon</i> 8:11. (See, however, Halachah 7 below.)</i> [Moreover,] she may not collect [her due] from the increment in the value of that property after the husband died.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., a woman was owed 200 <i>zuz</i> by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>. Her husband's property was worth 150 <i>zuz</i> at the time of his death. Although its value rose afterwards to 200, the woman is entitled to only 150, because that was its value at the time of her husband's death.</i> Similarly, after their father's death, [the woman's] daughters do not receive their subsistence<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This is one of the conditions of a woman's <i>ketubah</i>.</i> from the increment in the value of that property after his death.<br>Similarly, a woman may not collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> from the increment in the value of [landed] property accomplished through the efforts of a purchaser, although other creditors are entitled to collect their due from that increment.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Landed property that was owned by a man at the time of his marriage or acceptance of a financial obligation is considered to be on lien to his wife or to his creditor. Even if it is sold to another person, the debt can be collected from it, if the person or his estate has no other property, as stated in Halachah 10. (See also <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i>, Chapter 19.)<br>As mentioned in <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 21:1, a creditor is entitled to collect not only the property itself, but also any increment in its value, whether an increment that comes naturally, or even one that results because of effort on the part of the purchaser. A woman is not, however, given this privilege with regard to the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>.</i> These rulings are among the leniencies [granted with regard to the the payment of the money due a woman by virtue] of her <i>ketubah</i>.
Similarly, among the leniencies [granted with regard to the payment of the money due a woman by virtue] of her <i>ketubah</i> is that a woman will collect the money due her from the coinage that is of least value.<br>What is implied? A man married a woman in one country and divorced her in another. If the coinage of the country in which the couple married is more valuable than the coinage of the country in which they divorced, he may pay her with the coinage of the country in which they divorced. If, by contrast, the coinage of the country in which the couple divorced is more valuable than the coinage of the country in which they married, he may pay her with the coinage of the country in which they married.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the woman's <i>ketubah</i> mentioned 200 silver coins without specifying the type of coin, and there was a difference between the value of the silver coins used in the country where the <i>ketubah</i> was written and those used in the country where the divorce takes place. If this were a loan contract, we would say that the intent is the coins of the country in which the loan was given. As a leniency to the husband, however, the law is different with regard to a <i>ketubah</i>, and he is obligated to pay only the lesser of the two values.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 100:5) state that this law applies only when the value of the money the woman receives is not less than 100 <i>zuz</i> of the Talmudic period for a non-virgin, and 200 <i>zuz</i> for a virgin.</i><br>When does the above apply? When her <i>ketubah</i> states a sum of coins without specification. If, however, a specific type of coin is explicitly mentioned, whether with regard to the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i>, or with regard to the extra amount added by the husband, the law is the same as when a person lends a colleague a specific type of coin - he must return the loan in the coinage that he took, as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Halva'ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 4:11.</i>
The <i>geonim</i> of all the <i>yeshivot</i> ordained that after the death of a man, a woman should be able to collect her [money due her by virtue of her] <i>ketubah</i> from movable property,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that this ruling reflects a difference in the socio-economic status of the Jewish people. Land was commonly owned in the Talmudic period, and hence a woman would not feel secure unless the obligation of her <i>ketubah</i> were supported by land. In contrast, the ownership of land was less common in the era of the <i>geonim</i>. Movable property, thus, rose in importance, and a woman would feel secure even when an obligation was supported only by movable property.</i> just as they ordained that a creditor can collect the debt owed him from movable property.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Nizkei Mammon</i> 8:12.</i> This mandate spread throughout the majority of the Jewish people.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was accepted by the majority of the Jewish people, it should be adhered to. See, however, the following halachot.</i><br>Similarly, the other conditions of a woman's <i>ketubah</i> are governed by the same rules as [the fundamental requirement of] the <i>ketubah</i>, and they are binding on the movable property of the deceased's estate, as well as on the landed property. There is, however, one exception - the right of the sons to inherit their mother's <i>ketubah</i>. Since the custom of granting them this inheritance was not universally accepted by all the <i>yeshivot</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in certain places this practice was not followed. Although the Rambam maintains that the sons should be granted that privilege, their rights should not be extended beyond their original scope.</i> I maintain that the law of the Talmud should be applied in this instance, and they should inherit the money due their mother by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> only from the landed property [within the estate].<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's opinion is accepted by many authorities. Nevertheless, there are dissenting views. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 111:14) mentions both views without appearing to favor either one.</i>
In all the [Jewish] communities of which I know and have heard reports from, it has already become the custom to write the <i>ketubah</i> so that [its obligations are binding] on both the landed property and the movable property [in the estate].<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">See the Rambam's text of the <i>ketubah, Hilchot Yibbum</i> 4:33.</i><br>[Making] this addition is a great asset; it was ordained by learned men of great stature. For it is a monetary stipulation, and thus a widow is entitled to collect [the money due her] from the movable property [in her husband's] estate by virtue of this stipulation, and not by virtue of the mandate of the later sages.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">A person has the license to bind his estate to a particular obligation, although he would not be required to pay it by law. Once he makes such a commitment, his estate is bound by it.</i>
[The following rules apply when] this stipulation was not included in the text of the <i>ketubah</i>, but instead [the couple] married without making an explicit statement [in this regard]. If the husband knew of this ordinance established by the <i>geonim</i>, the woman may collect [the money due her from the movable property in his estate].<br>If, however, he was not [aware of this ordinance], or we are unsure whether he knew of it, we deliberate at length concerning this matter. For an ordinance of the <i>geonim</i> does not have the power to be applied and to have money expropriated from the heirs because of it, when it was not explicitly stated, as is the law regarding the conditions of the <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that in the Rambam's era, the observance of this ordinance had not spread throughout the entire Jewish world. Note the introduction to the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> which states that, in contrast to the ordinances of the Sages of the Talmud, an ordinance instituted by the <i>geonim</i> is not binding unless its observance has spread throughout the entire Jewish people.<br>The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 100:2 and the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 100:2 emphasize that the observance of this ordinance spread in the subsequent generations, and it is now universal Jewish practice. Therefore, it is binding even when it was not explicitly stated in the <i>ketubah</i>, and the husband's heirs claim that he was unaware of it.</i> [The distinction between the two is that the conditions of the <i>ketubah</i>] are ordinances instituted by the Great <i>Sanhedrin</i>.
Our Sages also ordained that all of a husband's property should be on lien for the woman's <i>ketubah</i>. Even if the woman's <i>ketubah</i> is [only 100 <i>zuz</i>] and [her husband] owns property worth several thousand gold pieces, it is all under lien to her <i>ketubah</i>.<br>[Her husband] is entitled to sell all his property if he desires, and his sale is binding. Nevertheless, all the property that he sells after his marriage can be expropriated [from the purchaser] by his widow [in lieu of payment for] her <i>ketubah</i> when he divorces her or when he dies, if he does not possess property that has not been sold.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the husband or his estate possesses property that has not been sold, neither the woman nor another creditor may expropriate property that has already been sold (<i>Gittin</i> 5:2).</i><br>When a woman expropriates property [from a purchaser], she must take an oath holding a sacred article, as is taken by any of [a person's] creditors [who seek to expropriate property from its purchasers]. This provision was instituted so that he should not view [the obligation of] the <i>ketubah</i> lightly.
When the court or the heirs require a widow to take an oath when she comes to collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>, the oath should be taken only outside the court.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi, the Ramban and the Rashba state that when an oath is made outside the court, God's name is not mentioned, and a Torah scroll is not held. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 96:19) quotes this view. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states, however, that the fact that the Rambam does not mention such a distinction indicates that he does not accept this concept.</i> For the court would refrain from administering the oath, lest she not be precise with herself when making it.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 35a explains that since a widow carries out certain activities on behalf of the heirs of her deceased husband's estate (who are, in most instances, her children), she feels free to take certain articles belonging to the estate, without taking this into consideration. Hence, she might be ready to take an oath that she did not benefit from the estate, when in fact she did.<br>Since the sin of - and the punishment for - taking a false oath is very harsh, our Sages wished to reduce this severity by having the oath administered outside the court.</i><br>If the heirs desired that she make a vow [instead of an oath], she may make a vow linked to any object they desire.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the woman will vow never to eat bread on the condition that she benefited from property belonging to the estate. Breaking a vow is considered a less severe transgression than taking a false oath.</i> This vow may be administered in a court. Afterwards, she should collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>.
If a widow dies before taking this oath, her heirs should not inherit her <i>ketubah</i> at all, for she does not have any rights to her <i>ketubah</i> until she takes an oath.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">This principle applies universally: Whenever a person is entitled to property only after taking an oath, that property is not transferred to the person's heirs if the oath had not been taken (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 96:1).</i><br>If the woman marries [a second time] before taking an oath [with regard to [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> from her previous husband's estate], she may take an oath after her remarriage and collect her due whenever she desires. She does not, however, have the option of making a vow, lest her [second] husband annul it.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since the woman's second husband has the option of annulling any vows made by his wife, it is possible that she will take a false vow, relying on her husband to nullify it (<i>Gittin</i> 35b).</i>
If [a woman's husband] designated a plot of land for her in her <i>ketubah</i>, whether he specified [only] one of its borders or all four of its borders, she may collect her <i>ketubah</i> from this plot of land without taking an oath.<br>Similarly, if he specified movable property [in the <i>ketubah</i>] and this movable property exists, she may take it without taking an oath. [Moreover,] if the [movable property that was specified] was sold and other movable property purchased with the proceeds, it being known that these goods were purchased with the proceeds of [the movable property specified in the <i>ketubah</i>], she may take them without taking an oath.
A woman who diminishes [the amount of money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> may collect her due only after taking an oath.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages required the woman to take an oath because they were not sure that she made a complete account of the money she received. Requiring her to take an oath insures that she will, in fact, be careful regarding this account (<i>Ketubot</i> 87b).</i><br>What is implied? A woman produces a <i>ketubah</i> that states [that she is due] 1000 <i>zuz</i>. Her husband claims that she received the entire amount, while she claims to have received only a portion of the amount. Even if there are witnesses who testify that she received the amount that she admits to having received,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that witnesses were made to observe payment of one portion of the <i>ketubah</i> is no proof that a second payment was not made without being observed by witnesses.</i> and even if she is extremely precise in accounting what she took, mentioning even [the last] half-<i>p'rutah</i>, she may collect the remainder only after taking an oath.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that she appears precise in reporting what she admits to having received is not proof that she has made a totally precise accounting (<i>Ketubot, loc. cit.</i>).<br>Similar laws apply to a creditor who states that he is actually owed a lesser amount than is stated in the contract of loan (<i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 14:1).</i>
[An oath is also required in the following instance.] The husband claims that [his wife] received all [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>], while the woman claims not to have received the money, and one witness testifies that she received either the entire sum or a portion of it. [The woman] may collect the entire [sum mentioned in] the <i>ketubah</i>, but only after taking an oath.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since the husband has only one witness to support his claim, and the woman's claim is supported by her <i>ketubah</i>, she is entitled to collect her full claim. Nevertheless, because of the witness, an oath is required.</i>
[When a divorcee collects [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>] outside the presence of her husband, she must take an oath before doing so.<br>What is implied? A man divorced his wife and departed. After his wife takes an oath, the court should expropriate his property and give [the woman the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>.<br>The above applies when the husband is in a distant place, where there is difficulty in notifying him. If, however, he is in a nearby place [where it is possible] to notify him, a message should be sent to notify him [of the court's impending action]. If he does not come, the woman should take the oath and collect [her due].
A woman who reduces the value of her <i>ketubah</i> is not required to take an oath before collecting [her due].<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to a woman who diminishes the amount of money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> (Halachah 14), this woman does not admit receiving any funds. Hence, there is no need to require an oath so that she will make a careful account (<i>Bayit Chadash, Even HaEzer</i> 96).</i><br>What is implied? A woman produces a <i>ketubah</i> that states [that she is due] 1000 <i>zuz</i>. Her husband claims that she received the entire amount, while she claims not to have received anything at all, but she admits: "I am owed only 500 <i>zuz</i>. Although he wrote 1000 for me [in the <i>ketubah</i>], there was an understanding between me and him [concerning this]." In this instance, she is not required to take an oath before collecting [her due].<br>If, however, [in the above situation,] the woman says: "My <i>ketubah</i> states only 500 <i>zuz</i>," she may not collect with this document that says [she is due] 1000 <i>zuz</i>, for she has negated it. It is as if she has admitted that it is false. Therefore, [the husband] may take a rabbinic oath [to support his claim]; he is then freed [of all obligations].
Whenever we have stated that a woman may not collect [her due] unless she takes an oath, the court tells her: "Take the oath and collect [your due]." Whenever we said that she may collect her due without an oath, [the court] tells the husband: "Give her [what is due her]. Your claim is not acceptable until you bring proof to support it."
If [in the latter instances], on his own initiative, the husband asks that [the woman] take an oath [denying] his claim, [the court] tells her: "Take the oath and collect [your due]." She must take this oath holding a sacred article.<br>If, [originally,] she made a stipulation with [her husband] enabling her to collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> without taking an oath, or that her word would be accepted regardless of what she claims, she may collect [her due] from him [in the event of a divorce] without taking any oath at all. [In the event of his death,] however, she must take an oath before collecting [her due] from his heirs.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Unless a specific statement was made to that effect, the stipulation that her husband accepted at the time of the composition of the <i>ketubah</i> applies only to himself and not to his heirs.</i>
If, [originally,] she made a stipulation with [her husband] enabling her to collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> from his heirs without taking an oath, or that her word would be accepted by his heirs regardless of what she claims, she may collect [her due] from the heirs without taking any oath at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's opinion is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 98:6). The Ramah, however, cites the opinion of Rabbenu Asher and other Ashkenazic authorities, who maintain that the husband's stipulation is not binding upon his heirs.</i><br>If, however, she comes to collect [her due] from property that has been sold, she must take an oath before collecting. Although her husband was willing to accept her word, the stipulation he made is binding only on himself and [the estate he left to] his heirs. It does not have the power to cause others to incur a financial loss.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad maintains that the woman's claim should be accepted without an oath. Since the husband made such a stipulation, it would have become public knowledge. Any person who purchased the property knew about the matter and accepted the risk. It is, however, the Rambam's ruling that is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>).</i>
A widow who is in possession of her [the document recording her] <i>ketubah</i> may collect her due, after taking an oath, even though 100 years have passed since her husband's death.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that she maintained possession of her <i>ketubah</i> indicates that her delay in presenting her claim does not indicate a willingness to forego it.</i> This applies regardless of whether she resides in her [deceased] husband's home or in her father's home.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">This distinction is, however, relevant in Halachah 23.</i><br>If, however, she does not have possession of her <i>ketubah</i>, she is not entitled to anything, even if she makes her claim on the day her husband dies.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">For perhaps she has already received the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>, or she has waived payment of this debt.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 100:6) quotes the Rambam's rulings. The Ramah quotes rulings that allow the woman to collect the money due her even in such an instance. Nevertheless, he states that the prevailing custom is not to allow a woman to collect her claim unless she is in possession of her <i>ketubah</i>.</i> Similarly, a divorcee is not entitled even to the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i> until she produces her <i>ketubah</i>.
When does the above apply? In a place where it is customary to compose a document [recording] the <i>ketubah</i>. [Different rules apply,] however, in a place where it is not customary to compose a document [recording] the <i>ketubah</i>, and instead, [the couple] rely on the conditions established by the Jewish court.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">In the Talmudic period, there were places where it was not customary to compose a written document spelling out the marriage contract. Nevertheless, it was understood by both the husband and the wife that the financial dimensions of their marriage would be governed by the rules expressed in our Sages' requirements for the <i>ketubah</i>.</i><br>[In such a situation, the woman is entitled to] collect the essential requirement of the <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">When the husband claims to have paid the woman her due, he must prove his assertion. Otherwise, the woman's claim is accepted (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> even when she is not in possession of a document recording the <i>ketubah</i>, regardless of whether she was widowed or divorced, or whether she [continues to] reside in her husband's home or [has returned to] her father's home. She is not, however, given [anything she claims her husband promised her] in addition unless she has definite proof [of such an obligation].<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This amount is granted the woman voluntarily by her husband and is not required by Jewish law. Therefore, unless the woman has proof that the commitment was made, she is not entitled to collect anything from her husband (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).<br>The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 100) states that even if a woman can prove that her husband made a commitment for an additional amount to her at the time of the marriage, she must also prove that this commitment was not met.</i>
Until when is a widow entitled to collect the essential requirement of the <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 101) maintains that the Rambam's wording indicates that even if she remains silent, she foregoes only the essential requirement of the <i>ketubah</i>, but not the additional commitment that her husband made. However, Rav Yosef Karo dismisses this interpretation in the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and does not mention it in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 101).</i> in a place where it is not customary to compose a <i>ketubah</i>? If she [continues to reside] in her husband's home, there is no limit on the time she is granted.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">As reflected in the conclusion of this halachah, although a widow has the right to continue dwelling in her deceased husband's home, the heirs also have the right to dwell there. Since she is deriving her subsistence from them, she is ashamed to demand payment of her due from them.</i> If she [resides] in her father's home,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Or she remarries (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 101:1).</i> [she has this prerogative] for twenty-five years.<br>If, [however,] she comes to collect [the money due her because of her <i>ketubah</i>] after twenty-five years, she is not entitled to anything. [The rationale is that] had she not foregone [the money due her], she would not have remained silent for this long. Nor is she living together with the heirs, so that she could [excuse her silence,] explaining that she was embarrassed to sue them while she was living together with them in [one] home.
For this reason, if [one of] the heirs was in the habit of bringing her subsistence while she was residing in her father's home and caring for her needs, she has the prerogative of demanding [her due] even after twenty-five years have passed. The reason why she remained silent and did not present her claim is that she was ashamed [to sue] the heir.
[The following rules apply when there is a difference between the claims of a husband - or his heirs - and his wife regarding the size of the essential requirement of her <i>ketubah</i>.] She says, "I was a virgin when I married, and the essential requirement of my <i>ketubah</i> is 200 [<i>zuz</i>]." Her husband or his heirs claim, "She was not a virgin, and she is due only 100."<br>If there are witnesses who saw that the customs that people in that locale carry out when virgins are wed were carried out on her behalf - e.g., there were different types of celebrations, [she wore a] crown or a particular garment [designated for this purpose], or other rites that are performed only for the sake of virgins were performed [for her] - she is entitled to 200 [<i>zuz</i>]. If there are no witnesses to this, she is entitled to only 100 [<i>zuz</i>].<br>[In the latter instance,] if her husband is alive, she can require him to take an oath required by the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Objections to this statement are raised by the Ra'avad, Rav Moshe HaCohen and others. The claim for which the husband is required to take an oath involves a liability for which landed property is under lien. In such instances, a Torah oath is never administered. The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 96) and others, therefore, maintain that a rabbinic oath (<i>sh'vuat hesset</i>) is administered.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam is referring to an instance in his time, when, as stated in Halachot 8-9, the lien of the <i>ketubah</i> applies to movable as well as landed property. Nevertheless, the <i>Maggid Mishneh's</i> explanation is challenged by other authorities, and even the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> himself raises questions. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 96:16) mentions both opinions.</i> for he has acknowledged a portion of a claim.<br>[In cases of this nature,] testimony is accepted [from a person]<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">As in all cases of Torah law, the testimony of two witnesses is required in this instance. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that according to the Rambam, both of these witnesses may have witnessed the events under consideration when they were minors. In both the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), Rav Yosef Karo differs and states that it is acceptable if one of these witnesses observed the events as a minor, but the other must have been past majority at that time.</i> once he became an adult, who says: "I remember that when I was a child, the rites performed for virgin brides were performed on behalf of such and such."<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, a witness's testimony is not accepted unless he is past majority - not only at the time he testifies in court, but also at the time he sees the event under discussion. In this instance, however, leniency is granted, because we rely on the fact that, by and large, most women are virgins when they marry. Moreover, the obligation of the <i>ketubah</i> is a point of Rabbinic law (<i>Ketubot</i> 28a; <i>Hilchot Edut</i> 14:3).</i><br>As mentioned, all the above applies [only] in places where it is customary not to compose a document recording the <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">For if a <i>ketubah</i> was composed, the text of the <i>ketubah</i> will clarify the matter. There is, however, an instance where this ruling would be applicable in a place where it is customary to compose a <i>ketubah</i>: an instance where the woman brings witnesses who testify that her <i>ketubah</i> was lost (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i>
When a woman tells her husband, "You divorced me," her word is accepted. [The rationale is that if this were not the truth,] she would not speak so boldly to her husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The simple meaning of the Rambam's words is that if a woman makes such a statement, she is free to marry another person. As in Chapter 4, Halachah 13, the Ra'avad differs, explaining that the woman's statements are accepted only after the fact - i.e., after she has already married another person - and only insomuch as to require that other person to divorce her.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:2) quotes the Rambam's view, while the Ra'avad's position is quoted by the Ramah. The Ramah also mentions that in the present age, since brash conduct has become more widespread, this claim is no longer accepted when presented by a woman. The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 17:4 mentions that, at present, since it is customary for a record of divorces to be kept by the rabbinical court that issues them, this claim is no longer accepted. This is particularly true in the present age, when record-keeping and communication have advanced.</i><br>Accordingly, when a woman produces her <i>ketubah</i>, [even] without having a bill of divorce, and tells her husband: "You divorced me. I lost my bill of divorce. Give me [the money due me by virtue of] my <i>ketubah</i>," [her claim is accepted, and her husband] is obligated to pay her the essential requirement of the marriage contract, even though he claims that he never divorced her.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad and the Ramah differ, as above.</i> He is not, however, [obligated to] give her the additional amount he promised,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The essential requirement of the <i>ketubah</i> is an obligation imposed by our Sages, granted so that she would have the means to marry another person in the case of divorce or widowhood. Hence, since she is granted the opportunity of remarrying in this instance, she is also entitled to the money due her by virtue of the <i>ketubah</i>. The additional amount, by contrast, is not an obligation, but rather a present promised by her husband. It is self-understood that he did not make this promise to enable her to marry another man, when he does not admit that a divorce took place (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> unless she brings proof that she has been divorced, or she manifests possession of both the bill of divorce and her <i>ketubah</i>.
[In the above situation,] if [the woman's] husband said: "This is what happened. I divorced her and paid her all [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>, both the essential requirement and the additional amount. She wrote me a receipt, but I lost it" [the following rules apply]. He requires her to take an oath while holding a sacred article [that he is liable to pay her] the essential requirement [of the <i>ketubah</i>], and then he must give her [that sum].<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The authorities who free the husband of obligation in the previous halachah also free him of all liability in this instance (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 100:40; <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 100:40).</i><br>With regard to the additional amount, his word is accepted. [The rationale is that] he could have claimed that he never divorced her, and in such an instance he would not be held liable for the additional amount. [We assume that had he desired to lie, he would have used that alternative.] He is, however, required to take a rabbinic oath with regard to the additional amount.
[The following rules apply when] a woman produces a bill of divorce, but does not have her <i>ketubah</i> in her possession. If the local custom is not to compose a <i>ketubah</i>, she is entitled to collect the essential requirement of her <i>ketubah</i> by [virtue of] the bill of divorce she is holding. If, however, it is the local custom to compose a <i>ketubah</i>, she is not entitled even to the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i> until she produces her <i>ketubah</i>, as was explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Halachot 21-22.</i> Her husband must take a rabbinic oath denying her claim, and he is freed of liability.
[When] a woman produces two bills of divorce and two <i>ketubot</i>, she is entitled to collect the amount due her by virtue of both <i>ketubot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that the man divorced his wife and did not pay her the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>. Afterwards, the couple remarried, and the husband subsequently divorced her a second time, without paying her the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>.</i> If she produces two <i>ketubot</i> and one bill of divorce, she is entitled to collect only [the money due her for] one <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The laws that follow apply when the dates of both the <i>ketubot</i> precede the date of the bill of divorce, and thus it is apparent that the woman was divorced only once.</i><br>Which <i>ketubah</i> should she collect? If they are both for the same amount, the later <i>ketubah</i> negates the earlier one, and she is entitled to collect [property that was sold to others] from the date of the later [<i>ketubah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned previously, all of a husband's property is under lien to the <i>ketubah</i>. Therefore, if he sells his landed property to others and he does not possess sufficient property after the divorce to give his wife her due, she may collect that money by expropriating property that was sold. In this instance, we say that the woman waived payment of her <i>ketubah</i> originally to free from the lien property that was sold. Afterwards, her husband wrote her a second <i>ketubah</i> for the same amount.</i> If one of them is for a greater sum than the other, she may collect whichever she desires, and the other one is voided.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, we assume that the husband wrote the woman a second <i>ketubah</i> that would preempt the first one. In this instance, the woman has the choice of selecting which <i>ketubah</i> she desires - the one with the greater sum, or the one that is dated first and thus gives her greater power with regard to the expropriation of property that has been sold.</i>
[When] a woman produces two bills of divorce and one <i>ketubah</i>, she has [the right to collect] only [the amount due her by virtue of] one <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">As reflected in the continuation of the Rambam's statements, this refers to a situation in which the man divorced his wife and did not pay her the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>. Afterwards, he remarried her without composing a second <i>ketubah</i>.</i> For when a man divorces his wife and remarries her without specifying any conditions, [it can be assumed] that he remarried her with the intent that her original <i>ketubah</i> [become binding again].<br>[The following rules apply when] a woman produces a bill of divorce and a <i>ketubah</i> after the death of her husband: If the bill of divorce is dated prior to the <i>ketubah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that her husband divorced her and did not pay her the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>. Afterwards, he remarried her and composed a <i>ketubah</i>.</i> [in a place where] it is not customary to compose a <i>ketubah</i>, she is entitled to collect the essential requirement of her <i>ketubah</i> by [virtue of this] bill of divorce,<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Halachah 28.</i> and she is entitled to collect the entire sum [mentioned] in the second <i>ketubah</i>, for she acquires this sum by virtue of [her husband's] death.<br>If her <i>ketubah</i> is dated prior to the bill of divorce, she is entitled to collect [the money due her by virtue of] the <i>ketubah</i> only once. [We assume] that when he remarried her, his intent was that her original <i>ketubah</i> [become binding again].
A woman's word is accepted if she says: "My husband died," so that she [be granted permission to] remarry, as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Gerushin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 12, Halachah 15. (See also <i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 7:2.)</i> One of the conditions of the <i>ketubah</i> is that if a woman remarries after the death of her husband, she is entitled to collect the entire sum written in her <i>ketubah</i>.<br>Therefore, if she came to the court and said: "My husband died. Grant me permission to remarry," without mentioning [the collection of the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> at all, she is granted permission to remarry. [Afterwards,] she is required to take an oath,<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the oath made by all widows before collecting the money due them by virtue of their <i>ketubah</i> (<i>Maggid Mishneh; Kessef Mishneh</i>), in contrast to the opinion of the <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 100), who requires the woman to take an oath that her husband died. (See <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 17:83.)</i> and then she is given [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>.<br>If she says, "My husband died. Give me the money due me by virtue of my <i>ketubah</i>," [not only is she not granted this money,] she is not even permitted to remarry. [We assume that] she came [only] because of the matter of the <i>ketubah</i>. Our presumption is that her husband has not died. Her intent is not to remarry, but merely to collect [the money due her by virtue of] the <i>ketubah</i> during [her husband's] lifetime.<br>If she came and said: "My husband died. Grant me permission to remarry and give me [the money due me by virtue of] my <i>ketubah</i>," she is permitted to remarry and is granted [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>. The rationale is that her primary intent is remarriage. If, however, she comes and says: "My husband died. Give me [the money due me by virtue of] my <i>ketubah</i>, and grant me permission to remarry," she is permitted to remarry, but she is not granted [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This is a question that is left unresolved by the Talmud (<i>Yevamot</i> 117a). The Rambam rules that since the matter is very severe - if the woman remarries, and it is discovered that she lied, she will be prohibited to remain married to both her first or second husbands, and her children from her second husband will be considered illegitimate - and if her first husband is alive, it is likely that the fact will be discovered - in theory, the woman should be allowed to remarry. Because she mentions her <i>ketubah</i>, however, there is a doubt, and because of the doubt, the money in question is allowed to remain in the hands of the party in whose possession it is at the time the question is raised - i.e., the heirs. That doubt, however, applies only to the financial dimension of the relationship, and not to the permission to remarry (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).<br>Rabbenu Asher differs and maintains that the doubt raised by the Talmud also applies with regard to the woman's permission to remarry. Both opinions are mentioned by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 17:44), although it appears that the Rambam's approach is favored.</i> If, however, she seizes possession [of this sum], the court should not expropriate it from her possession.

Chapter 17

[The following laws apply when] a person dies after having been married to several wives. Whichever of his wives was married first has the right to collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> [before the others]. None may collect [her due] without taking an oath.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Ketubot</i> 10:4), the Rambam explains that this oath differs from the oath that all widows take before collecting from an estate, as mentioned in Chapter 16, Halachah 4, and must be taken even when the wives are not obligated to take that oath. The woman must take this oath for the other widows, stating that she did not collect any money from their husband's estate previously. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 96:16), however, states that the first widow takes an oath to the second, the second to the third, the third to the fourth, and the fourth to the heirs.</i><br>The [wives who married] last are entitled to [collect their due] only from what remains after [those who married previously collect theirs].<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This procedure is followed even if doing so prevents one of the wives from collecting all that is due her. Indeed, even if there is nothing left for her at all, this order should be followed.</i> Even the last wife [to collect] must take an oath [before] she collects what remains.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling follows the opinion of ben Nanas in the above-mentioned <i>mishnah</i>. It involves a reversal of opinion for the Rambam, who, in his Commentary on the Mishnah favored the view of the other Sages.</i><br>Similarly, when there is [also] a promissory note [owed by the husband's estate], if the promissory note was dated before [the <i>ketubot</i>], the promissory note should be collected first. If the <i>ketubot</i> were each dated before [the promissory note], the woman should collect [her due] first, and the person owed the promissory note [should collect from] the remainder.
When does the above apply? When the land from which [the wives and the creditor] desire to collect was owned by [the deceased] at the time he married the women and took the loan. For [in such a situation], the law is that whoever's document is dated first takes precedence.<br>If, however, a man married several women in succession, and borrowed money - whether before marrying the women or afterwards - and [then - i.e.,] after marrying and borrowing he purchased land - it should be divided among all of them equally, for all their liens took effect at the same time. At the time he purchased the land, each one established a lien on it. None has precedence over the others.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a situation where the property owned by the estate is sufficient to cover all the obligations. Otherwise, the creditor takes precedence over the widows, as explained in Halachot 4-5 (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i>
Similarly, if all the <i>ketubot</i> and promissory notes were dated on one day - or at a specific time, in a place where it is customary to [include] the time [of a legal document] - it should be divided among all of them equally; none has precedence over the others.<br>Under all circumstances, [if one of the creditors or one of the wives] took possession of movable property [belonging to the estate as payment for] the loan or <i>ketubah</i>, the property that they took should not be expropriated from him or her. For no creditor has precedence over another with regard to movable property.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If one of the wives or creditors did not wait for the formal deposition of the estate's property, but took possession of some of the movable property on his or her own initiative, they are allowed to retain possession. For in contrast to landed property, the ownership of movable property is not a matter of public knowledge. Hence a creditor does not know whether another creditor preceded him, and therefore no creditor is given the right to collect his due from such property.<br>As reflected in the rulings of the <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 102:2), this ruling applies only when the movable property was not acquired together with and via the acquisition of landed property (<i>kinyan agav</i>). In the latter situation, the ownership of the movable property also becomes public knowledge, and therefore the order in which the liens were established is significant.<br>The Ramah quotes the opinion of the Mordechai, who states that, in the latter instance, if one of the widows seizes possession of the property, it should be expropriated from her.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a person divorces his wife at the time he has an outstanding promissory note, and his creditor and his divorcee come to collect [their due]. If the husband owns [enough] money and land to settle the debt and the obligations stemming from the <i>ketubah</i>, the creditor should be awarded the money,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">For it was money that he gave him.</i> and his divorcee should collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> from the landed property.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For a woman relies on the fact that she will ultimately be able to collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> from the landed property in her husband's estate (<i>Ketubot</i> 86a).</i><br>If all [the husband] possesses is land that is not of sufficient value to settle both debts, and neither [his divorcee nor his creditor] has a prior claim to this land, it should be given [toward the payment of the debt owed to] the creditor. If any [land] remains [after the settlement of the debt], it should be given to the divorcee. If nothing remains, the divorcee must yield to the creditor. [The rationale is that] the creditor suffered a loss; he [lent] money [to the husband]. The woman, by contrast,did not lose anything. For more than a man desires to marry, a woman desires to be married.
Similarly, if a man dies leaving a widow and a creditor, and land to which neither of them has a prior claim, the widow must yield to the creditor, and he collects the debt owed him first.
Since the <i>geonim</i> ordained<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 16, Halachah 7.</i> that a woman and a creditor may collect their due from movable property, and as is well known, no creditors are given precedence with regard to movable property<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See the notes on Halachah 3 with regard to a <i>kinyan agav</i>.</i> [the following rules apply]. If the husband did not leave enough movable property to settle both accounts, the creditor is allowed to collect the entire debt [owed him] first. If anything remains [after the settlement of the debt] for the wife to receive [by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>, it should be given to her. If nothing remains, the wife must yield.
[The following rule applies when] <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> were recorded in a woman's <i>ketubah</i> and she claims that they were lost or taken by her husband. With regard to <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>, a woman is regarded like any other creditor.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> refers to property that the woman brought to the household, for which the husband obligated himself to pay a fixed value. In this instance, since the woman, like a creditor, gave up something of value, she is considered on a higher level of precedence than usual.</i><br>Therefore, she is required to take an oath that she did not take possession of them, give them away or forego the obligation [to her husband]. Afterwards, she receives a share in the estate together with the other creditors.
When a person who has many wives and who dies or divorces them when none of them has a claim of higher priority to his property than the others, and his holdings are not of sufficient value to pay them each the money due them by virtue of their <i>ketubot</i>, how are his holdings divided? If his holdings are sufficiently valuable to provide only the wife with the <i>ketubah</i> of the least value, or if they are less valuable than that, all of his wives divide [his holdings] equally.<br>If his holdings are more valuable than that, they should be divided equally to provide the wife with [the money due her by virtue of] the <i>ketubah</i> of the least value. Afterwards, the remainder is divided among the remaining wives according to the same pattern.<br>What is implied? [To explain by example:] A man was married to four wives. The <i>ketubah</i> of the first was for 400 [<i>zuz</i>], that of the second for 300, that of the third for 200, and that of the forth for 100. The total sum is thus 1000 [<i>zuz</i>]. [The following rules apply] if he divorces all of them or dies. If his holdings are worth 400 [<i>zuz</i>] or less, they divide his holdings equally, and each receives 100 or less. If his holdings are worth 800 [it would be improper to divide them equally]. For if they were divided equally, the fourth wife would receive 200 [<i>zuz</i>], and [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> is only 100.<br>What is done instead? 400 [<i>zuz</i>] are set aside and divided equally, each receiving 100. Thus, the fourth wife has received [the full amount due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> and she withdraws [from the suit]. Thus, 400 [<i>zuz</i>] are left for three wives, each of whom has already received 100 <i>zuz</i>]. If the 400 were divided equally among the three of them [it would be unfair]. For the third wife would receive 233 and [the amount due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> was only 200. Therefore, 300 [<i>zuz</i>] are separated from the 400, and these are divided equally among the three. Thus, the third wife receives her 200 and withdraws [from the suit]. There remain two wives and 100 <i>[zuz]</i>. This sum is divided equally between the first and second wife. Thus, the first and second wife each received 250 <i>zuz</i>; the third wife received 200; and the fourth wife, 100. This pattern of allocation is followed even when there are 100 [wives].<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This pattern is also followed in the allocation of a person's holdings when they are not sufficient to pay the debts he owes, as explained in <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i>, Chapter 20. The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam's approach and follows the minority view that the Rambam cites in that source, which maintains that the money should be divided proportionately. The Rambam's view is followed by most other <i>Rishonim</i> (Rashi, Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi, the Rashba and Rabbenu Asher) and is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 96:18).</i>
A person who guarantees the value of a woman's <i>ketubah</i> is not obligated to pay [her the money due her in the event that her husband's holdings are not sufficient if he dies or divorces her]. [This applies] even when he affirmed his commitment with a contractual act.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 174b explains that the guarantor is not serious about his commitment. He feels that the couple needs only a small push to get married, and that is his intent, rather than making a serious financial commitment. Even a contractual act, which in other contexts serves as an indication of seriousness of purpose, is not sufficient in this instance.<br>The Ra'avad and the <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 102) differ, and maintain that if a guarantor affirms his commitment with a contractual act, he is liable. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 102:6) quotes the Rambam's view, while the Ramah cites that of the Ra'avad.</i> [The rationale is that] his [intent is to] perform a mitzvah,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he brought about the marriage between the couple.</i> and he did not cause the woman to lose anything.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">As can be deduced from the Rambam's wording, the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that if a person guarantees a woman's <i>nedunyah</i> (the goods she brought to the household), his commitment is binding. For in this instance, the woman did give up something of value.</i><br>If, however, a person guarantees the <i>ketubah</i> of his son and affirms his commitment with a contractual act,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance as well, the Ra'avad and the <i>Tur</i> differ and hold the father liable, even when he did not affirm his commitment with a contractual act. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 102:6) follows the Rambam's view, while the Ramah cites that of the Ra'avad.</i> he is obligated to pay. For a father will make a binding commitment on behalf of his son and decide to obligate himself.<br>A person who underwrites a <i>ketubah</i>, by contrast, is obligated to pay, even though he did not affirm his commitment with a contractual act. What is meant by a person who underwrites a <i>ketubah</i>? One who tells a woman: "Marry this man. I will give [the money for] this <i>ketubah</i>." If, however, he says: "I will guarantee this <i>ketubah</i>," "I will pay this <i>ketubah</i>," "I am obligated for it" or the like, he is not liable unless he is the father [of the groom].<br>When a person divorces a wife [whose <i>ketubah</i> has been underwritten in the above fashion], he must first take a vow<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Moreover, this vow must be taken <i>al da'at rabbim</i>, based on the judgement of the public, and it thus cannot be nullified (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 102:7).</i> that she is forbidden to derive benefit from him. Only then may she collect her <i>ketubah</i> from the underwriter or the [husband's] father, if he guaranteed it. [This precaution was instituted,] lest the husband remarry her,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., after the woman collected the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> from him.</i> and thus the two will [have acquired] the property of [the underwriter] through subterfuge.
Similarly, a person who consecrates his property and then divorces his wife must take a vow that she is forbidden to derive benefit from him. Only then may she collect [the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>] from the person who redeems the property from the Temple treasury.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The woman may not collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> from the property while it is the possession of the Temple treasury. After it is redeemed, however, she may collect her due from the property. The person who redeems the property must, however, be advised that the property is on lien to a woman's <i>ketubah</i>. (See <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 18:7; <i>Hilchot Arachin VaCharamin</i> 7:14-15.)</i> [This precaution was instituted,] lest the two attempt to deceive the Temple treasury.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the woman will collect her husband's property because it is on lien on her <i>ketubah</i>. Afterwards, she will remarry her husband, and he will be able to use his property, because of his rights as the woman's husband.</i><br>When, however, a person divorces his wife, and she comes to collect [the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>] from the [property that was sold to] purchasers, he is not required to take a vow that she is forbidden to derive benefit from him. Instead, she must take the oath required of her, and then she [is entitled to] collect [her due]. If afterwards she desires, she may return to her husband. For the purchasers know that the property was under lien to the <i>ketubah</i> of a woman, and they caused themselves the loss by taking property that was under such a lien.
When a husband sold his property, and afterwards the woman agreed to [her husband's] act and wrote the purchaser: "I have no claim against you," she may, nevertheless, collect [the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> by expropriating this property].<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The sale is valid, however, until the woman seeks to claim the property. If, by contrast, the husband sells property that belonged to the woman, or property from which she was designated to collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>, the sale is nullified immediately (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). (See <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 30:3.)</i> [This applies] even when she affirmed [her commitment] with a contractual act.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Note the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 90:17), who states that if the woman received money from the purchaser, her commitment is binding.</i> [The rationale is] that she wrote this [statement to the purchaser] only so that there will not be strife between her and her husband. She can [therefore excuse herself,] saying: "I was [merely intending] to please my husband."<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the woman is saying that her commitment was not sincere and was made only to satisfy her husband.</i><br>[A different rule applies, however, when the purchaser] enters into an agreement with the woman that she foregoes her lien on this property [before purchasing it from her husband]. If this agreement is affirmed with a contractual act, and afterwards the husband sells the property [to him], [the woman is not entitled to] expropriate this property.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since she entered into the agreement with the purchaser before her husband made the sale, she cannot excuse herself by saying that she made her statements only to please her husband.</i><br>Similarly, [a woman is not entitled to expropriate property sold by her husband in the following circumstance]. Her husband sold a property [on a previous occasion, and at that time] asked his wife to write the purchaser, "I have no claim to this property," and the woman refused, causing the sale to be nullified.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> questions the reason for this phrase. When this law is cited in the <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 90:17), this phrase is omitted. Nevertheless, based on the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Ketubot</i> 10:5), the <i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i> maintains that, according to the Rambam, a sale must have been nullified in order for the woman's commitment to be binding later on.</i> [If,] afterwards, the husband sells [property] - whether the same field he had sold previously or another field - to another person, and after the husband's sale the woman agreed, [made a commitment] that she has no claim to this field and affirmed it with a contractual act, she may not expropriate it. For she cannot say, "I did this [merely] to please my husband," since on the previous occasion, when she did not want [to waive her rights], she did not follow her husband's desires.
[The above ruling is also relevant in the following situation.] A man had two wives. He sold a field, and the purchaser had entered into a contractual act with one of [the husband's] wives, waiving her lien to this field in a manner in which the agreement was effective and the woman no longer had the privilege of claiming, "I did this [merely] to please my husband." Afterwards, the husband died or divorced both his wives.<br>The second wife may expropriate the property from the purchaser, for she did not enter into any agreement with him. The first wife may then expropriate [the property] from the second wife, for she had a prior claim to it, and she waived her lien only with regard to the purchaser [and not with regard to anyone else]. When the property comes into the possession of the first [wife], the purchaser may expropriate it from her, since she made an agreement with him. [The second wife can then expropriate it from the purchaser,] and the cycle continues until they reach a compromise among themselves.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">A three-way compromise would obviously be most desirable. Nevertheless, any compromise between two of the three parties that causes one to renounce his right to expropriate the property is sufficient to stop the cycle (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 100:26).</i>
[In the event of her husband's death,] a widow - regardless of whether she was widowed from <i>erusin</i> or <i>nisu'in</i> - may take the oath [required of her], sell land belonging to her husband and collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>. [The sale may be carried out] in a court of expert judges, or in a court whose judges are not expert,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages understood that the necessity to pursue judicial proceedings is a cause of hardship and embarrassment for women. They felt that rather than subject his wife to such distress, any husband would willingly grant her the right to collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> by selling his property without appearing in court (<i>Ketubot</i> 97b).<br>Therefore, rather than require her to take the matter to a formal court, they enabled her to resolve the issue by having the property evaluated by three acquaintances who possess the qualities mentioned above. Although these men would not be considered capable of participating in an ordinary court, an exception was made in this case. If, however, the widow has already remarried, she is required to undergo the ordinary judicial procedure.</i> provided it consists of three trustworthy men who are knowledgeable with regard to the evaluation of land. The responsibility for the sale falls on the estate belonging to the heirs.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., should the property be expropriated by a creditor of the deceased, his heirs must reimburse the purchaser.</i> A divorcee, by contrast, may sell [her ex-husband's property] only in a court of expert judges.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to a divorcee, by contrast, our Sages (<i>op. cit.</i>) felt that her ex-husband would not be disturbed by her being subjected to hardship when this is necessary to protect his own interests.<br>Although there are <i>Rishonim</i> who maintain that the provision made for a widow also applies to a divorcee, the Rambam's ruling is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 103:3).</i><br>Whenever a woman has property sold in court, she must have it sold after a public announcement has been made. In the laws of loans,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 12:8,10, which explains that public announcements that a property will be sold are made daily for thirty days (or on Mondays and Thursdays, for a period of sixty days).</i> the guidelines for the sale [of property] will be explained. When, by contrast, a woman sells property without the participation of the court,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., without the participation of a formal court.</i> a public announcement [of the sale] need not be made. It is, nevertheless, necessary [to consult] with three trustworthy men who are knowledgeable with regard to the evaluation [of property].
[The following rules apply when] a widow sells [her husband's] landed property privately in order to collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>:<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., without even the participation of the three acquaintances mentioned in the previous halachah.</i> If she sold the property at its proper value, the sale is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Although the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 103:1) appears to favor the Rambam's view, it does mention other opinions that differ.</i> [All that is necessary is for] her to take the oath required of widows after the sale.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">It would appear that the Rambam requires her merely to take the oath required of all widows before collecting the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>.
