In this instance, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 103:4) requires the woman to take an additional oath, stating that she did not sell the property for less than its worth.</i><br>The above applies when she sells the property to another individual. If she takes it as her own after evaluating it, her act is of no significance.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 103:11 state that, according to the Rambam, if the woman has the property evaluated by three trustworthy men who are knowledgeable with regard to the value of property, she is allowed to take the property as her own. Other opinions differ and maintain that this is possible only when the property is evaluated by a proper Rabbinical court.</i> [This applies even when] she had announced the sale of the property [and received no better offer].
[In the above situation,] if the woman's <i>ketubah</i> was for 200 [<i>zuz</i>], and she sold [property] that was worth 100 [<i>zuz</i>] for 200,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">When selling the property, the woman is considered to be the agent of the heirs, and the profit belongs to them and not to her (<i>Ketubot</i> 98b).</i> or property that was worth 200 for 100,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, the woman must accept the loss herself, because she took property that was worth the full value of her <i>ketubah</i>.</i> she has received the value of her <i>ketubah</i> and is no longer owed anything. She must, however, take the oath required of a widow.<br>If her <i>ketubah</i> was for 100 [<i>zuz</i>] and she sold [property] worth 101 [<i>zuz</i>] for 100, the sale is nullified.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">For she has no right to sell any property that is worth more than her <i>ketubah</i>.</i> [This applies] even if she says, "I will [accept the loss and] return the [outstanding] <i>dinar</i> to the heirs."
If her <i>ketubah</i> was for 400 <i>zuz</i> and she sold [four pieces of property], three that were each worth 100 [<i>zuz</i>] for 100 [<i>zuz</i>] each, and one that was worth 101 <i>zuz</i> for 100 [<i>zuz</i> - the final sale is nullified, but the [first three] are all binding.
A woman has the privilege of selling [the rights to] her <i>ketubah</i> or giving [them] as a present.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The requirement of a <i>ketubah</i> was instituted so that the husband will not consider divorce a light matter, because of the severity of the financial obligation that will result. This remains true even if the woman does not receive the money herself.</i> If her husband dies or divorces her, [the purchaser or the recipient] is entitled to come and collect [the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">As reflected in the continuation of the Rambam's words, the woman must first take the oaths required of her as if she herself were to collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>.</i> If she dies in the lifetime of her husband or [after his death, but] before she takes the oath [required of widows], he is not entitled to anything.
Although a woman sold [the rights to] a portion of her <i>ketubah</i>, used them as security [for a loan] or gave them as a present, she may sell landed property belonging to her husband and collect the remainder of [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>. [This sale may be carried out] in a court of three expert judges or through three trustworthy men.<br>[A woman] may sell [portions of her husband's property] many times. [These sales may be carried out] in a court of three expert judges or through three trustworthy men who are knowledgeable with regard to the evaluation of property.
When a woman sells [the rights to] her <i>ketubah</i> - whether to another person or to her husband - she does not forfeit the other privileges of her <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., her rights to support, medical attention and the like.</i> [As such,] if she has a son, [and she dies before her husband does,] he inherits the worth of her <i>ketubah</i> - [although it] was sold from his father's estate - in addition to his share [in the estate, as will be explained].<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 19, Halachah 2.</i><br>If, by contrast, a woman waives her <i>ketubah</i> in favor of her husband, she forfeits all the privileges associated with her <i>ketubah</i>. [Her husband] is not required to provide her even with her subsistence.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's wording, it appears that the woman is not entitled to her subsistence even during her husband's lifetime, while they remain married. (Note Chapter 10, Halachah 10, which states that if a woman waives her <i>ketubah</i> in favor of her husband, he must write her a new <i>ketubah</i>.) The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 93:9) quotes the opinion that even during the husband's lifetime, he is not required to support his wife, but appears to favor the view of other <i>Rishonim</i> (Rashi, the Ramban and the Rashba), who grant a woman the right to support during her husband's lifetime in such a situation.<br>See also Chapter 19, Halachah 12, which discusses another consequence of a woman's waiver of her <i>ketubah</i> in favor of her husband.</i><br>The waiver of a <i>ketubah</i> [in favor of the woman's husband] need not [be affirmed by] a contractual act nor [be observed by] witnesses,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to their role with regard to marriage and divorce, in financial matters witnesses are necessary only to confirm what happened. Their presence does not make a transaction or a commitment binding, nor hinder it from becoming so. (See <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 5:9.)</i> just as the forfeiture [of any obligations] does not require affirmation by] a contractual act nor [the observation of] witnesses. Through one's words alone [the forfeiture is binding], provided the statement is made seriously, [in a manner that] can be relied upon, rather than facetiously, as a joke, or rhetorically.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 5:11-13.</i>

Chapter 18

A widow is entitled to receive support from the estate [inherited by her husband's] heirs as long as she remains a widow, unless she collects [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Gittin</i> 35a) states that as long as the widow does not contemplate remarriage, she is showing honor to her deceased husband, and therefore our Sages ordained that she should receive her subsistence from his estate. However, by demanding payment of the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>, she indicates that she is seeking to remarry. From that time onward, her deceased husband's estate is no longer obligated to support her.<br>The option whether to continue receiving her subsistence or to demand payment of the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> is hers. The heirs cannot compel her to receive the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> and cease giving her support (<i>Ketubot</i> 95b; <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> From the time she demands payment for her <i>ketubah</i> in court, however, she is no longer entitled to receive her subsistence.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 93:13 explains that if the woman asks for payment of the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>, and the heirs refuse to pay her or are unable to do so, she is still entitled to support.</i><br>Similarly, if she sold [the rights to] her entire <i>ketubah</i>, gave them as security [for a loan] or made her <i>ketubah</i> an <i>ipotiki</i> for another person - i.e., she told him "Collect your debt from here" - she is not entitled to receive her subsistence from the heirs.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In all these instances, it is considered as if she has already collected the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>.</i> [The above applies] whether these exchanges were made in a court of expert judges or outside a court, or whether they were made in her husband's lifetime or after his death.<br>If, however, she sold [the rights to] only a portion of her <i>ketubah</i>, she is entitled to receive her subsistence.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, however, the heirs have the right to pay her the remainder of the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>, and thus prevent her from continuing to collect her subsistence from the estate. If this provision were not granted, every widow would collect all the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> except for the final <i>p'rutah</i>, and continue to receive support (Rabbenu Asher, quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 93:10.).</i> When a widow becomes consecrated<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In the present age, this law applies even when the woman has merely become engaged to a new husband (<i>Beit Yosef, Even HaEzer</i> 93, as quoted by the Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 93:7).</i> [to a new husband], she forfeits [her rights to receive] subsistence [from her deceased husband's estate].<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Even if she has not collected the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>.</i>
Just as the woman receives her subsistence from her husband's estate after his death, so, too, is she granted a wardrobe, household utensils and [the right to continue] living in the dwelling she lived in during her husband's lifetime.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Nevertheless, the dwelling becomes the property of the heirs, and they are also entitled to live there. The widow is, however, granted a place of dignity in the household (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>; Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 94:1).</i> She may continue to make use of the pillows, spreads, servants and maidservants that she made use of during her husband's lifetime.<br>If the dwelling falls, the heirs are not required to rebuild it.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Nor are they required to give her a room in it if they rebuild it themselves. Instead, they may rent her a different dwelling, as stated in the following halachah.</i> [Even] if the widow asked, "Allow me to rebuild it at my own expense," she is not granted this option. Similarly, she may not repair it, nor have the walls sealed [and painted].<br>She must [continue to] dwell in it in the condition it [was in her husband's passing], or she must leave [and find other accommodations]. Should the heirs sell the dwelling in which a widow is living, their deed is of no consequence.
If the dwelling [in which she was living fell] or her husband had been renting a dwelling, [the estate must] provide her with a dwelling appropriate to her social standing. Similarly, her subsistence and the wardrobe given her are granted according to her social standing.<br>If her husband's social standing exceeded her own, she is granted the above according to his social standing. For a woman's [social standing] ascends according to [her husband's] social standing, but does not descend [according to his]. [This applies] even after his death.
[The widow is given her subsistence as a member of] the household at large. What is the intent of [the latter term]? When five people who would each require a <i>kav</i> of food when they eat alone [live] in the same house and eat together [their needs are reduced]. Four <i>kabbim</i> will be sufficient for them. The same applies with regard to other necessary household [supplies].<br>Therefore, if a widow says: "I will not leave my father's house. Ascertain the amount of support I deserve for my subsistence and give it to me there," the heirs have the right to tell her: "If you desire to dwell with us, you will receive [a full measure of] support. If not, we will give you only your share as a member of the household at large."<br>If she explains [that she desires not to live with them] because she is young, and they are young [and the situation would be immodest, her claim is accepted]. [The heirs are required] to provide her with support sufficient for her as she lives alone, while she lives in her father's home.<br>[Any money] remaining from [the funds granted for] the support of a widow or from her wardrobe belongs to the heirs.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam and maintains that these funds are granted to the widow, but the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 95:5) follows the Rambam's ruling.</i>
[The following laws apply when] a widow becomes sick. If she requires medical treatment that is of an undefined nature, it is considered as support for her subsistence, and the heirs must provide her with it.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The heirs may, however, fix a price with the physician for the widow's treatment, and then she becomes responsible for the financial burden (<i>Ketubot</i> 52b; <i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 79:2).</i> If, however, she requires medical treatment of a limited nature, the treatment [should be paid for by deducting it] from [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>.<br>If she is taken captive, the heirs are not required to redeem her. [This applies] even if she is a <i>yevamah</i> [and it is a mitzvah for her late husband's brother to marry her]. [Indeed,] even when she was taken captive during her husband's lifetime [and he was thus obligated to redeem her], if he dies while she is in captivity, there is no obligation to redeem her from his estate. Instead, she must be redeemed from her private funds, or she must collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> and redeem herself.
When a widow dies, her late husband's heirs are responsible for her burial. If, however, she had already taken the oath required of a widow [before collecting the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>], her heirs inherit her <i>ketubah</i>, and they are required to bury her, and not her late husband's heirs.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is, as stated in Chapter 12, Halachah 4, that the burial of the woman was granted her in return for the husband's right to inherit her <i>ketubah</i>. If her heirs can collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>, they are required to bury her. If not, since the money for her <i>ketubah</i> remains within the husband's estate, his heirs are responsible for her burial.<br>Although this is the Rambam's view, the Ra'avad and Rabbenu Nissim do not accept it. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 89:4) mentions the Rambam's view and states that it was not accepted by the other authorities.</i><br>[Her late husband's] heirs are entitled to the income [from the work] of the widow. If the heirs tell the widow, "Take the income you generate in exchange for [receiving] your subsistence," their words are of no substance. If, however, she desires such an arrangement, she is given this prerogative.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The same laws apply with regard to her husband during his lifetime, as stated in Chapter 12, Halachah 4.</i>
All the household tasks that a wife performs on behalf of her husband, a widow must perform on behalf of his heirs, with the exception of pouring them drinks, making their beds and washing their face, hands and feet.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">These tasks are acts of endearment, appropriate only for a wife to her husband.</i>
An ownerless article discovered by a widow and the benefit that accrues from the property that the woman brought to her husband's household belong to the woman herself; the heirs [to her husband's estate] have no right to them at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Although a husband is granted these rights (Chapter 12, Halachah 3), his heirs are not. The husband is granted the rights to the objects his wife finds so that strife will not arise between them. That rationale is not considered with regard to his heirs (<i>Ketubot</i> 96a).<br>With regard to the rights to her property: as mentioned in Chapter 12, Halachah 4, our Sages associated the rights to a woman's property with her redemption from captivity. Since the heirs are not obligated to redeem her, they are not entitled to this privilege.</i>
The property that [a woman brought to the household as] her <i>nedunyah</i> may be taken by the woman without her having to take an oath.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The property that a woman brings to her household belongs to her. Her husband has merely the right to derive benefit from it; he is not the owner. With regard to this property, she is treated like any of the other creditors of the estate, and no oath is required of her.</i> The heirs to her husband's estate have no claim with regard to it, except if the <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> have increased in value during her husband's lifetime. [In this instance,] the increase belongs to the husband<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nichsei tzon barzel</i> is property that the husband has had evaluated, and it is the value of the article for which he obligates himself or his estate. Nevertheless, if the property itself exists, it is given to the woman. If the property has increased in value, however, the husband - and therefore his heirs - are entitled to the increase.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 96:1) state that this law refers only in an instance where the property that the woman brought to the household - or an article exchanged for it - is still intact. Otherwise, she is required to take an oath before collecting the money paid in lieu of the property.</i> [and is given to his heirs].<br>[Even] if a widow dies without taking the oath [required of her], her heirs inherit her <i>nedunyah</i>, even if it is <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>. If, however, it has increased in value, the increase must go to her husband's heirs.
When a woman seizes movable property [belonging to her husband's estate, so that she can sell it and use the money] for her subsistence, the property should not be removed from her possession.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Although the movable property in her husband's estate is not under lien for her subsistence, it is not taken away from her if she takes possession of it. As the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> emphasizes, the above applies with regard to the Talmudic era. As stated in the following halachah, it is customary at present to consider movable property as under lien to all a husband's obligations.<br>There are some <i>Rishonim</i> who differ with the Rambam and equate the provisions for the widow's subsistence with the collection of the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>. (See the following halachah.) The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 93:20) follows the Rambam's view.</i> [This applies regardless of] whether she took possession of the movable property during her husband's lifetime or afterwards. Even if she takes possession of a talent of gold<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a sum that will last far longer than thirty days - the length of time for which the court sells property to provide her with her subsistence - or perhaps more than the worth of the woman's entire <i>ketubah</i>.</i> [it is not removed from her possession].<br>Instead, the court documents what she has taken into her possession and defines the amount she should be given for her subsistence. Calculations are made, and she is allowed to derive her subsistence from [the property] in her possession until she dies or until she is no longer entitled to support for her subsistence. [At that time,] the heirs are granted the remainder.
Similarly, if she took possession of movable property during her husband's lifetime [to provide] for [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>, she may collect [the money due her] from this [property after he dies]. If, however, she took possession of it after her husband's death [to provide] for [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>, she may not collect [her due] from it.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Instead, it must be returned to the heirs.<br><i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Ketubot</i> 96a) explains the distinction between a woman's taking possession of movable property to collect for her subsistence and the collection of the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> as follows. Our Sages ordained that a woman may collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> from property that had belonged to her husband and was sold. Therefore, it is likely that the woman will ultimately receive her due. As such, she is required to return the movable property. With regard to her subsistence, however, no such provision was made. Hence, she is given an alternative, to take possession of movable property.<br>As explained in the following note, according to the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and others this law describes the practices of the Talmudic age and not those of the present era.</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>K'nesset HaGedolah</i> explains that, contrary to the standard published texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, Halachah 12 begins here. This is not a continuation of the previous halachah, because there is a difference with regard to the laws governing movable property between the practices of the Talmudic age and those of the present era.</i>The <i>geonim</i> ordained that a woman may collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> and every obligation due her as a stipulation of her <i>ketubah</i> from the movable property [in her husband's estate]. Based on this [provision], a woman may receive her subsistence from [the sale of] movable property [from her husband's estate].<br>Nevertheless, if her husband left movable property and she did not take possession of it, the heirs take possession of it, and they must provide her with her subsistence. She has no right to prevent them from taking possession, by saying: "Have the movable property held in the court [so that] I can derive my subsistence from it, lest it become depleted,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">For if the heirs sell it, the woman has no claim to the proceeds of the sale, nor may she expropriate the property from the purchasers. Similarly, if the heirs destroy the movable property, she has no claim against them. From an ethical perspective, however, the heirs are enjoined not to sell this movable property.</i> and I will have no means of support." Even if an explicit stipulation was made [by her husband at the time her <i>ketubah</i> was composed] that she could derive her subsistence from this movable property, she cannot prevent [the heirs] from taking possession of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 93:36 states that if a specific clause was included in the <i>ketubah</i> regarding this matter, although the widow cannot nullify the sale she has a right to receive her subsistence from its proceeds.</i> This is the ruling that is universally followed in all courts.
If, however, her husband left landed property, she has the right to prevent the heirs from selling it. If they do sell it, however, she does not have the right to expropriate [the property] from the purchasers. A widow and a man's daughters may derive their subsistence only from the property that remains in his estate. [In this regard, they have no claim to property that was sold.]<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The Rashba states that if a clause was added to the <i>ketubah</i> specifically stating that the woman has the right to collect her subsistence from movable property after her husband's death, then she is allowed to expropriate the landed property from the purchasers (<i>Maggid Mishneh; Ramah, Even HaEzer</i> 93:21).</i>
If the deceased left many wives, they all have equal rights to receive their subsistence. [This applies] even when he married them one after the other. For the concept of a prior claim does not exist with regard to a claim for support.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on manuscripts and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The standard printed texts substitute "movable property" for "claim for support." Apparently, this version reached the Ra'avad who objects, and states - as is the halachah - that the principle applies with regard to landed property as well.</i>
[The following rules apply with regard to] a widow who has an obligation to marry a <i>yavam</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., her husband died childless, and he had a brother who is commanded to marry his widow.</i> During the first three months,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This time period is granted in order to determine whether the woman was made pregnant by her husband before he died. If three months pass without pregnancy becoming noticeable, we can assume that a child was not conceived.</i> she derives her subsistence from her deceased husband's estate.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Until she gives birth or miscarries, she is not entitled to remarry, lest she become bound by the obligation of <i>yibbum</i>. Since it is because of her husband that she may not remarry, his estate is required to provide for her (Rashi, <i>Yevamot</i> 41b).</i> If it can be determined that she is pregnant, or if it was known that she was pregnant when her husband died, she continues to derive her support [from his estate] until she gives birth. If she bears a viable child, she may continue to derive her subsistence throughout her widowhood as other women do.<br>If after three months have passed, it is [either] not evident that she is pregnant or she miscarries, she is not entitled to support from either her husband's estate or from her <i>yavam</i>. Instead, she must file a suit against her <i>yavam</i> either to marry her or [to free her of her obligation through] <i>chalitzah</i>.
If she filed a suit against her <i>yavam</i> either to marry her or [to free her of her obligation through] <i>chalitzah</i>, he appeared in court and then fled or became ill, or if the <i>yavam</i> lives overseas,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that the latter two clauses - that the <i>yavam</i> became sick or that he lived overseas - apply also only if the <i>yavam</i> had previously appeared in court. If, however, he has never appeared in court, he is not under any obligation.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 160:1) follows the opinion of Rabbenu Asher, who states that the <i>yavam</i> is obligated to support her in the latter instances only when he consented to marry her. If he desired to perform <i>chalitzah</i>, he is under no obligation to her.</i> the woman is entitled to derive her support from the property of the <i>yavam</i> without taking any oath at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">There is no need for her to take an oath that the <i>yavam</i> had not given her property. For since they have not established a relationship, such suspicions are unfounded (<i>Ketubot</i> 107b).</i>
If the <i>yavam</i> she was obligated to marry is a minor,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Who should not perform the mitzvah of <i>yibbum</i> until he attains majority.</i> she is not entitled to receive her support from him until he comes of age and resembles other <i>yevamim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is forbidden to marry her, he is not required to support her. Nor is she entitled to support from her husband's estate. <i>Yevamot</i> 41b says that it is as if she is penalized from heaven.</i>
Should a person designate a portion of land to be used for support of his wife after his death, by saying: "This particular place will be for [my wife's] support,"<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is referring to statements made by a dying man with regard to the allocation of his property. If these statements are observed by witnesses, they are binding. This practice, referred to as a <i>matnat sh'chiv me'ra</i> (the oral will of a dying man) is described in <i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 8:2).</i> he has granted her additional rights with regard to her support.<br>If the income [from this land] is less than the support due her, she is entitled [to collect] the remainder from the other portions of his estate. If the income [from those portions of land] is more than the support due her, she is entitled to the entire amount.<br>If, however, he told her, "Your support will come from this particular place," and she remained silent,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since it is possible that the woman will suffer a loss, she has the right to protest. If, however, she remained silent, we assume that she accepted her husband's decision.</i> her sole source of support is the income from that particular place. [Her husband] has specificied [the source for] her support.
There are those who have ruled that when a widow comes to the court to ask for support she should be allotted support without requiring her to take an oath.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The reference is to Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi, who ruled this way in a responsum. His opinion is favored also by the Ra'avad, the Ramban, the Rashba and Rabbenu Asher. <i>Ketubot</i> 105a states that the woman should take an oath "at the end and not at the beginning." They explain that this refers to a woman whose husband has died. The woman should take the oath when she comes to collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>, and not when she comes asking for support. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 93:19) appears to favor this view, and the Ramah states that it should be followed.</i> This ruling should not be followed; they have misunderstood [the situation, erroneously associating it with that of] a woman whose husband left on an overseas journey.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachah 16.</i><br>My teachers<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yosef Migash.</i> ruled that she should not be allotted support until she takes an oath in court.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">They interpret <i>Ketubot</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) to be referring to a woman whose husband traveled overseas. She should not take an oath at the outset - i.e., when she comes to collect her subsistence - but rather at the end, if her husband comes and requires this of her. See Chapter 12, Halachah 21.<br>The dissenting authorities refute this interpretation, explaining that it is far more reasonable to require an oath of a woman when her husband is alive than after his death, for after his death it is very likely that the woman will soon take an oath to collect her <i>ketubah</i>.</i> For she is coming to collect from property in the possession of heirs, and anyone who collects property in the possession of heirs may do so only after an oath has been taken. My own conception [also] follows [this approach], and it is proper to rule accordingly.
When a woman comes to the court to collect support for her subsistence, an oath is administered to her at the outset. The property is then sold without being publicized, and an allotment is made for her subsistence.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the sale of property so that the woman can collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> (Chapter 17, Halachah 13), in this instance the sale need not be publicized. The rationale is that the woman needs the money for her subsistence immediately and should not be required to wait.</i><br>Similarly, she is entitled to sell property for her subsistence without involving a court of expert judges; three trustworthy individuals are sufficient, and the sale need not be publicized. Similarly, if she sells property by herself for its appropriate value to provide for her subsistence, the sale is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Chanan'el and the Ramban differ with the Rambam on this point. Although their opinion is also mentioned by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 93:25), it appears that the Rambam's opinion is favored.</i> When the heirs come and require her to take an oath, she must take the oath.
How much property is sold to provide for her subsistence? Enough to provide for her support for six months,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In this manner, a large amount of property is sold. If a smaller amount were sold, the parcel of land would be too small to fetch a proper price.</i> but not for longer than that. The sale is made on the condition that the purchaser give the widow an allotment for food every thirty days.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the purchaser gives the widow only enough money to support herself for thirty days at a time. The rationale is that if she remarries or seeks to collect her <i>ketubah</i>, she is no longer entitled to receive support for her subsistence. Since there is the possibility that this will happen at any given time, she is given support for only a limited period of time. In the event that she remarries, the remainder of the money left from the sale is given to the heirs (Rashi, <i>Ketubot</i> 97a).</i> Afterwards, another parcel of property is sold for another six months.<br>The property should continue to be sold in this manner until all that remains from the estate is [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>. She should collect this sum and complete her dealings with the court.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that this is simply proper advice for the woman. For she can sell all the land necessary to provide her with the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> at one time, while to collect her subsistence she must sell the land in small parcels. If she chooses, however, she may take the latter alternative.</i>
When the court allots a widow support for her subsistence, they do not reckon the money she earns until the heirs come and demand it. [If such a demand is made,] and the woman has earned money, they are entitled to it. If not, they have no further claim against her.<br>I maintain [however] that if the heirs are below majority, the court should make a reckoning with the widow with regard to [her income].<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Since the heirs are orphans, the court is obligated to look after their interests. Therefore, it is obligated to ensure that the woman's earnings are given to them.</i> Just as she is allotted a subsistence, the court declares that her income [should be given to the orphans].
When a widow does not manifest possession of her <i>ketubah</i>, she is not granted money for her subsistence. [The rationale is that] perhaps she waived her <i>ketubah</i> [in favor of her husband] or sold it or gave it as security [for a loan].<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In all these cases, the widow is no longer entitled to receive support from her deceased's husband's estate, as stated in Chapter 12, Halachah 18.</i><br>Even when the heir[s] do not issue such a claim against her, the court makes this claim on their behalf and tells her: "Bring your <i>ketubah</i>, take the required oath and collect [the money for] your subsistence." [This law applies] unless it is not customary [in a particular locale] to compose a document recording the <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, since the probability is that the woman would not have been given a document recording her <i>ketubah</i>, the fact that she does not have such a document in her possession is not considered detrimental to her position.</i>
[The following laws apply when] a woman and her husband traveled overseas, and she returned, claiming [her husband] died. If she desires, she is entitled to receive her subsistence from her husband's estate, as are other widows. If she desires, she may collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 16, Halachah 31.</i><br>If she claims, "My husband divorced me," her word is not accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 12:1.</i> She is, however, entitled to derive her subsistence from his estate until she receives a sum equal to [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>. [The rationale is] that if she is still his wife, she is entitled to receive her subsistence [from his holdings]. If he divorced her, she is entitled to receive [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>, [provided] she manifests possession of her <i>ketubah</i>. Therefore, she may collect the support for her subsistence until she receives [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>. [From this point on,] she has completed her dealings with the court.
[The following laws apply when] there is doubt whether a woman was divorced, and her husband died [afterwards]. She is not entitled to receive her subsistence from his estate, for property cannot be expropriated from an heir on the basis of a doubtful claim.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since her status is questionable, she is not entitled to support. For this is granted only to a man's wife and not to his divorcee.</i> During her husband's lifetime, by contrast, she is entitled to her subsistence until she is divorced in a complete and binding manner.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since divorce is dependent on the husband's initiative, as long as a woman's status is in question - and for that reason she may not marry another person - he is required to continue to support her (Rashi, <i>Ketubot</i> 97b).</i>
If a poor<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketubot</i> 96a mentions two years and three years, stating that the difference is between a rich widow (who can afford to wait) and a poor one; alternatively, between a brash widow (who is not embarrassed to appear in court) and a modest one (who will hesitate before coming). The Rambam does not mention the second opinion at all (although generally, when the Talmud mentions two opinions, he rules according to the second opinion), nor does the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 93:14). Rabbenu Asher and the <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 93:26, however, do mention the latter opinion.</i> widow waits two years before she sues for support - or if a rich widow waits three years - it can be assumed that she has waived her claim to support for the previous years.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The Rashba maintains that if, however, the woman took property as security, or if she borrowed money to be repaid with the money she will receive for her support, she is still entitled to receive the money retroactively. This opinion is cited by the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>op. cit.</i>).</i> Therefore, she is not granted support for that period. From the time she issues a claim onward, however, she is entitled to support.<br>If, however, she waited even one day less [before presenting her claim], she is not considered to have waived her claim, and she may collect her support for the previous years.
[The following rules apply when] a widow demands support for her subsistence from the heirs, and they claim to have paid her, while she claims that she did not receive payment. Until she remarries, the burden of proof is on the orphans. [If they do not support their claim], the widow is entitled to take a rabbinical oath and collect the money due her.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">As long as she has not remarried, the property of her husband's estate is considered under lien to her and in her possession. Hence, she is given this privilege.</i> If she has already remarried, the burden of proof is upon her. [If she does not support her claim,] the heirs are entitled to take a rabbinic oath that they paid her [and are freed of obligation].<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For once she remarries, the property is considered to be in the possession of the heirs. Hence, they are given this privilege.</i>
The laws governing the extra sum added by the husband to the <i>ketubah</i> are the same as those governing the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i>. Therefore, if a widow demands payment of this additional amount - or sells it, waives payment of it [in favor of her husband] or gives it as security - together with the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i>, she is not entitled to support for her subsistence.<br>If she demanded payment for a portion and left a portion uncollected,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The same law applies if the widow demanded payment of the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i>, but did not demand payment for the additional amount.</i> it is as if she demanded payment for a portion of the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i> and left a portion uncollected.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 1.</i><br>Whenever a woman sells or waives payment of her <i>ketubah</i> without making any further specification, she is considered to have sold or waived this additional amount together with the fundamental requirement of the <i>ketubah</i>. For the term <i>ketubah</i> is universally used to refer to both these items.

Chapter 19

One of the provisions of [a woman's] <i>ketubah</i> is that her male offspring will inherit the money due their mother by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> and the <i>nedunyah</i> she brought to the household as <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This and the laws that follow are relevant only in situations where a man has children from two different wives and he did not divorce the wives before their death. When a man's wives die before he does, he inherits their <i>nedunyah</i> and is not required to pay them the money due them by virtue of their <i>ketubot</i>. Nevertheless, our Sages ordained that a woman's children should benefit from her investment in the household and the commitment made to her. Hence, before the father's estate is divided among all the heirs, the children of each of his wives are entitled to receive the monies mentioned above.</i> Afterwards, these children divide the remainder of the estate with their brothers equally.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Note the statements of the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 111:16), who states that this practice is not followed in the present age. The rationale is that the practice was instituted in the Talmudic era to encourage a father to give his daughter a generous <i>nedunyah</i>. (For because of this practice, he can be assured that the money he gives will remain within his family.) In the present age, however, this encouragement is not necessary, for it has become customary for parents to endow their daughters generously before marriage.</i>
What is implied? A man married a woman whose <i>ketubah</i> and <i>nedunyah</i> were together valued at 1000 [<i>zuz</i>]. She bore a son, and then she died within [her husband's] lifetime. Afterwards, the man married another woman whose <i>ketubah</i> and <i>nedunyah</i> were together valued at 200 [<i>zuz</i>]. She bore a son, and then she died within [her husband's] lifetime. Afterwards, the man died, leaving an estate worth 2000 [<i>zuz</i>].<br>His first wife's son should inherit 1000 [<i>zuz</i>] by virtue of his mother's <i>ketubah</i>, and his second wife's son should inherit 200 [<i>zuz</i>] by virtue of his mother's <i>ketubah</i>, and the remainder they should [both] inherit and [divide] equally. Thus, the first wife's son will receive 1400 [<i>zuz</i>], and the second wife's son will receive 600 [<i>zuz</i>].
When does the above apply? When [the estate] is worth at least one <i>dinar</i> more than the amount [due the children by virtue of their mothers'] <i>ketubot</i>. If, however, there is not a <i>dinar</i> or more remaining [in the estate],<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam stated in Chapter 16, Halachah 7, the children's inheritance of the money due their mother by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> applies only when there is enough landed property remaining in the estate to pay for both <i>ketubot</i>.<br>From the wording of the Rambam, it would, nevertheless, appear that it is sufficient that the additional <i>dinar</i> be movable property; it need not be landed property. This indeed is the ruling of the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 111:14). If this is the intent, it would reflect a change in the Rambam's decision from his ruling in his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Ketubot</i> 10:3).</i> the entire estate should be divided equally [without applying the provision mentioned above].<br>[The rationale is that] if [the children of one of the mothers] will inherit [what is due them by virtue of] their mother's <i>ketubah</i>, [the other mother's children] will inherit [what is due them by virtue of] their mother's <i>ketubah</i>, and at least one <i>dinar</i> will not remain to be divided among the heirs, then this provision [which is of Rabbinic origin] will supersede [entirely] the equal division of the estate among the children that is required by Scriptural law.
The same law applies to a man who married many wives, whether one after the other or several at one time. If they have all died in his lifetime, and they have all borne male children from this man, if his estate contains at least a <i>dinar</i> more than the <i>ketubot</i> of all his wives, each of the [sets of] sons inherits the money due their mother by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>. The remainder [of the estate] is divided equally.
[Should the estate not be large enough to satisfy the obligations of both <i>ketubot</i> and the additional <i>dinar</i>,] and the heirs say: "We will increase the value of our father's estate so that there will be more than a <i>dinar</i> [in addition to the value of the <i>ketubot</i>]," so that they can collect [the money due their mother by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>, their request is not accepted. Instead, the estate should be evaluated in court according to its value at the time of their father's death [and the decision rendered on the basis of this figure].<br>Even if the value of the estate increases or decreases [in the time between] the death of their father and the actual division of the property, [the decision whether to grant the heirs their mothers' <i>ketubot</i>] depends only on the value of the estate at the time of their father's death.
If the value of the estate was a <i>dinar</i> or more than the sum of the two <i>ketubot</i>, each of the sons inherits the money due his mother by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>. Even if there is a promissory note due against the estate for the amount that exceeds the value of the <i>ketubot</i>, it is not considered to have reduced [the value of the estate].
[The following rules apply when a man] was married to two wives. One died within his lifetime and one died afterwards, and he has sons from both wives. Although the value of the estate he left does not exceed the value of the two <i>ketubot</i>, the sons of the [wife who died after her husband's death] have the right to inherit the money due their mother by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> first, [provided] she took the oath required of a widow before she died.<br>[The rationale is] that they do not inherit their mother's <i>ketubah</i> by virtue of this provision, but rather through the Torah's laws of inheritance.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., once the woman took the oath required of her, the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> is considered to be justly hers. Her children then inherit her property.</i> Afterwards, the sons of the wife [who died during her husband's lifetime] inherit [the money due their mother by virtue of her] <i>ketubah</i> on the basis of this provision. If anything remains in the estate afterwards, it should be divided equally.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, they are entitled to inherit the money due their mother by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> even if the estate is not large enough to allow for the division of the inheritance according to Scriptural law afterwards (<i>Ketubot</i> 91a; <i>Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer</i> 111:8).</i><br>If [the woman who died after her husband] died before she was able to take the oath [required of her], only the sons of [the woman who died in her husband's lifetime] are entitled to inherit [the money due their mother by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since the woman did not take the oath required of a widow, there is room to suspect that her husband already gave her the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>, or that she took possession of it herself. Therefore, her sons are not entitled to collect her <i>ketubah</i>.</i> The remainder is divided equally.
[The following rules apply when a man] was married to two wives, fathered sons with both of them and then died. If the wives died after the father did, but after taking the oath [required of widows], each of their sons is entitled to inherit [the money due his mother by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> according to the Torah's laws of inheritance, and not by virtue of this provision. Therefore, in this instance it is not significant whether the estate is more valuable than the sum of the two <i>ketubot</i> or not. [The claim of] the heirs of the wife married first takes precedence over the claim of the wife married afterwards.<br>If neither of the wives took [the required] oath, the sons [of both women] divide the entire estate equally. Neither has the right to inherit [his mother's] <i>ketubah</i>, for a widow is not entitled to her <i>ketubah</i> until she takes the [required] oath.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Nor are the sons entitled to inherit the money due their mothers by virtue of their <i>ketubot</i> based on the provision mentioned above, because this is applicable only when the woman dies in her husband's lifetime.</i>
[In the above instance,] if one of the widows took the [required] oath and one did not, the sons of the one who took the oath inherit [the money due their mother by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> first, and then the remainder of the estate is divided equally [among all the heirs].<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The sons of the widow who did not take the oath are not entitled to inherit the money due their mother by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>.</i><br>Whenever [a son] inherits [the money due his mother by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> after she died in his father's lifetime, he does not have the right to expropriate property that was sold to others; [he inherits] only property in the possession of the estate.
Among the provisions of the <i>ketubah</i> is that after the death of their father, [his wife's] daughters have the right to receive support for their sustenance from their father's estate<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 21, Halachah 18, which states that the daughters are granted this right even when their father divorced their mother before his death, and they took up residence with their mother.</i> until they become consecrated<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Once the daughter is consecrated by a husband, her support is no longer the responsibility of her father's estate. (See also Halachah 15.)</i> or until they reach the age of <i>bagrut</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">During a man's lifetime, he is required only to provide his daughters with their sustenance until the age of six (Chapter 12, Halachah 14). After his death, however, they are entitled to support until the age of twelve and a half.</i><br>If a daughter reaches the age of <i>bagrut</i> but has not been consecrated, or if she is consecrated before she reaches the age of <i>bagrut</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's wording, it would appear that he maintains that a girl forfeits her right to support if she becomes consecrated while she is a minor. This ruling is not universally accepted by the <i>Rishonim</i>. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> quotes Rabbenu Chananel and the Rashba as saying that she does not forfeit this right in such an instance. The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 112) mentions a third view: that if she consecrates herself, she forfeits her support, but if her brothers are involved in her consecration, she is still entitled to support. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 112:3) quotes the Rambam's view, while the Ramah mentions the other opinions.</i> she is not entitled to receive her sustenance.<br>When a daughter receives her sustenance from her father's estate after his death, her earnings and the ownerless objects she discovers belong to her, not to her brothers.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Although during his lifetime, her father is entitled to her earnings and the objects she discovers, this right is not given to his sons. The rationale is that the father would prefer for his daughter to receive her own earnings than to have them given to his sons.</i>
An allotment of support, garments and living quarters should be made for a man's daughters from his estate, just as it is made for his widow. His [landed property] may be sold to provide his daughters with their sustenance and garments without a public announcement, just as it is sold to provide for his widow's sustenance and garments.<br>[There is, however, one difference between the two.] The allotment to the widow is made according to her social standing and that of her husband, while his daughters are given only their necessities. The daughters are not, however, required to take an oath.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Although a widow is not required to take an oath when collecting her support, this is because she is required to take an oath when she collects the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>. Therefore, one might think that a daughter would be required to take such an oath. Indeed, the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 112:15, based on the statements of <i>Tosafot</i>, requires that such an oath be taken.</i>
A man's sons are not entitled to inherit [the money due their mother by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>, nor are his daughters entitled to receive their sustenance according to the provisions mentioned above unless they manifest possession of the document [recording their mother's] <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad and the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> question the Rambam's ruling with regard to the support the man's daughters receive for their sustenance. They maintain that this support is not dependent on whether the mother receives the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> (and therefore, the waiver of that payment has no effect). The Rambam's opinion appears to be based on his statements in Chapter 17, Halachah 19, in which he states that a woman who waives payment of her <i>ketubah</i> forgoes all the provisions of her <i>ketubah</i>. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> does not mention this issue, and the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 112:1) cites the opinion of the Ra'avad.</i> If, however, they do not manifest possession of the document, they are not entitled to anything, for it is possible that their mother waived her <i>ketubah</i> [in favor of her husband]. In a locale where it is not customary to record the <i>ketubah</i> in a document, however, the children are entitled to [the benefits stemming from] these provisions.
When, shortly before his passing, a man orders that one of the provisions of [his wife's] <i>ketubah</i> be ignored - e.g., he said: "My daughters should not derive their sustenance from my estate," "My widow should not derive her sustenance from my estate," or "My sons should not inherit the money due their mother by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>" - his words are of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that the obligation took effect at the time of his marriage, and he is incapable of negating it at a later time.</i><br>[Although] a person gives his entire estate to others through an oral will<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">An oral will refers to a person's disposition of his property verbally before his death. As explained in <i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i>, Chapter 8, our Sages ordain that such a disposition of property is acceptable.</i> [all the provisions of his wife's <i>ketubah</i> must be met]. [The rationale is] that the transfer of property through an oral will does not take effect until after death, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 8:8. (See also <i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 8:9.)</i> Thus, the mandate of the will and the obligations of the estate due to the provisions [of the <i>ketubah</i>] take effect simultaneously. Therefore, the widow and [the deceased's] daughters receive support for their sustenance from the estate, and [the deceased's] sons inherit the money due their mother by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> if she dies during her husband's lifetime.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam with regard to the rights of a person's sons and daughters. Nevertheless, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 111:17) follows the Rambam's view.</i>
A daughter of a girl who nullifies her marriage through <i>mi'un</i> is considered like any other daughter, and she is entitled to support for her sustenance [after her father's death].<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling has been contested by other authorities on several grounds. First, the Ra'avad challenges the Rambam, asking: how is it possible for a girl who nullifies her marriage through <i>mi'un</i> to have a child? By definition, <i>mi'un</i> is possible when a girl is a <i>k'tanah</i>, a minor (see Chapter 4, Halachah 7), and while she is a minor it is impossible for her to conceive a child. He explains that <i>Ketubot</i> 53b is speaking about a girl who leaves her husband through <i>mi'un</i> - she is entitled to return to her deceased father's home and receive support for her sustenance.<br>Second, the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> accepts the fact that a girl can conceive a child while a minor, but asks: Since the mother nullifies the marriage through <i>mi'un</i>, it is as if her husband had never had any obligations to her at all. Her <i>ketubah</i> and all of its provisions are nullified entirely. Why then is his estate liable for the support of his daughter after his death? See the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 112:11 for a possible explanation.</i> Nevertheless, the daughter of a <i>yevamah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">When a man dies childless, his brother (the <i>yavam</i>) inherits his entire estate, and that estate is responsible for the <i>ketubah</i> of the <i>yevamah</i> (the widow who is married by the <i>yavam</i>). If a <i>yevamah</i> bears a girl, the deceased brother's estate is not liable for the girl's support after her father's (the <i>yavam's</i>) death, for she is not the daughter of the deceased brother. Nor is the <i>yavam's</i> estate responsible for her support, for he never gave a <i>ketubah</i> to the <i>yevamah</i>.<br>Note, however, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 112:5), which states that if the deceased brother did not leave an estate, the <i>yavam</i> must give the <i>yevamah</i> a <i>ketubah</i> from his own property. Hence, in this instance, his estate becomes liable for the support of his daughters.</i> the daughter of a <i>sh'niyah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since the mother's marriage is forbidden, our Sages did not grant her a <i>ketubah</i>. <i>Ketubot</i> 54a questions whether they also did not grant her the rights stemming from the <i>ketubah's</i> provisions, including her daughter's right to support in this instance. Since the question is left unresolved, her daughter is not granted this privilege.</i> the daughter of one's <i>arusah,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Who was born before the couple entered the phase of <i>nisu'in</i> (<i>Shulchan Aruch, loc. cit.</i>). Since the <i>ketubah</i> takes effect only after <i>nisu'in</i>, this daughter is not entitled to support.</i> and the daughter of a woman who has been raped<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>anusah</i> refers to a virgin who was raped. The rapist is required to marry her and is forbidden to divorce her (Deuteronomy 22:28 . Since he is forbidden to divorce her, she is not granted a <i>ketubah</i>. Our Sages (<i>ibid.</i>) question whether or not she was not granted the provisions of a <i>ketubah</i>. This question is also left unresolved, and her daughter is not granted the privilege of deriving her livelihood from her father's estate. Similarly, the daughter of a woman who was raped and never married by the rapist is not entitled to support from her father's estate.</i> are not entitled to support for their sustenance after their father's death by virtue of this provision. During their father's lifetime, however, he is obligated to support them like any of his other sons and daughters.
A man who consecrates a girl who is receiving her sustenance from her brothers is obligated to provide her with support from the time of consecration onward. [Although a husband is ordinarily required to support his wife only after <i>nisu'in</i>, an exception is made in this instance, because] the girl is not entitled to support from her brothers after she becomes consecrated. Nor is she past the age of majority, when she is capable of providing for her own sustenance, but rather she is a minor, or a <i>na'arah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 112:6 interprets the Rambam's wording as implying that after the girl reaches the age of <i>bagrut</i>, she is required to support herself.<br>The <i>Beit Shmuel</i> also mentions that other <i>Rishonim</i> interpret <i>Ketubot</i> 53b, the source for this halachah, differently. According to their interpretation, the husband is not liable for the girl's support. If the husband desires, continues the <i>Beit Shmuel</i>, he may rely on this opinion.</i> [Hence, her husband is obligated to support her, because] a man would not desire that the woman he consecrated be put to shame [by having to] wander and beg [for her support].<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">It is as if he had made a commitment to support her when he consecrated her.</i>
Should a daughter marry and then leave her husband through the rite of <i>mi'un</i>, or be divorced, or be widowed - even if she is obligated to marry a <i>yavam</i> - since she returns to her father's home and has not reached the age of <i>bagrut</i>, she is entitled to support from her father's estate until she reaches the age of <i>bagrut</i> or until she becomes consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 112:4) cites the Rambam's view. The Ramah differs, however, citing the opinion of Rabbenu Asher, who maintains that from the time a girl becomes consecrated after her father's death, and onward, she is not entitled to support from his estate.</i>
When a man dies leaving both sons and daughters, the sons inherit his estate,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The estate is given to them, and they may use it as they see fit. They are, however, forbidden to sell the property except in an extreme situation - e.g., to use the proceeds to redeem captives (Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 112:11). Moreover, if the court sees that the sons are spending lavishly and abusing the resources of the estate, they should set aside the daughters' portion.</i> and it is their responsibility to provide their sisters with support until they reach the age of <i>bagrut</i>, or until they become consecrated.<br>When does this apply? When the estate is large enough to provide both the sons and the daughters with their sustenance until the daughters reach the age of <i>bagrut</i>. This is called an ample estate.<br>If, however, the estate contains only a lesser amount, the funds necessary to support the daughters until they reach the age of <i>bagrut</i> are set aside,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">They are entrusted to a guardian appointed by the court.</i> and the remainder is given to the sons. If the estate contains only enough to provide for the support of the daughters, the daughters are entitled to their sustenance until they reach <i>bagrut</i> or until they become consecrated, and the sons should beg for their support.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For it is more common for males to beg for alms than for females to do so (<i>Ketubot</i> 67a). This principle is also followed with regard to the distribution of charity. If there is a needy male and a needy female, and the communal fund cannot provide both of them with their needs, the female is given priority (<i>Hilchot Matnot Aniyim</i> 8:15).</i>
When does the above apply? When the estate contains landed property. If, however, the estate contains movable property, since it is only by virtue of the ordinance of the <i>geonim</i> that the daughters are entitled to derive their support from the movable property, the sons and the daughters should receive their support equally from this meager estate. For with regard to movable property, [the daughters] were given the right to be considered like the sons, but not superior to them. The <i>geonim</i> have ruled in this manner.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 112:12) states that according to the custom to include within the <i>ketubah</i> a clause stating that the obligations of the estate are binding on movable property as well, the estate is considered to be meager and the support for the daughters is set aside.</i>
If [a man] left an ample estate of landed property, and afterwards [the value of the estate decreased until] it became meager, the heirs have already acquired [the property].<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the property should remain in the possession of the sons, and they must continue to provide for their sisters' sustenance. It is not expropriated from the sons and given to a guardian.</i><br>If [the estate was deemed] meager [in value] at the time of the man's death, and [the value increased afterwards]<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> mentions a difference of opinion with regard to the interpretation of the word "afterwards." Rashi (<i>Ketubot</i> 91a) maintains that this means "after the man's death, but before the matter is brought to the court and a guardian appointed." Others (Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi and the Rashba) maintain that even after a guardian is appointed, the property can be given to the heirs if its value increases.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 112:14) quotes the Rambam's wording without relating to this issue. The Ramah mentions the latter view.</i> to the point that it is considered ample, the heirs are given the right to inherit it. Even if the value did not increase, if the sons sold an estate that was considered meager, the sale is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The opinion of <i>Tosafot, et al.</i> is that even if the property has been entrusted to a guardian, if it is sold by the heirs the sale is binding. The Ramah (<i>loc. cit.</i>), however, appears to follow the view that the sale is binding only before the property has been entrusted to a guardian.<br>According to Rabbenu Asher, the daughters have no lien on the money received from the sale. Although Rav Hai Gaon differs, it appears that Rabbenu Asher's view is favored (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 112:30).</i>
If the estate was ample but a debt was owed, or [the man] had made a provision with his wife, [promising] to support her daughter [from a previous marriage], the debt or [the obligation to] support the widow's daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 23, Halachah 17.</i> does not prevent the estate from being considered ample.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 112:15) states that the payment of the money due the widow by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> is, however, considered in determining whether the estate is ample or not.</i> Instead, the sons inherit the entire estate. [It is their responsibility] to pay the creditor his debt, to support the widow's daughter for the time stipulated and to support their sisters until they reach majority, or until they become consecrated and leave their domain.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling entitles the sons to derive their sustenance from the estate together with the daughters until the funds are depleted.</i>
[The following rules apply when a man] left a widow and a daughter, either from her or from another wife, and his estate is not large enough to provide support for both of them. The widow should derive her support from the estate, and the daughter should beg [for alms].<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Rambam, the property set aside for the widow's support should be given to a third party, and he should follow the guidelines set in Chapter 18, Halachah 21 (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).<br>There are opinions that maintain that property is set aside for the widow's support only when there is a son and a daughter, and the estate is too meager to support both of them. In that instance, since property is being set aside for the daughters' support, and the widow takes precedence over the daughters, property is also set aside for her. When property is not required to be set aside for the daughters, it is not set aside for the widow's support either. Instead, she, the daughters and the sons, all derive their sustenance from the estate together.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 93:4) mentions both opinions, and the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 93:9 states that the latter view is favored by most authorities. This difference of opinion also leads to another (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 112:15): Does the obligation to support the widow cause the estate to be considered meager or not? According to the Rambam it does, but according to the other authorities it does not.</i><br>Similarly, I maintain that support for [a man's] daughter takes precedence over [his] sons' inheritance of their mother's <i>ketubah</i> if she died in her husband's lifetime, although both [rights] are provisions of the <i>ketubah</i>. [This can be derived by making] an inference from a more serious responsibility to a less serious one: If the inheritance [of a man's estate to which the sons are entitled] by virtue of Scriptural law is superseded by [the obligation to provide] the daughter with her support, how much more so should [the sons'] inheritance of [their mother's] <i>ketubah</i>, which is only a Rabbinic ordinance, be superseded by [the obligation to provide] the daughter with her support.
When a man dies and leaves older daughters and younger daughters, without leaving a son, we do not say that the younger daughters should be granted their sustenance until they reach the age of <i>bagrut</i>, and then the entire estate should be divided equally. Instead, the entire estate should be divided equally [immediately].

Chapter 20

Our Sages decreed that a man give a certain portion of his holdings to his daughter as a dowry.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketubot</i> 52b states that an allusion to this concept can be found in Jeremiah 29:6: "Give your daughters to men." "Is it possible for a father to initiate marriage proceedings?" our Sages ask. And they explain that the intent of the verse is that a man should provide his daughter with a dowry attractive enough for a man to desire her.</i> This is referred to as <i>parnasah</i>. When [a man] marries off his daughter, he should provide her with at least the wardrobe that is given to the wife of a poor Jewish man, as we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., 50 <i>zuz</i>, as stated in Chapter 13, Halachah 1.</i><br>When does the above apply? When [the bride's] father is poor. If he is wealthy, he should provide for his daughter according to his standards.
If a father explicitly tells the prospective husband that his daughter does not possess anything, and that [his intent is that] he marry her although she does not possess a wardrobe, [the bride] is not entitled to anything of her father's.<br>[In such a situation, the prospective] husband should not say: "When she comes to my home, I will provide her with a wardrobe." Instead, he should provide her with a wardrobe while she is living in her father's home.
When a father dies and leaves [at least one son and] a daughter [she is provided with a dowry from his estate]. We estimate what the father would have desired to give the daughter as a dowry, and she is given [that sum].<br>How is it possible to arrive at such an estimate? [We survey the habits of] his friends and acquaintances, his business affairs and his standard of living. If he married off a daughter during his lifetime, we base our estimate [on what she was given]. If the court is unable to determine what he would have desired [to give his daughter], she is given a tenth of his estate as a dowry.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's wording, it appears that one tenth is the average, but that if a man is known to be generous, his daughter may be given more than a tenth. The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 113:1) mentions the opinion of certain authorities who maintain that a girl should never be given more than a tenth of the estate, but states that the common practice is not to follow this view.</i>
When a man leaves [a son and] many daughters, the first [daughter] who desires to marry<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The dowry is given to the daughter only when she prepares to marry, not beforehand. Nevertheless, she is given a tenth of the value of the estate at the time of her father's death, regardless of its present value (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>; Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 113:4).</i> is given a tenth of the estate. The second [daughter to marry] receives a tenth of what was left after providing the first [daughter with her dowry]. And the third daughter receives a tenth of what was left after providing the second [daughter].<br>If all [a man's] daughters come to marry at the same time, [money is set aside for them according to the above pattern,] even if there are ten daughters [or more]. Afterwards, [all the allotments are pooled], and then divided equally among the daughters. The remainder of the estate is given to the sons.
The allotment of a tenth [of the estate] as a dowry is not one of the provisions of the <i>ketubah</i>. Therefore, even according to the enactment of the later Sages,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even according to the Sages who ordained that the payment of the money due a woman by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> may come from movable property (Chapter 16, Halachah 8), the payment of the dowry is from landed property alone. Note, however, the opinion of <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Ketubot</i> 51a), who differ and maintain that this allotment may also be collected from movable property.</i> it is only to be collected from landed property.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Based on the wording of Halachah 12, the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that the Rambam's opinion is that the movable property in the estate is not included in the calculation of the size of the estate on which the amount of the dowry is based. Rav Moshe HaCohen and Rabbenu Asher differ, emphasizing that although the dowry allotment is not collected from movable property, the movable property is included in this appraisal. Both authorities agree, however, that if an assessment is made of the amount that the father would have given his daughter, that assessment includes the movable property in the estate.</i> It may, however, be collected from rent due for landed property.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to rent due the father for landed property that was uncollected at the time of his death. The Ramah (<i>loc. cit.</i>) states that if the heirs have already collected the rental fee, they are not obligated to give it to their sister.</i> If, however, [a girl's] brothers desire to give her money in lieu of a tenth of the landed property, they have that right.
With regard to this allotment of a tenth [of the estate], the daughter is considered to be a creditor of her brothers. Therefore, she is entitled to collect it from property of intermediate quality. An oath is not required of her.<br>If her brothers die, she is entitled to collect it from their sons, [expropriating] property of inferior quality, and an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">That she has not received any of the estate.</i> is required of her. For she is collecting property from heirs, and [it is an accepted principle that] a person who comes to collect property from heirs may collect only from that of inferior quality and is required to take an oath [before doing so], as will be explained in the laws of loans.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 14:1, 19:1.</i>
