Should her brothers have sold the landed property of their father's estate, or given it as collateral, the daughter may collect her dowry from the purchasers,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that it is known that a girl is entitled to receive a dowry, and the purchasers of the property of the estate should have taken precautions before buying the property.</i> just as other creditors are entitled to collect from the purchasers, as will be explained in the laws of loans.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Malveh V'Loveh</i> 18:1.</i>
When a man has [several daughters, but] no sons, [his estate] is divided equally [among his daughters at the time of his death]. Although he married off the older daughters during his lifetime [and provided them with dowries], we do not grant dowries to the younger daughters and then divide the estate.
[The following rules apply when a man] has died, leaving two daughters and a son. The older daughter received a tenth of the estate as a dowry, but before the younger daughter had collected her dowry, the son died [without leaving any heirs], and [the two sisters] inherited the entire estate. [In this situation,] the younger sister is not entitled to her tenth of the estate.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketubot</i> 69a explains that the rationale for this ruling is that the daughter has received a far larger portion of the estate than she could have hoped for.</i> Instead, the entire estate is divided equally, but the older sister is granted the tenth [she had received previously].<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's opinion is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 113:8). The Ramah quotes the opinion of Rabbenu Asher, who maintains that the second daughter is given her dowry and then the estate is divided.</i>
When a man gives an order at the time of his death: "Do not give my daughters a dowry from my estate," his words are heeded. [The rationale is that a dowry] is not one of the provisions of a <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The provisions of the <i>ketubah</i> - e.g., the support of the daughters - become binding at the time of the marriage, and the man's statements have no effect regarding them (Chapter 19, Halachah 13). The dowry, by contrast, is a gift that we assume a man would make. Therefore if he explicitly states that he does not desire that it be made, his wishes are heeded.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a man dies, leaving a widow and a daughter. It has already been explained<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 19, Halachah 21.</i> that the support of a man's widow<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 113:17 and the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 113:16 state that the same ruling applies with regard to the daughters. I.e., if there are older daughters who wish to collect their dowry and marry, and younger daughters who still have to receive support from the estate, the younger daughters are entitled to object to the property's being given to their sisters. The rationale is that the support for the widow and for the daughters is considered to be a debt owed by the estate, while their dowry is considered to be a debt owed by the heirs.</i> takes precedence over the support of his daughter. Similarly, if the daughter marries, she is not entitled to collect her tenth [of the estate], because of [the obligation to] support the widow.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Once the widow has remarried or received payment for her <i>ketubah</i>, the daughter is entitled to inherit the tenth of the estate that should have been given to her. Even when she has already married, her brothers are required to give her these funds from the remainder of the estate.</i><br>Even if the daughter dies after she marries, her husband is not entitled to inherit the dowry that should have been given her.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">At times a woman's husband is considered to be a purchaser of the property he inherits from his wife, and at times an heir. If he were considered to be a purchaser, he would be entitled to take possession of the dowry due his wife, for a widow is not entitled to collect her support from property that has been sold. Nevertheless, in this instance, out of consideration for the widow, our Sages considered him like an heir, and thus enabled the widow to continue receiving her sustenance (<i>Bava Batra</i> 139b).</i> For the entire estate is considered to be in the possession of the widow so that she can derive her sustenance.
When an orphan girl is married off by her brothers or her mother as a child with her consent, and she is given 50 or 100 <i>zuz</i> as a dowry, she is entitled to collect the dowry that is due her - according to the estimation of her father's desires or one tenth of the landed property<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> cites this phrase as proof that the tenth of the estate set aside as a dowry is expropriated from landed property alone.</i> [of his estate] - from them after she attains the age of majority.<br>[This applies] even if her brothers did not provide her with sustenance,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">See the following halachah.</i> and even if she did not object at the time of the wedding. For a minor is not capable of making an objection [in court].<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the fact that she did not object at the time of the marriage is not significant. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> adds that even if the girl did not object immediately at the time she reached majority, she is entitled to object afterwards. This decision is quoted by the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 113:7).</i>
When a daughter marries after she reaches majority - whether as a <i>na'arah</i> or as a <i>bogeret</i> - and does not demand her dowry, she forfeits her dowry. If, however, she protested at the time of her marriage, she may collect her due whenever she desires.<br>[A further point must be considered when] she reaches the age of <i>bagrut</i> and remains in her father's house - regardless of whether she reaches <i>bagrut</i> after his death, or [he died] when she had already reached the age of <i>bagrut</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher writes that a girl who reached the age of <i>bagrut</i> in her father's lifetime is not entitled to a dowry from her brothers. The later Ashkenazic authorities (see <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 113:19) state, however, that this ruling is not applied.</i> If her brothers have already ceased providing her with her sustenance, which is their prerogative, as we have explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 19, Halachah 10.</i> and [the girl] remained silent and did not demand her dowry, she forfeits her dowry. If she protests, she does not forfeit her dowry.<br>If, however, her brothers had not ceased providing her with her sustenance [although] she reached <i>bagrut</i>, she is not considered to have forfeited her dowry as long as they continue to provide her with her sustenance, even though she did not protest. For she can claim that she did not demand her dowry because [her brothers] are supporting her although they are not obligated to do so,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, she is ashamed to come to them with this request (<i>Ketubot</i> 68b).</i> and she has not yet married.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Implied is that once a <i>bogeret</i> marries without demanding her dowry, she has forfeited it even though her brothers continue to provide her with her sustenance (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Karo writes that if the brothers of a <i>na'arah</i> continue to provide her with her sustenance after marriage, she does not forfeit her dowry, even if she does not protest. Although his wording in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 113:7) is slightly problematic, the later authorities explain that this is his intent. A source for both the statements of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> can be seen in the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Ketubot</i> 6:6).</i>
[The following rules apply when a man] stated - whether while making an oral will before death or while healthy - that his daughter should be given a specific sum of money as a dowry, and that this sum should be used to purchase landed property, and [then] died [afterwards].<br>When the money is in the possession of a third party and the daughter states: "Give the money to my husband and let him do with it as he desires," [the third party should do as follows]. If [the daughter] has reached the age of majority and has married, she is granted this prerogative.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that the father's intent was that the money should be entrusted to a third party only until after her marriage (Rashi, <i>Ketubot</i> 69b).</i> If she is [past majority, but merely] consecrated, the third party should follow the instructions he was given.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">For it is a mitzvah to carry out the directives of a person who dies, even if he was healthy at the time he gave these directives (<i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 4:5).</i> And if she is a minor, even if she is already married, her request is not heeded.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that the father's intent was to safeguard his daughter and her husband against wasting the funds intended for them.</i> Instead, the third party should carry out her father's instructions.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 54:1) quotes the Rambam's ruling. The Ramah refers to this ruling in <i>Choshen HaMishpat</i> 252:2, which states that this applies only if the funds were specifically entrusted to the third party for this purpose by the deceased at the time he made this statement. If they came into his possession afterwards, the concept that it is a mitzvah to carry out the directives of a person who dies does not apply.</i>

Chapter 21

A husband is entitled to [any ownerless objects] discovered by [his] wife,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ketubot</i> 47a states that since a woman's husband supports her, he might object if she were granted ownership over the items that she discovers. The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Ketubot</i> 6:1) offers a slightly different explanation: that if women were entitled to the objects that they discover, a woman might conceal her earnings and later claim that the funds came to her for ownerless objects that she discovered.</i> and the proceeds of her labor.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Chapter 12, Halachah 4, in exchange for the obligation incumbent on the man to support his wife, our Sages granted him the right to the income she generates.</i> What [type of work] must she perform on his behalf? Everything follows the custom of the country. In a place where it is customary for women to weave, she should weave. [In a place where they] embroider, she should embroider. [In a place where they] spin wool or flax, she should spin.<br>If it is not customary for women in that place to perform these labors, he may compel her only to spin wool; [wool, but not flax,] because flax damages [a woman's] mouth and lips. [This occupation is chosen because] spinning is a task designated for women, as [implied by Exodus 35:25]: "And all the skilled women put their hands to spinning...."
If a woman exerts herself and produces more than would be expected of her,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">There are two interpretations of "more than would be expected of her": a) that she worked overtime, more hours than common custom requires, b) that she performed several tasks at one time.</i> her husband is entitled to the extra amount.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">There are authorities who differ with the Rambam and maintain that a woman is entitled to keep the additional amount she earns. The <i>Bayit Chadash</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 80) states that it is not Ashkenazic custom to require a woman to give her husband any of her additional earnings.</i><br>Even when her husband<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Literally, "he is." Many manuscript copies and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> state "he and she are," instead of "he is."</i> is very wealthy and even when the woman has several maids, she may not sit idle, without work. For idleness leads to lewdness. [Her husband] may not, however, compel her to work for the entire day. Instead, according to the extent of his wealth, her obligation to work is minimized.
When a man takes a vow that prevents his wife from doing any work at all, he is obligated to divorce her and pay her [the money due her by virtue of her] <i>ketubah</i>. [The rationale is that] idleness leads to lewdness.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, rather than compel a woman to follow a course of conduct that will lead to wanton behavior, <i>Ketubot</i> 59b requires the husband to divorce his wife.</i><br>Every wife is obligated [to perform the following household tasks] on behalf of her husband: to wash his face, feet and hands, to pour him beverages, to make his bed,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries discuss whether the intent is to make her husband's bed or to make all the beds in the house. The difference is with regard to a rich woman, who could have maids perform household services. She is, nevertheless, obligated to make her husband's bed as a reflection of their personal closeness. The question is whether this applies to making the other beds in the house. <i>Ketubot</i> 61a uses the expression "makes the bed for him," indicating that the emphasis is on the husband's bed.</i> and to do his bidding - e.g., to bring him water or a utensil, to remove an article from his presence, or to perform similar tasks. She is not, however, required to do the bidding of his father or his son.
These tasks should be performed only by a man's wife alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they are a reflection of their personal closeness.</i> Even if she possesses several maids, these tasks are performed for a man only by his wife.
There are other tasks that a woman performs for her husband when they are poor; they are: to bake bread in an oven<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to bread baked in a pan or over coals - i.e., the woman must bake in an ordinary manner (<i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i>).</i> - Ezra ordained that a woman get up early and bake bread so that there will be bread available to give the poor.<br>She should cook food, wash clothes, nurse her child, place straw before her husband's beast<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the animal on which he rides. This reflects the version of <i>Ketubot</i> 61b possessed by the Sephardic authorities. The <i>Shitah Mekubetzet</i> explains that this is included in a woman's household duties, because those duties involve tasks that affect her husband's person. The standard printed text of that Talmudic passage reverses the decisions regarding his beast and cattle. (See the rationale offered by Rashi.)</i> - but not before his cattle - and grind [flour].<br>What does grinding [flour] involve? [Not that the woman actually operates the mill herself,] but that she stays at the mill, sifts<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Alternatively, guards the flour (<i>Tur, Even HaEzer</i> 80).</i> the flour and prods the animal [who turns the mill], so that [the operation of] the mill will not be hampered. If it is the [local] custom, for women to grind [flour] using a hand mill, [a woman] should grind [flour in this manner].
When does the above apply? With regard to a poor [couple]. If, however, a woman brings a maid to [the household] or property with which a maid could be purchased, or if the man possesses a maid or funds with which a maid could be purchased, the wife is not required to grind [flour], to bake, to do laundry or to place straw before her husband's beast.<br>If the wife brings two maids to [the household] or property with which two maids could be purchased, or if the man possesses two maids or is [wealthy] enough to purchase two maids, the wife is not required to cook or to nurse her child. Instead, she gives him to a maid to nurse.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries mention the importance of selecting a Jewish nursemaid. For milk that comes from non-kosher food will breed undesirable tendencies in the son's character.</i>
Thus, there are five tasks that every woman must perform on behalf of her husband: to spin [thread], to wash his face, hands and feet, to pour beverages for him, to make his bed and to do his bidding. And there are six tasks that some women perform and some women do not perform. They are: to grind [flour], to cook, to bake, to do laundry, to nurse, and to place straw before her husband's beast.
All the tasks that a woman must perform on behalf of her husband must also be performed by a woman while she is in the <i>niddah</i> state, with the exception of pouring beverages, making his bed and washing his face, hands and feet. [The rationale for the exceptions is that] this is a decree, [enacted] lest [sexual] thoughts arise, and the husband be prompted to engage in relations.<br>Therefore, when she is in the <i>niddah</i> state, she should make his bed when he is not present. When pouring a beverage for him, she should not place it in his hand as is her usual practice, but rather leave it on the ground, on a utensil or on a table, and he will take it.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam does not mention washing her husband, because his wife is forbidden to touch him while in the <i>niddah</i> state. (See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 11:18-19.)</i>
When a woman breaks utensils while performing household tasks,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 80:29 states that based on the Jerusalem Talmud, this law applies even when she breaks household articles while she is not in the midst of her household chores.</i> she is not held liable. This ruling does not reflect the dictates of the law, but is instead an enactment [of our Sages]. For if this were not the case, there would never be peace in a household. For a woman would be overly cautious and would refrain from performing many tasks, and there would thus be strife between [the couple].<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">While he accepts the Rambam's ruling, the Ra'avad offers a different rationale. The commentaries, however, justify the Rambam's view.</i>
Whenever a woman refrains from performing any of the tasks that she is obligated to perform, she may be compelled to do so, even with a rod.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Kapach emphasizes that the Rambam's intent is not that the husband should beat his wife himself, but that he should bring her to the court, which should administer corporal punishment if they see fit.<br>The Ra'avad objects to this ruling, explaining that it is unheard of to compel a woman by corporal punishment. Instead, her support should be cut back until she accepts her household duties. The Rashba offers other options - to place her under a ban of ostracism or to sell her <i>ketubah</i> and use the proceeds to hire a maid.<br>When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 80:15) mentions that the woman is compelled to perform her tasks, but omits reference to the means of compulsion employed. The Ramah quotes the opinion of the Rambam together with that of the Ra'avad and the Rashba, but appears to favor the latter views.</i> When a husband complains that [his wife] does not perform [her required tasks], and [the wife] claims that she does, [the dispute should be clarified by having] a [neutral] woman dwell with them or [by asking] the neighbors.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The woman is not required to support her claim with an oath, because a pattern of the husband's complaining and the woman's being compelled to take an oath would arise, and peace would not reign within the household (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 80:28).</i> The judges should clarify the matter in the best way they see fit.
During the time a woman nurses her child, she is not compelled to perform as many tasks [as usual], and wine and foods that are beneficial to nursing are added to her support.<br>If, despite the fact that she was allotted the foods appropriate for her, she desires to eat more or desires to eat other foods, because of the craving in her stomach, she is entitled to eat everything she desires [provided she pays for the additional food] from her own funds. The husband cannot prevent her, saying: "Perhaps she will overeat or eat harmful foods<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Although a woman is enjoined not to eat foods that would harm her milk supply, she is allowed to do so if she feels physical pain, because her needs take priority over those of the child. In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Karo objects to the Rambam's ruling; in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 80:11), he quotes dissenting views together with that of the Rambam, without favoring either view.<br>Note the <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 80:22, who emphasizes that when there is a real danger to the child's life, and no danger to the mother, the woman must adjust her diet to help the child.</i> and the child will die." [The rationale is] that the physical pain the woman feels takes priority.
When a woman bears twins, she cannot be compelled to nurse both of them. Instead, she is required to nurse one, while the husband is required to hire a nursemaid for the second child.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Asher states that she is required to nurse both children, but this view is not accepted by the later authorities.</i><br>If a woman desires to nurse another woman's child together with her own, her husband may object and restrict her to nursing only her own child.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 80:14) quotes the <i>Tur</i>, which states that a husband may even prevent a woman from nursing her own child who was born to her from a previous husband.</i>
Although a woman takes a vow not to nurse her child, she may be compelled to do so until the child - whether a boy or a girl - is 24 months old.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Implied in the marital contract is that the woman will nurse her children. Hence, she may not object.</i><br>If a woman desires to nurse her child, but her husband objects, claiming that this will mar her beauty, she is given this prerogative, for it is painful for her to part from her child.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Ketubot</i> 61a) mentions another rationale: having milk without nursing causes pain.</i> [This law applies] even if she owns several maids.
If she is poor and would thus be obligated to nurse her child, but her husband is rich, and it is appropriate that his wife not be obligated to nurse his child - if his wife does not desire to nurse, he must hire a nursemaid or buy a maid, even if he did not possess any maidservants beforehand. [The rationale is] that the woman's social standing rises together with that of her husband and does not descend with his.
If a woman claims that [her husband] is of [a social standing that] requires him to hire or purchase a maid, and he claims that he is not, the burden of proof is on the woman. [The husband] is not [required] to take an oath.
When a woman is divorced, she cannot be compelled to nurse [her child]. If she desires to nurse the child, [her ex-husband] must pay her a wage.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">For a father is obligated to pay for his child's sustenance until the age of six, as stated in Chapter 12, Halachah 14.</i> If she does not desire [to nurse], she should give the son to his father, and he should care for him.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The husband cannot, however, be compelled to pay for a nursemaid if the mother is given custody.</i><br>When does the above apply? When she did not nurse the child long enough for him to recognize her. If, however, [the child is able] to recognize his mother, even if [the child] is blind,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For even a blind child can recognize his mother from her smell and the flavor of her milk.</i> he should not be separated from his mother because of the [possible] danger [the separation will cause] the child.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The pain of separation from his mother could cause the child to undergo travail that might lead to weakness. Rashi (<i>Ketubot</i> 59b) states that it is possible that the infant might reject another nursemaid and hence starve to death.</i> Instead, the woman is compelled to nurse the child for a wage until he reaches the age of 24 months.
[A husband] is not [obligated to] support his divorcee, even when she is nursing his child. In addition to the wage she receives [as a nursemaid], he must, however, provide her with those things that the child needs for clothing, food, drink, salves and the like. A woman who is pregnant is not entitled to any [payment] at all [from her ex-husband].<br>[The following rules apply after the 24] months have been completed, and the child has been weaned. If the divorcee desires that her son remain in her custody, he is not separated from her until he completes his sixth year [of life].<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's decision, explaining that a father is obligated to begin educating his child at an early age, and this is impossible when the child is in the mother's custody. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> refutes that argument, explaining that the amount of teaching that the father is obligated to give the child can be communicated at visits.<br>It must be emphasized, however, that the Rambam is referring to a situation in which the mother shares the same standards of observance as the father. If that is not the case, and the mother's observance is lacking, the father should be given the right to custody.</i> Instead, his father is compelled to provide him with his sustenance while he lives with his mother.<br>After the child completes his sixth year, the father has the right to say: "If [my son] is in my custody, I will support him. If, however, he continues to live with his mother,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's wording, it appears that if a son desires to stay in his mother's custody, and the mother is willing to support him, he is entitled to do so. (See <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 82:9.)</i> I will not give him anything."<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">At present, there are many courts that require the father to continue supporting his son, even if he desires to remain with his mother.</i><br>A mother, by contrast, is given custody of her daughter forever, even after [she passes] the age of six.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For her mother is more prepared to train her to grow up as a woman.<br>The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 82:7) states that this law applies only when the court feels that it is in the daughter's best interests to remain in her mother's custody. If, however, it appears that the daughter's interests will be served better when she is in her father's custody, he is awarded that privilege.</i>
What is implied? If the father is [wealthy enough] to be obligated to give charity, the money necessary for his daughter's support should be expropriated from him and used to support the daughter, while she is in her mother's custody.<br>Even if the mother marries another [man], her daughter remains in her custody, and the father is obligated to provide for her sustenance until his death, as an act of charity. [Moreover, even if the girl's] father dies, she is entitled to receive her sustenance from his estate, as a provision of [her mother's] <i>ketubah</i>, although she remains in her mother's custody.<br>If a mother does not want her children - either males or females - to remain in her custody after she weans them, she has this prerogative, and she can give their father their custody, or make them wards of the community if there is no father, and [the community] must care for them.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that this implies that a woman does not have any responsibility to raise her children. The <i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i> maintains that if the mother has means, she is required to provide for her children's support and cannot cast the burden on the community. She need not, however, raise them in her home, lest this deter other men from desiring to marry her. This latter opinion is not, however, mentioned in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 82:8) or its commentaries when dealing with this situation.</i>

Chapter 22

The husband takes precedence over any other person with regard to the inheritance of his wife's estate.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Chapter 12, Halachah 3 and notes, this is one of the four privileges our Sages granted a husband as part of the marriage contract. (See also <i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 1:8.)</i><br>When does the husband acquire this right? When his wife leaves her father's domain, even though she has not entered the <i>chuppah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The second phase of marriage, <i>nisu'in</i>, does not start until the woman enters the <i>chuppah</i>, and it is only at that time that the marriage contract takes effect. Nevertheless, an exception is made in this instance, as explained in the following halachot and notes.</i> Since the woman has entered her husband's domain, he [has the right] to inherit [her estate].
What is implied? When a woman has been consecrated and her father hands her over to her husband or to his agents, or the agents of the woman's father hand her over to her husband or to his agents, and the woman dies on the way, before she enters the <i>chuppah</i>, her husband inherits her estate, even though her dowry is still in her father's home.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 57:1) cites the opinion of the <i>Tur</i> and other Ashkenazic authorities, which is that the husband does not have a right to inherit his wife's dowry until it enters his possession.</i><br>Similarly, if the father or his agents went together with the husband, and the husband entered into privacy in a courtyard together with [his bride] with the intent of marriage,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Moreover, if the courtyard belongs to the husband, it is assumed that the couple entered for the sake of marriage, even when they do not explicitly state so. This is the view of all authorities, and the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that it is also shared by the Rambam.</i> and afterwards she dies, her husband inherits [her estate].<br>If, however, [the woman and] her husband or his agents are still accompanied by her father or his agents on their journey to the husband's house, her father inherits [her estate] if she dies, even if her dowry is already in her husband's home. [This law applies even if the woman] and her husband entered a courtyard together to spend the night, as travelers lodge together in one inn.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If the courtyard belonged to her, this intent is understood even when it is not explicitly stated.</i> [The rationale is that] she is accompanied by her father or his agents, and [her husband] has not entered into privacy with her for the sake of marriage.
Similarly, when a <i>bogeret</i>, an orphan, or a widow<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This law applies even if the widow is still a minor, as reflected in Chapter 3, Halachah 12.</i> goes from her father's house to her husband's home on her own initiative without being accompanied by her husband or his agents, and dies on the way, her husband does not inherit [her estate].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Once she is met by her husband or his agents, however, he is entitled to inherit her estate.</i>
Although a man marries a woman with whom he is forbidden [to have relations], if she dies [during his lifetime], he inherits her estate when his consecration of her is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachot 6 and 7; Chapter 4, Halachah 14.</i> Similarly, a man who marries a <i>k'tanah</i> [after her father's death]<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Or after she becomes divorced or widowed in her father's lifetime (Chapter 4, Halachot 7 and 8).</i> inherits her estate if she dies in his lifetime, even though his consecration of her is not binding entirely.<br>When, by contrast, a mentally capable man marries a deaf mute, he is not entitled to inherit her estate when she dies.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that she is not entitled to a <i>ketubah</i> (Chapter 11, Halachah 4). Moreover, since she is not responsible for her actions, she has no right to transfer her property.</i> When, however, a deaf mute marries a mentally capable woman and dies, he should inherit her estate. For she is capable of understanding and married him voluntarily. [In doing so,] she gave him a right to her property.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Although the Ra'avad objects to this ruling, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 90:3) quotes the Rambam's view.</i>
When a <i>k'tanah</i> was consecrated with her father's consent, but married without his consent - whether in his presence or outside his presence - her father has a right to object, as we have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 3, Halachah 13.</i> [In such a situation,] if the girl dies, her husband should not inherit her estate, even if the father remains silent, unless he expressed his consent to her marriage.
The <i>geonim</i> ruled that when a woman falls sick and asks her husband to divorce her so that he will not inherit her estate, her words are of no consequence, [even if] she [agrees to] forfeit her <i>ketubah</i>. Even if she says: "I hate him and no longer desire to live with him," her words are not heeded, and she is not judged as a woman who rebels against her husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 14, Halachah 8.</i> This is a desirable ruling.
During a woman's lifetime, her husband enjoys the benefits of all the property she owns, regardless of whether it is classified as <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> or <i>nichsei m'log</i>. If she dies in her husband's lifetime, her husband inherits everything.<br>Therefore, if the woman sold property classified as <i>nichsei m'log</i> after she married, even if she became the owner of that property before she became consecrated, her husband may expropriate the income from that property from the purchasers throughout his wife's lifetime. He may not, however, expropriate the land itself, for he has no right to the land itself, if it is classified as <i>nichsei m'log</i>, until his wife dies.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The advantage in the purchaser's continuing to own the land itself is that if the husband dies before his wife, her sale is binding, and the land becomes the purchaser's property. From this time onward, he is entitled to benefit from the land as well.<br>Rabbenu Asher differs with the Rambam on this issue and maintains that the husband has the right to take the property from the purchaser, even during his wife's lifetime. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 90:9) follows the Rambam's view, while the Ramah quotes that of Rabbenu Asher.</i><br>If she dies in his lifetime, he may expropriate the land from the purchasers<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Even the Ramah and Rabbenu Asher accept this ruling.</i> without paying them for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference of opinion with regard to this matter among the <i>geonim</i>, but all the later authorities accept this view. The rationale is that the husband's right to the land supersedes that of the purchaser. The money that the purchaser paid is considered to have become a debt owed him by the woman's estate, and the husband is not required to pay his wife's debts.</i> If the actual money that [the woman] took from the purchasers still exists, however, it must be returned to the purchasers. The husband cannot say: "Perhaps this money was found [by my wife]" [and on that basis take it as his own].<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">A husband is entitled to any ownerless object discovered by his wife. His claim is not accepted, however, if he states that money that appears to have come from the sale of property came from the discovery of a lost object. There is no need for witnesses to testify that this is the money from the sale. It is sufficient that it appears to be so. If, however, the money has been changed into a different coinage or currency, the husband is not required to return it (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i>
When does the above apply? With regard to property about which the husband knew.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Ketubot</i> 8:2), the Rambam states that this refers to property located in the bride and groom's city or the surrounding locale, as opposed to property owned by her in more distant locales. It is questionable, however, if the same geographic restrictions apply in today's global village.</i> When, however, a woman inherits property in another country without her husband's knowledge and sells it, the sale is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 90:11) states that preferably, a woman should not sell this property, because her husband is entitled to inherit it.<br>The commentaries explain that when the husband knows of his wife's financial holdings, it is an implicit part of the marriage contract - and perhaps part of his intent in entering into the marriage relationship - that he will inherit this property. When, however, he is unaware of her ownership of property, this motive cannot be given as the reason for his desire to enter this relationship.<br>It must be added that as soon as the husband becomes aware of this property, it is considered to be part of the woman's <i>nichsei m'log</i> and is bound by all the laws pertaining to such property (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, <i>loc. cit.</i>:12). Moreover, if the woman dies without selling this property, her husband is entitled to inherit it, although he was never aware of his wife's ownership of it during her lifetime.</i><br>Similarly, if a woman sells [property]<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 90) states that the woman has the full right to sell any property that she owned before she was consecrated. With regard to property that she acquired after she was consecrated, it is preferable that she not sell it - but if she sells it, the husband has no claim to it.</i> between her consecration and the consummation of the marriage bond, the sale is binding. For the husband has no right to his wife's property until their marriage is consummated.
When a woman signs over all<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i>, slightly different rules apply if the woman signed over only a portion of her property.</i> of her property to another person - regardless of whether or not that person is a relative - before she marries, even when there is a provision that if she is divorced or if she becomes a widow, this present is nullified - as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Matanah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 6:12. It is clearly obvious that the woman's intent in giving the present is to protect her holdings from being inherited by her husband.</i> - her husband is not entitled to benefit from the income of this property. And if she dies in his lifetime, he does not inherit it.<br>[The rationale is] that she gave this property away before she married. When she dies during her husband's lifetime, the recipient of the present acquires full title to it.<br>Moreover, [the same laws apply] even if she gave away a portion of her property - or all her holdings - before she married and wrote [in the deed of transfer] to the recipient: "Acquire the property from this time onward, [dependent] on my consent."<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In his gloss on <i>Ketubot</i> 79a, Rabbenu Nissim explains that the Rambam equates this provision with the one mentioned in the previous clause. The only difference between the two is one of tact. The provision in this clause is more gently worded, so that the intent to free the woman's holdings from her husband is less obvious.</i> [Although] the recipient does not acquire complete ownership until the woman expresses her consent,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Unlike the Rambam, Rabbenu Asher and other authorities maintain that the woman need not explicitly mention her consent to the present. All that is necessary is that she refrain from nullifying it.</i> her husband is not entitled to benefit from the income of this property. And if she dies in his lifetime, he does not inherit it.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Nissim asks, according to the approach of the Rambam (in contrast to the approach of Rabbenu Asher mentioned in the previous note): If the woman did not explicitly mention her consent to the present before her death, why does her husband have no right to inherit her property? The provision on which the present was based was never fulfilled.<br>Seemingly, this property can be compared to a woman's property of which her husband was unaware. As mentioned in the notes on the previous halachah, the husband has the right to inherit such property, and thus he should also inherit the property mentioned in this clause.<br>Rabbenu Nissim explains that since the Rambam maintains that a husband's right to inherit his wife's property is a Rabbinical ordinance, there is room for leniency when, as in the present case, it is obvious that the woman did not desire her husband to inherit her estate. Obviously motivated by the same question, but unwilling to offer such a resolution, Rav David Arameah explains that the Rambam's ruling applies in an instance when the woman in fact expressed her consent to the present before her death.</i>
While a woman is waiting for her <i>yavam</i> [to marry her according to the rite of <i>yibbum</i>], she may sell or give as a present property that she acquires during the time she is in this status.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the notes on Halachah 8, preferably a woman should not sell property she acquires after her consecration. One might think that the same principle applies to a <i>yevamah</i>, for she also shares a bond to her <i>yavam</i>. There is, however, a distinction between the two: a woman who is consecrated will most likely be married, while a <i>yevamah</i> may be freed from her obligation through <i>chalitzah</i>. Hence, there are no restrictions placed upon her with regard to the sale of her holdings.</i> Until he marries the <i>yevamah</i>, the <i>yavam</i> has no right to benefit from the property, even the <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between <i>nichsei m'log</i> and <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> is that with regard to <i>nichsei m'log</i>, the object itself belongs to the woman, while the property regarded as <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> is considered to belong to her late husband. He was, however, obligated to pay his wife for the value designated at the time of marriage (Chapter 16, Halachah 1).<br>Since the <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> are considered to belong to the <i>yevamah's</i> late husband's estate, one might think that the <i>yavam</i> would have a right to them. Hence, it is necessary to clarify that he is given this right only after marriage.<br>As mentioned by the Ra'avad and the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, most authorities differ with the Rambam on this point. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> maintains that the <i>yavam</i> is entitled to half of the benefit that accrues from the <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>. This opinion is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 160:6). (The Rambam's opinion is also quoted, but it appears that the other opinion is favored.) The Ramah mentions the opinion of the Ra'avad which goes even further and gives the <i>yavam</i> rights to half the benefits of <i>nichsei m'log</i> that the <i>yevamah</i> acquired while she was married to her deceased husband.</i> that she brought to his [deceased] brother's household.<br>If the <i>yevamah</i> dies in this status, her heirs from her father's household inherit her <i>nichsei m'log</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For this property belongs to her outright.</i> and half of her <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">A division is necessary because this property is considered to belong to the <i>yevamah's</i> late husband, as explained above. Therefore, his heirs have a claim to it. Nevertheless, since he died in his wife's lifetime, and she did not receive payment for this property, her own heirs also have a claim.<br>This ruling is also disputed by other authorities, who maintain that all the <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> are considered the property of the husband's heirs, together with the woman's <i>ketubah</i>. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>:7) quotes the Rambam's view, while the Ramah quotes that of the other authorities.<br>The Ramah also adds that these laws do not apply in the Ashkenazic community in the present era - or in other communities - where the rite of <i>yibbum</i> is not practiced, and instead, the <i>yevamah</i> is freed from her obligation through the rite of <i>chalitzah</i>. Since the <i>yevamah</i> will not marry the <i>yavam</i>, he has no rights with regard to her property.</i> Her husband's heirs inherit [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., both the essential requirement of the <i>ketubah</i> and any additional amount added by her deceased husband.</i> and the remaining half of her <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>, and they are responsible for her burial.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For our Sages associated a woman's burial with the inheritance of her <i>ketubah</i> (Chapter 12, Halachah 14).</i>
The money due a <i>yevamah</i> by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> is considered to be a lien on her [late] husband's estate. Therefore, a <i>yavam</i> is not entitled to sell any of his brother's property<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the value of the property left by the deceased brother is many times the value of the woman's <i>ketubah</i>, none of the property may be sold, lest the remaining property be destroyed and the woman have difficulty collecting the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i> from the purchasers (<i>Ketubot</i> 81b).</i> - neither before <i>yibbum</i> nor after <i>yibbum</i>.<br>If he sells the deceased's property, gives it away as a present, divides it with his brothers - whether before <i>yibbum</i> or after <i>yibbum</i> - his actions are of no consequence. For it is already obligatory to make this property available to the widow so that she can collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> from it.
When a man marries his <i>yevamah</i> at a time when there is produce growing on the land left by her husband, this produce should be sold,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since the produce requires the land, it is considered as if it were landed property, and the money received from the sale has the same status as the landed property mentioned in the previous halachah.</i> and the proceeds used to purchase land from which the <i>yavam</i> will derive the benefit that accrues.
When, [by contrast, the deceased] left produce that was already harvested, money or movable property, it becomes the property of the <i>yavam</i>. He may use it as he sees fit, and [the <i>yevamah's</i>] objections are of no consequence.<br>[The rationale is that the woman's right] to collect [the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> from movable property stems only from an enactment of the <i>geonim</i>, and this enactment does not have the power to prevent [the <i>yavam</i> from taking possession] of his brother's property,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, the Rivash (Responsa 365 and 366), and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 168:5) emphasize that the law stated by the Rambam applies only when the husband did not follow the suggestion (Chapter 16, Halachah 8) of stating explicitly in the <i>ketubah</i> that the woman may collect from movable property the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>. (There are other authorities who differ with the Rambam and maintain that even if the provision is not stated explicitly in the woman's <i>ketubah</i>, the movable property should be sold and land purchased.)</i> and cause him to be restrained from dealing with them because of this lien.
[The following rules apply when] a <i>yevamah's</i> [first husband was not obligated to] grant her a <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., he died after he consecrated her, but before he consummated the marriage - in which instance, the woman is obligated to undergo either <i>yibbum</i> or <i>chalitzah</i>, and yet her deceased husband was not obligated to grant her a <i>ketubah</i>.</i> or [when] she waived her <i>ketubah</i> in his favor. The <i>yavam</i> acquires his brother's estate and may sell [portions of it] or give them away as he desires. When he marries his <i>yevamah</i>, he is obligated to compose a <i>ketubah</i> for 100 [<i>zuz</i>]. All of his property will be considered as being on lien for the <i>ketubah</i>, [i.e., the same laws apply to her] as apply to other women who have a <i>ketubah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since she has no claim to her deceased husband's property, she is not judged by the laws pertaining to a <i>yevamah</i>, but rather by those pertaining to other women.</i>
When a woman sells <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> - whether to her husband or to others - after she marries, her act is of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">If she sells the land to her husband, the sale is rescinded because she can claim that she did not sell it willingly; she did so only to appease her husband (<i>Bava Batra</i> 49b). If she sells the land to others, the sale is rescinded because her husband has a right to benefit from her property, and she cannot take away this right from him without his consent. If, however, the husband agrees to her sale, it is binding, as stated in <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 30:3).<br>Note the ruling of the Ramah (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 90:13), who writes that if the husband dies, the sale made by the woman is effective retroactively. (But see also the gloss of the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 90:46.)</i><br>Similarly, if her husband sells landed property belonging to his wife - whether it be <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> or <i>nichsei m'log</i> - his act is of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to <i>nichsei m'log</i>, it is obvious that the husband's sale is of no consequence, for the woman owns this type of property. With regard to <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>, which are considered to be the husband's property, there are authorities (e.g., the Ra'avad) who differ with the Rambam and maintain that the sale is valid until the time comes when the woman desires to collect the money due her by virtue of her <i>ketubah</i>.<br>The commentaries support the Rambam's opinion, explaining that even though the woman has the potential to expropriate the property afterwards by force of law, the sale should be nullified. For women are not comfortable presenting claims in court. If the sale were allowed to remain binding, the only way the woman could receive her due would be by lodging a legal claim. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 90:13) quotes the Rambam's view.</i>
[Should the husband] sell movable property that is classified as <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">If the woman is divorced, she is entitled to this property. Hence, the husband does not have the prerogative of selling it.</i> - although he is not allowed to make such a sale - the sale is binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The husband is allowed to destroy this property through frequent use. Therefore, the woman does not rely on receiving this property, and thus if he sells it the sale is binding (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, gloss on <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 30:5; <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 90:45).<br>This is the opinion of the Rambam and Rabbenu Tam, and is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 90:14). Rabbenu Asher, the Rashba and others differ and maintain that the sale is of no consequence. Their view is quoted by the Ramah.</i><br>If both [the husband and the wife] sell <i>nichsei m'log</i>, the sale is binding,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Although both the husband and his wife have a share in the property, since the purchaser dealt with both of them, the sale is binding.</i> regardless of whether the purchaser purchased the property from the husband first and then from the wife, or if he first purchased it from the wife and then from the husband.
Similarly, when a woman sells her <i>nichsei m'log</i> to her husband or gives them to him as a present, the sale or the gift is binding. She cannot rationalize her actions by saying, "[This was not my true intent.] I did it [only] to appease my husband."<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since this property itself belongs to her, there is no reason for her husband to become upset if she does not desire to sell it to him.</i> With regard to other property, however, she may offer such a rationalization.
What is implied? When a woman sells her <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> to her husband or gives them to him as a present, her husband does not acquire this property. [This applies to] landed property and movable property [in this category], to a field that was designated for her from which [she could collect the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i>, a field belonging to her that was specifically mentioned in her <i>ketubah</i> or a field that [her husband mentioned in her <i>ketubah</i>] as his present to her [to be included in her dowry].<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 23, Halachah 11; <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 30:3.<br>There is a difference of opinion among the Rabbis if similar laws apply when a woman waives her claim to property mentioned in her <i>ketubah</i> in favor of her husband. The Ra'avad and Rabbenu Asher maintain that her deed is of consequence, while the Rashba and the Ramban state that it is not. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> maintains that the Rambam subscribes to the latter view.</i><br>[In all the above instances,] even though [the husband] formalized the transaction with his wife through an act of contract that she voluntarily agreed to, she has the prerogative of recanting whenever she desires.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> emphasizes that until the woman recants, the transaction is binding. The <i>Beit Meir</i>, however, objects, explaining that the Rambam's wording in <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 30:3 does not indicate such a distinction.</i> [We assume that] she gave the present or made the sale only for the sake of maintaining peace in her home.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Her husband will pressure her by saying, "You are either planning my death or considering a divorce. Otherwise, you would not hesitate to sell this property to me" (<i>Bava Batra</i> 49b, 50a).</i><br>Accordingly, a husband has no way of substantiating his claim to his wife's property<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he has a deed or witnesses that testify to the claim, his wife may also negate his claim based on the above rationale.</i> except with regard to <i>nichsei m'log</i>, as explained [in the previous halachah].<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 90:16) states that if the woman explicitly accepts responsibility for the field if expropriated from the husband, then the transaction is binding.</i>
It appears to me that a woman is not entitled [to nullify her statements, based on the rationale]: "I did it [only] to appease my husband," when her <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> were lost or stolen, and she waived the debt in favor of her husband. [This applies even when the commitment] is formalized in the presence of witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Based on the Rambam's statements in Chapter 17, Halachah 19, it is questionable why witnesses are necessary. See <i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 90:1, <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 90:6.</i><br>To what can this be compared? To a man and a woman who formalized an agreement in which she forgoes the responsibility [he had taken for property that had been classified as <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>] and considers it instead <i>nichsei m'log</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">A husband is not obligated to pay for <i>nichsei m'log</i> that have been destroyed, lost or stolen, while in such situations, he is obligated to pay the original value for <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>. Thus, by changing the status of her property, the woman is in effect waiving a financial obligation due her from her husband.<br>There is reason to say that just as a woman can say that she was forced to give or sell this property to her husband to appease him, she could also say that she was also forced to waive her husband's obligation in the loss or theft of this property. The Rambam, however, does not accept this rationale. Since this obligation is due only after the husband's death or divorce, there is nothing pressuring her husband to pay it. If he demands that his wife waive this obligation, she may refuse, asking him: "Is it because you want to divorce me that you are asking me to waive this obligation?" (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> For the husband is not bringing a proof for the sake of taking possession or maintaining possession of property, merely to free himself of the obligation to pay a claim [his wife will issue].<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling. Nevertheless, it is the Rambam's decision which is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 90:18).</i><br>If, by contrast, she gives him movable property that exists and was considered to be <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>, the husband does not acquire it. For the wife may rationalize her conduct saying: "I did this to appease my husband."
When a husband sells [the right to] the benefits from landed property [that belongs to his wife, to another person, while the legal owner of the property remains his wife, the sale] is of no consequence. [The rationale is that] the reason our Sages granted a man [the right to] the benefit that accrues from his wife's property is [to afford him additional income] so that he will spend more generously on the household expenses.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">If, at the outset, the husband sold the rights to benefit from the property for a lump sum, it is possible that all that money would be spent in a short period of time and that afterwards, there would be nothing left for household expenses (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 85:41).</i><br>[Based on that rationale,] if he sells the benefit to be derived [from the landed property to another person] and takes the money and invests it in a business [which offers profit], he is given that prerogative.
[The following laws apply if] the woman possesses financial resources [that she brings to the household]. If they are <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>, her husband may use them for commercial enterprises.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">For their value is explicitly stated in the woman's <i>ketubah</i> and will be returned to her in the event of divorce or her husband's death.</i><br>If they are <i>nichsei m'log</i> - regardless of whether she brought them to the household at the time of marriage or she inherited them or received them as a present<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For money that a woman acquires while married is automatically considered to be <i>nichsei m'log</i>.</i> [- landed property should be purchased with them, from which her husband is entitled to the benefit that accrues].<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In this way, the woman is assured that the principal will remain hers. If the husband desires to use the money for commercial enterprises, he may afterwards sell the right to benefit from the property, as mentioned in the previous halachah.</i> [Similarly,] if she inherited or was given movable property, it should be sold, and the proceeds of the sale should be used to purchase landed property, from which her husband is entitled to the benefit that accrues.
Similarly, if a woman was injured by others,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., people other than her husband. If her husband himself injures her, he is not entitled to benefit from the proceeds of her property, as mentioned in Halachah 28.</i> all the money that is ordained to be given to her should be used to purchase land, from which her husband is entitled to the benefit that accrues, as stated in <i>Hilchot Chovel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Chovel UMazik</i> 4:15. As stated in that source, the husband also has a right to receive a certain portion of the damages as his own funds to which his wife has no right.</i>
[The following law applies when] a woman inherits servants [while she is married]. Even if they are old, they should not be sold, because they bring honor to her family's household.<br>[The following law applies when] she inherits olive trees or vines, but did not [inherit] the land on which these trees were planted. If they produce enough to pay for their upkeep, they should not be sold, because they bring honor to her family's household. If they do not, they should be sold as firewood, land should be purchased with the proceeds, from which the husband is entitled to the benefit that accrues.
When [a married woman] inherits produce that is still attached to the land [on which it is growing], it becomes her husband's [property].<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Although in most instances, produce that is still attached to land is considered equivalent to landed property, an exception is made in this case, because the husband is entitled to the benefit that accrues from his wife's property.</i> When the produce has been uprooted from the land, it should be sold and used to purchase landed property, from which her husband is entitled to the benefit that accrues.<br>When, however, a husband divorces his wife, and there was produce that was still attached to the ground, it belongs to the woman. If it has already been reaped, it belongs to the man.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">For he is entitled to receive all the benefit from her property throughout the duration of their marriage.</i>
A husband is obligated to provide for the sustenance and all the needs of the servants<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Avadim</i> 9:7, which states that a husband does not have the prerogative of telling a servant: "Work for me, but I will not provide for your sustenance."</i> and livestock that belong to his wife and are classified as <i>nichsei m'log</i>. They must work for him, and he is entitled to the benefit that accrues. Therefore, a baby born to a maid classified as <i>nichsei m'log</i> belongs to the husband. And a calf born to a cow that is classified as <i>nichsei m'log</i> belongs to the husband.<br>If, however, the husband divorces his wife and she desires to pay the worth of a child born from a maidservant who is classified as <i>nichsei m'log</i> and take the child as her property because this brings honor to her family's household, she is given that prerogative.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording implies that the concept of maintaining the honor of one's household applies only with regard to the children of one's servants, and not to the offspring of one's livestock. See the <i>Beit Shmuel</i> 85:38, which quotes a difference of opinion among the Rabbis on this issue.</i>
[The following laws apply when] a woman brings two utensils or two maidservants to the household and has them classified as <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>. They were [originally] evaluated at 1000 <i>zuz</i>; afterwards, their value increased and they were evaluated at 2000 <i>zuz</i>. If the woman's husband divorces her, she is entitled to one [utensil or maidservant] for the 1000 <i>zuz</i> that she is owed. With regard to the other - if she desires to pay its value and take it because of the honor it brings to her father's household, she has that prerogative.
When a man gives a present to his wife - regardless of whether he gave her landed property, or he gave her money and she bought landed property - her husband is not entitled to the benefits that accrue from the present [that was given].<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bava Batra</i> 51b states that a person who gives a present gives with a generous spirit. Therefore, we may assume that the husband gives the gift to his wife without wanting to restrict her in any way.</i><br>Similarly, when a man gives a woman a present on the condition that her husband not be entitled to derive the benefits from it, but rather the benefits that accrue will belong to the wife to be used for whatever she desires,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The specific wording of the provision that the giver must make is discussed in <i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 3:13.</i> [the provision is binding, and] the husband is not entitled to the benefits that accrue from this present.<br>Similarly, if a woman sells the rights to her <i>ketubah</i> [in the event of her divorce or her husband's death],<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The person purchasing the rights to the woman's <i>ketubah</i> is taking a risk, because it is possible that she will die in her husband's lifetime and then he will not receive anything.</i> the money she receives belongs to her, and her husband is not entitled to derive the benefit that accrues from it.
When a calf born from cattle that was classified as <i>nichsei m'log</i> is stolen, and the thief is apprehended and forced to pay twice the amount, the woman receives the extra payment. [The rationale is that] this is not the benefit that our Sages granted [the husband].<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages entitled a husband to derive the benefit that would ordinarily accrue from property belonging to his wife - e.g., produce that grows on a field, rent from a home, labor from a servant. They did not grant him rights to benefits that arise from abnormal circumstances.</i><br>When a man injures his wife, the entire [amount he must pay] - the damages and the restitution for the pain and the embarrassment - belongs to the woman,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam does not mention two other payments that a person who inflicts an injury would ordinarily pay: <i>shevet</i> - reimbursement for the wages that were not earned during the period of convalescence, because the husband is entitled to his wife's wages - and <i>ripui</i> - payment for the medical treatment required, because a husband is always required to pay for his wife's medical care.</i> and the husband is not entitled to the benefits that accrue from [property purchased with this money], as explained in <i>Hilchot Chovel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Chovel UMazik</i> 4:16. This differs from instances in which the injury is inflicted by other parties, in which case the husband also has a right to receive a certain portion of the damages as his own funds, to which his wife has no right.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a husband sells landed property [that he owns] to his wife. If the husband knew about the funds with which she purchased the land previously, the sale is binding,<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The husband cannot claim that the funds belonged to him, but since he could not take them from his wife in any other way, he sold the property to her as a ruse. This applies even if he makes a definite claim (<i>ta'anat bari</i>) that the funds belong to him (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 85:22,24).</i> and the husband is entitled to the benefit that accrues from that land.<br>If, however, [the existence of] these funds was concealed, she does not acquire the land. For the husband may [explain that he did not really intend to complete the sale]. [His intent was] to reveal the existence of funds that his wife had hidden. The funds that were revealed should be used to purchase landed property,<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling depends on the halachah to follow, which states that a woman's claim is accepted with regard to money found in her possession.</i> from which the husband is entitled to the benefits that accrue.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's ruling is cited by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 85:9). The Ramah mentions the opinion of Rabbenu Asher, who states that if the husband makes a definite claim that the hidden funds belong to him, his claim is accepted.</i>
When funds or movable property are discovered in a woman's possession, and she claims that they were given her as a present, while her husband claims that they stem from the fruits of her labor and hence belong to him, it is the woman's claim that is accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Ketubot</i> 6:1) states that a present will be spoken about. Therefore, the woman will be afraid to claim that she was given a present unless the claim was true.</i> [The husband] may, however, have a ban of ostracism [conditionally] issued against anyone who makes false statements.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot, however, require his wife to take an oath unless he lodges a definite claim against her (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> [The funds should be used] to purchase landed property, from which [the husband] is entitled to the benefit that accrues.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, a woman was given responsibility to deal freely with the property belonging to her husband's household, and she claims that funds discovered in her possession belong to her privately, her claim is not accepted (<i>Bava Batra</i> 52b; Ramah, <i>Even HaEzer</i> 85:12).</i><br>If the woman claims that the funds were given to her with the provision that her husband have no control over them, but rather that they be used for whatever purpose she desires, she must bring proof [that such a provision was made].<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Although the giving of a present will become public knowledge, the details of the present might not. Therefore, the woman is required to substantiate her statements. Otherwise, we assume that this present was given without any extraordinary conditions (<i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i>).<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> mentions the opinion of the Rashba, who differs and maintains that the woman's claim is also accepted in this instance as well. This opinion is mentioned by the later authorities.</i> [The rationale is that] the prevailing assumption is that a husband has the right to the benefits from all the funds found in a woman's possession, unless she brings proof otherwise.
If [a wife] tells [her husband]: "You gave me [these funds] as a present," she is required to take a Rabbinic oath that her husband gave her [the funds]. [After she takes that oath,] her husband is not entitled to the benefit [from the property purchased with these funds].<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that a woman will not be brazen enough to make false statements in her husband's presence with regard to a matter that he knows to be true (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i>
One should not accept an article for safekeeping that was given by a wife, a servant or a minor.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">For the likelihood is that the article belongs to the husband, master or father, respectively. It is forbidden to assist a person who takes property that is not his or her own. Moreover, if no one accepts the article for safekeeping, it is likely that it will be returned (<i>Bava Batra</i> 51b).</i> If one transgressed and accepted [an article given by] a woman, one should return it to the woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">For there is no proof that the article was stolen.</i> If she dies, one should return it to her husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">For even if the article is rightfully hers, he inherits her property.</i><br>If one accepted [an article given by] a servant, one should return it to the servant. If he dies, one should return it to his master. If one accepted [an article given by] a minor, one should purchase a Torah scroll with the proceeds or an article that will provide [the minor] with benefit.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The entrusted article should not be given to the minor, for it is possible that he will not care for it properly and it will be destroyed (Rashbam, <i>Bava Batra</i> 52a).</i><br>[The following rules apply] with regard to all [the abovementioned individuals], if at the time of their death, they say: "The article I gave for safekeeping belongs to so and so." If the person caring for the article operates under the presumption that the person who entrusted it to him is true to his word, he should carry out the command he was given. If not, he should give [the article] to the person's heirs.
[The following rules apply when] a woman has financial resources sufficient [to purchase property] from which the husband would derive the benefits [but they disagree with regard to the property fit to purchase]; he suggests that this type of property be purchased, and she desires that another type be purchased. A property should be purchased that brings a large revenue and requires little upkeep,<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">In every society, the nature of the type of article purchased depends on the conditions prevalent at that time (<i>Chelkat Mechokek</i> 85:33).</i> regardless of whether this is the article desired by [the husband] or by [the wife]. We do not purchase any article that does not renew itself,<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The Hebrew literally means "whose bark is renewed" - i.e., reaping the benefits one year will not prevent them from being reaped in the future.</i> lest the entire property be used and the principal lost.
[The following rule applies when] a woman brings to her husband's household a goat [that she is entitled] to milk, a sheep [that she is entitled] to shear or a date palm whose fruit [she is entitled to take], although she is entitled only to these benefits [and not to the principal]. [Her husband] is entitled to [these benefits] although the principal is dwindling.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in this instance, the goat's milk is not sold and the money used to purchase property that produces benefit, but rather the goat's milk is used for the household.</i><br>Similarly, if she brought utensils or articles of clothing to his household that were classified as <i>nichsei m'log</i>, he may use them, wearing them or using them as spreads or as covers until the articles themselves are destroyed. If he divorces [his wife], he is not required to pay for any <i>nichsei m'log</i> that became worn out.
The <i>geonim</i> [issued the following] ruling. A husband takes responsibility for the diminished value of <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>. Nevertheless, if [such property] exists [at the time a woman's <i>ketubah</i> is due for payment,] and still serves its initial purpose, the woman must take it regardless of its condition at that time.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Even if they are worth substantially less than they were originally.</i><br>If they are no longer fit to serve their initial purpose, it is as if they were stolen or lost, and the husband is obligated to pay the value appraised originally at the time of the marriage.<br>This is the common custom. Whenever a man marries, he accepts responsibility for [the woman's] dowry as contingent on this custom.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">As the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> emphasizes, at different times and in different countries, other customs have prevailed.
